- "She passed the exam" → Subject: she; Verb: passed; Complete thought? Yes → IC ✓
- Both are ICs → Semicolon is correct ✓

**Common Error — Semicolon with a Dependent Clause:**
- "Because she studied hard; she passed the exam." → WRONG. "Because she studied hard" is a dependent clause (starts with "because"). A semicolon cannot join a dependent clause to an independent clause.

**Common Error — Semicolon with a Phrase:**
- "Studying hard for the exam; she passed." → WRONG. "Studying hard for the exam" is a participial phrase, not an independent clause.

**The Semicolon as a "Soft Period":**

Think of the semicolon as a period with a softer pause. It connects two independent clauses that are closely related in meaning. If you could put a period between them and both would be valid sentences, a semicolon is also acceptable.

**Period version:** "She studied hard. She passed the exam." → Both are valid sentences.
**Semicolon version:** "She studied hard; she passed the exam." → Also correct, and it shows the closer relationship between the ideas.

**When to use a semicolon vs. a period:** Both are grammatically correct. The semicolon signals a closer relationship between the two ideas. On the ACT, if both a period and a semicolon are options, the semicolon is usually preferred when the ideas are closely related, but the ACT will not make you choose between them — it will test whether you know that a semicolon requires two ICs.

### Rule 2: Semicolons Separate Items in a Complex List

**Rule:** When items in a list already contain commas, use semicolons to separate the list items for clarity.

**Structure:** Item with internal comma; item with internal comma; and item with internal comma.

**Examples:**
- "The conference attendees came from Paris, France; London, England; and Tokyo, Japan." (Each list item contains a city and country separated by a comma; semicolons separate the items)
- "The committee included Dr. Smith, the chairperson; Ms. Jones, the treasurer; and Mr. Lee, the secretary." (Each item has a name and title separated by a comma; semicolons separate the items)

**Why this is necessary:** Without semicolons, the list becomes confusing:
- "The conference attendees came from Paris, France, London, England, and Tokyo, Japan." → How many items? Six? Three? It's unclear.

**Note:** This rule is tested less frequently than Rule 1 but does appear on the ACT. The key signal is a list where items already contain commas.

### Rule 3: Semicolons with Transition Words (Conjunctive Adverbs)

**Rule:** When a transition word (conjunctive adverb) connects two independent clauses, use a semicolon before the transition word and a comma after it.

**Structure:** Independent Clause; transition, Independent Clause.

**Examples:**
- "She studied hard; therefore, she passed the exam."
- "The experiment was flawed; however, the results were still published."
- "The data was incomplete; consequently, the study was inconclusive."
- "He was tired; nevertheless, he continued working."

**The Pattern:** IC; transition, IC

**Why the semicolon?** The transition word (however, therefore, moreover, consequently, etc.) is NOT a FANBOYS conjunction. It cannot join two ICs with just a comma. The semicolon provides the necessary "strong" punctuation to join the two ICs, and the comma after the transition word sets it off as an interrupter.

**Common transition words that follow this pattern:**

| Transition | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| However | Contrast | "It rained; however, we went hiking." |
| Therefore | Result | "She studied; therefore, she passed." |
| Moreover | Addition | "He is smart; moreover, he is kind." |
| Furthermore | Addition | "The plan is risky; furthermore, it is expensive." |
| Consequently | Result | "He was late; consequently, he missed the train." |
| Nevertheless | Contrast | "It was difficult; nevertheless, she succeeded." |
| Nonetheless | Contrast | "The evidence was weak; nonetheless, he was convicted." |
| Meanwhile | Simultaneous time | "She cooked; meanwhile, he set the table." |
| Otherwise | Alternative | "We must hurry; otherwise, we'll be late." |
| Instead | Alternative | "He didn't rest; instead, he worked harder." |
| Indeed | Emphasis | "The task was hard; indeed, it was nearly impossible." |
| Thus | Result | "The road was icy; thus, driving was dangerous." |
| Hence | Result | "She was absent; hence, she missed the announcement." |
| Accordingly | Result | "The weather was bad; accordingly, the event was canceled." |
| Similarly | Comparison | "She excels in math; similarly, he is gifted in science." |
| Likewise | Comparison | "He was tired; likewise, she needed rest." |
| Also | Addition | "She sings; also, she plays piano." |
| Still | Contrast | "It was risky; still, they proceeded." |

**CRITICAL DISTINCTION:** The semicolon + transition + comma pattern is ONLY used when the transition word connects TWO INDEPENDENT CLAUSES. If the transition word is used within a single clause or at the beginning of a sentence, the punctuation changes:

- "However, she passed the exam." (Transition at the start of a sentence → comma after it, no semicolon)
- "She passed the exam, however." (Transition at the end of a sentence → comma before it, no semicolon)
- "She, however, passed the exam." (Transition in the middle of a sentence → pair of commas around it, no semicolon)

### What a Semicolon Can NEVER Do

1. **A semicolon can NEVER be used with "and" to join two ICs.**
   - WRONG: "She studied hard; and she passed the exam." → WRONG. The semicolon and "and" together are redundant. Use one or the other: "She studied hard; she passed the exam" OR "She studied hard, and she passed the exam."

2. **A semicolon can NEVER join an independent clause to a dependent clause.**
   - WRONG: "Because she studied hard; she passed the exam." → WRONG. "Because she studied hard" is a dependent clause.

3. **A semicolon can NEVER join an independent clause to a phrase.**
   - WRONG: "She studied hard; passing the exam." → WRONG. "Passing the exam" is a phrase, not a clause.

4. **A semicolon can NEVER be used after a verb or preposition to introduce a list.**
   - WRONG: "She bought; apples, oranges, and bananas." → WRONG. Use a colon or no punctuation.

### Summary of Semicolon Rules — Quick Reference

| Rule | Structure | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Join two ICs (no FANBOYS) | IC; IC | "She ran; he walked" |
| Complex list separation | Item, detail; item, detail; and item, detail | "Paris, France; London, England; and Tokyo, Japan" |
| With transition words | IC; transition, IC | "She ran; however, he walked" |
| NEVER with "and" | IC; and IC → WRONG | "She ran; and he walked" → WRONG |
| NEVER with DC | DC; IC → WRONG | "Because she ran; he walked" → WRONG |

---

## Section 4: The Colon — The Signpost

The colon is a powerful but narrowly defined punctuation mark. On the ACT, it has one primary rule and one secondary use, and the test focuses heavily on the primary rule.

### Rule 1: A Colon Must Be Preceded by a Complete Sentence (Independent Clause)

**Rule:** The text before a colon MUST be a complete, independent clause. The colon then directs attention to what follows — a list, an explanation, an elaboration, or a quotation.

**Structure:** Complete Sentence**Examples:**
- "She bought three things: apples, bananas." (Complete sentence before colon → correct)
- goal: to win the championship." (Complete sentence before colon → correct)
- "The reason is simple: she worked harder than anyone else." (Complete sentence before colon → correct)
- "He scored a 36: a perfect score." (Complete sentence before colon → correct)

**The Critical Test:** Read the text before the colon. Does it express a complete thought? Can it stand alone as a sentence? If yes, the colon is potentially correct. If no, the colon is wrong.

**Common Error — Colon After a Verb:**
- "She bought: apples, oranges, and bananas." → WRONG. "She bought" is not a complete thought — bought what? The verb "bought" is transitive and needs an object. The colon incorrectly separates the verb from its object.

**Common Error — Colon After a Preposition:**
- "She went to: the store, the bank, and the post office." → WRONG. "She went to" is not a complete thought — to what? The preposition "to" needs an object.

**Common Error — Colon After "Including" or "Such As":**
- "She enjoys many activities, including: hiking, swimming, and biking." → WRONG. "Including" is a preposition; the text before the colon ("She enjoys many activities, including") is not a complete sentence.

**The ACT's Favorite Colon Trap:** The ACT frequently places a colon after a verb or preposition that introduces a list. This is ALWAYS wrong. The colon must follow a complete sentence.

**How to fix it:**
- WRONG: "She bought: apples, oranges, and bananas."
- CORRECT: "She bought three things: apples, oranges, and bananas." (Now "She bought three things" is a complete sentence)
- CORRECT: "She bought apples, oranges, and bananas." (No colon needed at all — just a direct object)

### Rule 2: What Follows a Colon

**Rule:** What follows a colon can be almost anything — a list, a single word, a phrase, or even a complete sentence. The colon's job is to say "here is what I mean" or "here is the list/explanation."

**Examples:**
- **List:** "She packed the essentials: a tent, a sleeping bag, and a flashlight."
- **Single word:** "There was only one explanation: sabotage."
- **Phrase:** "His advice was straightforward: work hard and stay humble."
- **Complete sentence:** "Remember this: no one achieves greatness without sacrifice." (The second sentence explains or emphasizes the first)

**Note:** When a complete sentence follows a colon, it may or may not be capitalized. Both are acceptable on the ACT. The ACT will not test capitalization after a colon.

### Rule 3: Colons vs. Semicolons — The Critical Distinction

This is one of the most frequently tested punctuation distinctions on the ACT.

**Colon:** The second part EXPLAINS, illustrates, or lists what the first part introduces. The first part must be a complete sentence.
- "She had one goal: to win." (The second part explains the goal)

**Semicolon:** The second part is a SEPARATE but RELATED idea. Both parts must be complete sentences.
- "She had one goal; she was determined to win." (Two separate but related ideas)

**The key question:** Does the second part explain or list what the first part introduces? If yes, use a colon. If the second part is just a related independent clause, use a semicolon.

**Examples for comparison:**
- "He had a plan: to infiltrate the building and disable the alarm." (Colon — the second part explains the plan)
- "He had a plan; he would infiltrate the building and disable the alarm." (Semicolon — two related ICs)
- "He had a plan; specifically, to infiltrate the building and disable the alarm." (Semicolon + transition — "specifically" is a transition word, and the second part is not a complete IC, so a colon would also work here, but the semicolon + transition pattern is the tested structure)

### Rule 4: Colons with Quotations

**Rule:** A colon can introduce a formal or block quotation, especially when the introduction is a complete sentence.

**Examples:**
- "The author makes a bold claim: 'All progress depends on the unreasonable man.'"
- "She summed up her philosophy: 'Never give up.'"

**Note:** A comma can also introduce a quotation (and is more common for short, informal quotations). The ACT does not typically test the distinction between colon and comma for quotations.

### Summary of Colon Rules — Quick Reference

| Rule | Structure | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Preceded by complete sentence | IC: list/explanation | "She bought: apples" → WRONG; "She bought fruit: apples" → CORRECT |
| Never after verb or preposition | She bought: → WRONG | "She bought apples" or "She bought fruit: apples" |
| Introduces list, explanation, or quotation | IC: what follows | "One thing: honesty" |
| vs. Semicolon | Colon = explanation; Semicolon = related IC | "She had a plan: to win" vs. "She had a plan; she would win" |

---

## Section 5: The Dash — Emphasis, Interruption, and Informality

The dash (specifically the **em dash**, which is the long dash used in English punctuation) is a versatile but specific punctuation mark on the ACT. It has three primary uses, all of which are tested.

### Understanding the Dash Family

Before proceeding, it is essential to distinguish between three similar marks:

1. **Hyphen (-):** The shortest mark. Used to join compound words (e.g., "well-known," "twenty-one"). NOT tested as punctuation on the ACT in the same way as the other marks.

2. **En dash (–):** Slightly longer than a hyphen. Used for ranges (e.g., "pages 10–25," "2020–2025"). Rarely tested on the ACT.

3. **Em dash (—):** The longest mark. Used for emphasis, interruption, and setting off nonessential elements. THIS is the dash tested on the ACT.

**Note:** On the ACT, when you see a dash in answer choices, it is always the em dash. The test does not distinguish between en dashes and em dashes in its answer choices.

### Rule 1: Dashes Set Off Nonessential (Nonrestrictive) Elements — Interchangeable with Commas and Parentheses

**Rule:** A pair of dashes can set off a nonessential element in the middle, beginning, or end of a sentence, just like a pair of commas or parentheses.

**Structure:** Sentence — nonessential element — continuation.

**Examples:**
- "The dog — which was old and gray — barked loudly." (Equivalent to: "The dog, which was old and gray, barked loudly.")
- "She finally answered — after a long pause  not be attending." (Equivalent to: "She finally answered, after a long pause, that she would not be attending.")
- "The results — surprising and unexpected — changed everything." (Equivalent to: "The results, surprising and unexpected, changed everything.")

**The Three-Way Interchangeability:**

When setting off nonessential elements, dashes, commas, and parentheses are all grammatically correct. The difference is in **emphasis:**

| Punctuation | Emphasis Level | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| **Dashes** | Most emphatic | Draws attention to the enclosed material; creates a dramatic pause |
| **Commas** | Standard | Smoothly integrates the material; neutral emphasis |
| **Parentheses** | Least emphatic | De-emphasizes the material; treats it as an aside or afterthought |

**ACT Implications:** The ACT will NOT ask you to choose between dashes, commas, and parentheses based on emphasis alone. All three are grammatically correct for nonessential elements. However, the ACT WILL test whether the punctuation is used in PAIRS (for middle elements) or SINGLY (for end elements).

**Middle element (needs a pair):**
- "The dog — which was old — barked." (Pair of dashes ✓)
- "The dog — which was old barked." (Single dash  — WRONG)

**End element (single dash is correct):**
- "The dog had one quality — loyalty." (Single dash at the end ✓)

### Rule 2: A Single Dash Adds Information at the End of a Sentence

**Rule:** A single dash can be used at the end of a sentence to add emphasis, a summary, or a dramatic afterthought. This is similar to a colon but with a more informal or emphatic tone.

**Structure:** Sentence — additional information.

**Examples:**
- "He had only one thing on his mind — revenge." (Similar to a colon: "He had only one thing on his mind: revenge.")
- "The answer was obvious — she had been lying all along." (Adds a dramatic explanation)
- "They found what they were looking for — nothing." (Emphatic contrast)

**Dash vs. Colon at the End of a Sentence:**

Both can be used to add information at the end of a sentence, but they differ in tone:

| Feature | Colon | Dash |
|---|---|---|
| **Tone** | Formal, direct | Informal, emphatic, dramatic |
| **Function** | Introduces a list, explanation, or quotation | Adds emphasis, surprise, or an afterthought |
| **Requirement** | Must be preceded by a complete sentence | Must be preceded by a complete sentence |

**On the ACT:** Both are grammatically correct when preceded by a complete sentence. The ACT will not make you choose between them based on tone. However, if the text before the dash/colon is NOT a complete sentence, both are wrong.

**Examples:**
- CORRECT: "She had one goal: to win." (Colon — complete sentence before it)
- CORRECT: "She had one goal — to win." (Dash — complete sentence before it)
- WRONG: "She had: to win." (Colon after incomplete sentence — WRONG)
- WRONG: "She had — to win." (Dash after incomplete sentence — WRONG)

### Rule 3: Dashes Indicate Interruption or Broken Thought

**Rule:** Dashes can indicate an interruption in dialogue or a broken thought.

**Examples:**
- "I was just trying to — well, it doesn't matter now." (Interrupted thought)
- "But I thought you said —" "I know what I said." (Interrupted dialogue)

**Note:** This use is more common in narrative passages and is less frequently tested than Rules 1 and 24: Dashes with Appositives Containing Commas

**Rule:** When an appositive itself contains commas, use dashes to set off the appositive for clarity.

**Example:**
- "Three of the cities — Paris, France; London, England; and Rome, Italy — were included in the study." (The appositive contains internal commas and semicolons; dashes make the boundaries clear)

**Without dashes:** "Three of the cities, Paris, France; London, England; and Rome, Italy, were included in the study." → This is confusing because the commas blend together.

### Summary of Dash Rules — Quick Reference

| Rule | Structure | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Set off nonessential element (pair) | Sentence — nonessential — continuation | "The dog — old and gray — barked" |
| Add information at end (single) | Sentence — addition | "She wanted one thing — victory" |
| Indicate interruption | Sentence — [broken off] | "I was just — never mind" |
| Clarify appositives with internal commas | Sentence — appositive, with commas, inside — continuation | "Three cities — Paris, London, and Rome — were studied" |
| Interchangeable with commas/parentheses | All three work for nonessential elements | Dashes = emphatic; Commas = standard; Parentheses = de-emphasized |

---

## Section 6: The Hyphen — Compound Words and Clarity

While less frequently tested than the other punctuation marks, the hyphen does appear on the ACT, primarily in questions about compound adjectives and number expressions.

### Rule 1: Hyphenate Compound Adjectives Before a Noun

**Rule:** When two or more words work together as a single adjective BEFORE a noun, they are usually hyphenated.

**Examples:**
- "a well-known author" (Compound adjective before noun → hyphenated)
- "a full-time job" (Compound adjective before noun → hyphenated)
- "a two-year-old child" (Compound adjective before noun → hyphenated)
- "a high-speed chase" (Compound adjective before noun → hyphenated)

**When the compound adjective comes AFTER the noun, the hyphen is often dropped:**
- "The author is well known." (After noun → no hyphen)
- "The job is full time." (After noun → no hyphen)
- "The child is two years old." (After noun → no hyphen)

**Exceptions:** Some compound adjectives are always hyphenated, regardless of position:
- "a state-of-the-art facility" / "The facility is state-of-the-art."
- "a well-rounded student" / "The student is well-rounded."

### Rule 2: Hyphenate Compound Numbers and Fractions

**Numbers twenty-one through ninety-nine:**
- "twenty-one," "thirty-five," "sixty-seven," "ninety-nine"

**Fractions used as adjectives:**
- "a two-thirds majority" (Hyphenated when used as adjective)
- "two thirds of the population" (Not hyphenated when used as noun)

### Rule 3: Hyphenate with Certain Prefixes

**Prefixes that typically require a hyphen:**
- **Ex-** (meaning former): "ex-president," "ex-wife"
- **Self-**: "self-esteem," "self-confidence," "self-aware"
- **All-**: "all-inclusive," "all-knowing"

**Prefixes that do NOT require a hyphen (most common):**
- **Re-**, **un-**, **pre-**, **post-**, **anti-**, **co-**, **mid-**, **non-**, **over-**, **under-**, **sub-**, **super-**, **inter-**, **semi-**, **multi-**
  - "rewrite," "unhappy," "preexisting," "postwar," "antisocial," "cooperate," "midterm," "nonessential," "overestimate," "underestimate," "subcategory," "supernatural," "international," "semicircle," "multimedia"

**Exception — Avoiding ambiguity:**
- "re-cover" (to cover again) vs. "recover" (to get better)
- "re-create" (to create again) vs. "recreate" (to relax/amusement — though this is archaic)

**Note:** The ACT rarely tests hyphenation with prefixes, but it may appear in questions about compound adjectives.

---

## Section 7: The Apostrophe — Contraction and Possession

The apostrophe is tested on the ACT in two primary contexts: contractions and possession. Both are high-frequency test topics, and the ACT has specific traps for each.

### Rule 1: Apostrophes in Contractions

**Rule:** A contraction merges two words into one, with the apostrophe replacing the missing letter(s).

**Common contractions:**

| Contraction | Full Form | Missing Letter(s) |
|---|---|---|
| it's | it is / it has | i (from "is") or ha (from "has") |
| they're | they are | a |
| we're | we are | a |
| you're | you are | a |
| I'm | I am | a |
| he's | he is / he has | i or ha |
| she's | she is / she has | i or ha |
| that's | that is / that has | i or ha |
| there's | there is / there has | i or ha |
| here's | here is | i |
| who's | who is / who has | i or ha |
| what's | what is / what has | i or ha |
| where's | where is / where has | i or ha |
| when's | when is / when has | i or ha |
| why's | why is / why has | i or ha |
| how's | how is / how has | i or ha |
| isn't | is not | o |
| aren't | are not | o |
| wasn't | was not | o |
| weren't | were not | o |
| don't | do not | o |
| doesn't | does not | o |
| didn't | did not | o |
| haven't | have not | o |
| hasn't | has not | o |
| hadn't | had not | o |
| won't | will not | wi (irregular) |
| wouldn't | would not | o |
| can't | cannot | no (irregular) |
| couldn't | could not | o |
| shouldn't | should not | o |
| mustn't | must not | o |
| I'll | I will | wi |
| you'll | you will | wi |
| he'll | he will | wi |
| she'll | she will | wi |
| we'll | we will | wi |
| they'll | they will | wi |
| I've | I have | ha |
| you've | you have | ha |
| we've | we have | ha |
| they've | they have | ha |
| I'd | I would / I had | woul or ha |
| you'd | you would / you had | woul or ha |
| he'd | he would / he had | woul or ha |
| she'd | she would / she had | w| we'd | we would / we had | woul they'd | they would / they had | woul or ha |
| let's | let us | u |

### Rule 2: The "Its" vs. "It's" Distinction — The #1 Apostrophe Trap

This is the single most tested apostrophe concept on the ACT. You must know this distinction cold.

**It's (with apostrophe) = Contraction of "it is" or "it has"**
- "It's raining outside." = "It is raining outside."
- "It's been a long day." = "It has been a long day."

**Its (without apostrophe) = Possessive pronoun (belonging to it)**
- "The dog wagged its tail." (The tail belongs to the dog → possessive → no apostrophe)
- "The company changed its policy." (The policy belongs to the company → possessive → no apostrophe)

**The Test:** Whenever you see "it's" or "its" in an ACT question, try replacing it with "it is" or "it has." If the sentence still makes sense, use "it's." If not, use "its."

**Examples:**
- "___ going to be a long winter." → "It is going to be a long winter." → Makes sense → "It's"
- "The cat licked ___ paw." → "The cat licked it is paw." → Nonsense → "Its"

**Why this matters:** The ACT deliberately places "it's" and "its" in answer choices to test this distinction. It is one of the most reliable question types on the entire test.

### Rule 3: Possessive Apostrophes for Nouns

**Singular nouns (not ending in s): Add 's**
- "The boy's hat" (The hat belongs to the boy)
- "James's book" (The book belongs to James — yes, even though James ends in s, the ACT uses 's for singular proper nouns)
- "The child's toy" (The toy belongs to the child)
- "The woman's car" (The car belongs to the woman)
- "The dog's bone" (The bone belongs to the dog)

**Singular nouns ending in s: Add 's (ACT preference)**
- "James's book" (Not "James' book" — the ACT prefers 's for singular nouns, even those ending in s)
- "The boss's office" (Not "the boss' office")
- "Dickens's novels" (Not "Dickens' novels")

**Note:** Some style guides allow just an apostrophe for singular nouns ending in s (e.g., "James' book"), but the ACT consistently uses 's for singular nouns. When in doubt on the ACT, use 's for singular nouns.

**Plural nouns ending in s: Add just an apostrophe (')**
- "The boys' hats" (The hats belong to multiple boys)
- "The teachers' lounge" (The lounge belongs to multiple teachers)
- "The dogs' bones" (The bones belong to multiple dogs)
- "The students' grades" (The grades belong to multiple students)

**Plural nouns NOT ending in s: Add 's**
- "The children's toys" (The toys belong to multiple children — "children" is plural but doesn't end in s)
- "The women's room" (The room belongs to women — "women" is plural but doesn't end in s)
- "The people's choice" (The choice belongs to the people)
- "The mice's nest" (The nest belongs to the mice)

**The Decision Tree for Possessive Apostrophes:**

1. Is the noun singular or plural?
2. If **singular**: Add **'s** (always, even if the noun ends in s)
3. ends in s**: Add just **'** (apostrophe only)
4. If **plural and does NOT end in s**: Add **'s**

### Rule 4: Possessive Pronouns — NO Apostrophe

**Rule:** Possessive pronouns NEVER take apostrophes. This is a fundamental rule that the ACT tests frequently.

**Possessive pronouns (NO apostrophe):**

| Pronoun | Possessive Form | Example |
|---|---|---|
| I | my / mine | "That is my book." / "The book is mine." |
| you | your / yours | "That is your book." / "The book is yours." |
| he | his | "That is his book." |
| she | her / hers | "That is her book." / "The book is hers." |
| it | its | "The dog wagged its tail." |
| we | our / ours | "That is our house." / "The house is ours." |
| they | their / theirs | "That is their car." / "The car is theirs." |
| who | whose | "Whose book is this?" |

**The Critical Traps:**

- **Its vs. It's** (covered above) — "its" is possessive (no apostrophe); "it's" is a contraction (apostrophe)
- **Whose vs. Who's** — "whose" is possessive (no apostrophe); "who's" is a contraction of "who is" or "who has" (apostrophe)
  - "Whose coat is this?" = "The coat belongs to whom?" → possessive → "whose"
  - "Who's coming to the party?" = "Who is coming to the party?" → contraction → "who's"
- **Their vs. They're** — "their" is possessive (no apostrophe); "they're" is a contraction of "they are" (apostrophe)
  - "Their house is beautiful." = "The house belongs to them." → possessive → "their"
  - "They're going to the store." = "They are going to the store." → contraction → "they're"
- **Your vs. You're** — "your" is possessive (no apostrophe); "you're" is a contraction of "you are" (apostrophe)
  - "Your book is on the table." = "The book belongs to you." → possessive → "your"
  - "You're going to love this." = "You are going to love this." → contraction → "you're"

**The Replacement Test for All Contractions:**

Whenever you see a word with an apostrophe that could be either a contraction or a possessive, try replacing it with the full form:
- If "it is" / "it has" works → "it's" (contraction)
- If not → "its" (possessive)
- If "who is" / "who has" works → "who's" (contraction)
- If not → "whose" (possessive)
- If "they are" works → "they're" (contraction)
- If not → "their" (possessive)
- If "you are" works → "you're" (contraction)
- If not → "your" (possessive)

### Rule 5: Apostrophes in Joint and Individual Possession

**Joint possession (two people own the same thing together): Add 's to the last noun only.**
- "Jack and Jill's pail" (The pail belongs to both Jack and Jill together)
-esson's revolver" (The revolver belongs to the company as a unit)

**Individual possession (two people own separate things): Add 's- "Jack's and Jill's pails" (Jack has his own pail; Jill has her own pail)
- "Smith's and Wesson's contributions" (Each made separate contributions)

### Rule 6: Apostrophes with Time and Money

**Rule:** Use 's to indicate possession with expressions of time and money.
- "A day's work" (The work of a day)
- "A week's vacation" (The vacation of a week)
- "A dollar's worth" (The worth of a dollar)
- "Two weeks' notice" (The notice of two weeks — plural, so just apostrophe)
- "Five dollars' worth" (The worth of five dollars — plural, so just apostrophe)

### Rule 7: Apostrophes Are NOT Used for Plural Nouns

**Rule:** Do NOT use an apostrophe to make a regular noun plural. This is one of the most common errors in English writing, and the ACT tests it.

**WRONG:**
- "The dog's are in the yard." (Should be "The dogs are in the yard.")
- "I bought three apple's." (Should be "I bought three apples.")
- "The 1990's were a great decade." (Should be "The 1990s were a great decade." — though some style guides allow the apostrophe for decades, the ACT does not)

**CORRECT:**
- "The dogs are in the yard."

# Chapter 4: Deep-Dive Grammar Mastery — Sentence Structure and Modifier Logic

---

## 4.0 — Introduction: Why This Chapter Exists

On the ACT English section, no cluster of skills generates more wrong answers—and more wasted time—than sentence structure and modifier logic. These two domains alone account for the single largest category of grammar questions on the test (Sentence Structure is ranked #1 in frequency by virtually every test-prep analysis). They are also the skills most commonly misunderstood by students who "go by ear," because spoken English tolerates structural ambiguities that formal written English does not.

This chapter will give you a complete, surgical understanding of:

1. **The anatomy of clauses** — so you can identify what is a sentence and what is not.
2. **Every way two clauses can be (and cannot be) joined** — so run-ons and comma splices become impossible for you to miss.
3. **The complete modifier system** — introductory, middle, and ending comma clauses, plus the logical rules governing what each one must describe.
4. **Parallelism** — the rule that governs lists, comparisons, and correlative constructions.
5. **Fragment diagnosis** — how to recognize and fix incomplete sentences.

Each subsection includes definitions, rules, exceptions, ACT-specific patterns, and step-by-step processes you can execute on test day.

---

## 4.1 — Clause Anatomy: The Foundation of Everything

### 4.1.1 — What Is a Clause?

A **clause** is a group of words that contains both a **subject** and a **verb** (predicate). This is the single most important structural definition on the entire ACT English section. Every decision you make about punctuation, sentence boundaries, and modifier placement depends on your ability to identify clauses.

**Independent Clause (IC):**
An independent clause expresses a complete thought and can stand alone as a sentence.

> *The researcher analyzed the data.*  
> Subject = researcher | Verb = analyzed | Complete thought = Yes

**Dependent Clause (DC):**
A dependent clause contains a subject and a verb but begins with a **subordinating conjunction** or a **relative pronoun**, which prevents it from standing alone.

> *because the researcher analyzed the data*  
> Subject = researcher | Verb = analyzed | Complete thought = No (it leaves us hanging)

### 4.1.2 — The Subordinating Conjunctions (Triggers for Dependent Clauses)

Memorize these words. The moment you see one, you know the clause it introduces is dependent and cannot stand alone.

| Category | Subordinating Conjunctions |
|---|---|
| **Time** | after, before, when, whenever, while, until, as soon as, since (temporal), once |
| **Cause/Effect** | because, since (causal), so that, in order that, now that, as (causal) |
| **Contrast/Concession** | although, even though, though, whereas, while (contrastive), despite the fact that |
| **Condition** | if, unless, provided that, assuming that, even if, whether |
| **Manner/Comparison** | as, as if, as though, like (in formal writing, "like" should not introduce a clause—though the ACT rarely tests this) |
| **Place** | where, wherever |
| **Relative Pronouns** | who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when (relative) |

**Critical ACT Insight:** The word "since" can indicate time OR causation. You must read context to determine which meaning is intended, because the grammatical treatment is the same (it creates a dependent clause), but the logic of the passage may depend on it.

Similarly, "while" can indicate simultaneous time OR contrast. Again, context determines meaning, but grammatically it still creates a dependent clause.

### 4.1.3 — Relative Clauses

A **relative clause** is a dependent clause that begins with a relative pronoun (who, whom, whose, which, that) and functions as an adjective, modifying a noun.

> *The book **that she recommended** was out of print.*  
> Relative clause = "that she recommended" | Modifies = "book"

**Restrictive vs. Nonrestrictive Relative Clauses:**

- **Restrictive (Essential):** The clause is necessary to identify which noun you mean. NO commas.
  > *The students **who studied hard** scored well.* (Only the ones who studied—not all students.)

- **Nonrestrictive (Nonessential):** The clause adds extra information about an already-identified noun. MUST be set off with commas.
  > *My professor, **who studied at Harvard**, explained the theory.* (We already know which professor; the clause just adds bonus information.)

**ACT Rule:** If you can remove the clause without changing the fundamental identity of the noun, it is nonessential and needs commas. If removing it makes the sentence ambiguous about which person/thing you mean, it is essential and takes no commas.

### 4.1.4 — Noun Clauses

A **noun clause** is a dependent clause that functions as a noun (subject, object, or complement).

> ***What she discovered*** *changed the field.* (Noun clause as subject)  
> *I know ***that the meeting is at noon.*** (Noun clause as direct object)

Noun clauses are rarely tested for their own structure on the ACT, but recognizing them prevents you from misidentifying the subject of a sentence. In "What she discovered changed the field," the subject is the entire noun clause, not "she." This matters for subject-verb agreement.

### 4.1.5 — Quick Identification Process

When you encounter any group of words on the ACT, run this diagnostic:

1. **Find the verb(s).** How many conjugated (tensed) verbs are there?
2. **Find the subject(s).** Who or what is performing each verb?
3. **Check for subordinating conjunctions or relative pronouns.** If a subject-verb pair is introduced by one, it is a dependent clause.
4. **Count the independent clauses.** If you have two or more ICs, you have a compound structure that requires proper joining (covered in §4.2).

---

## 4.2 — Joining Independent Clauses: The Five Legitimate Methods

This is the heart of sentence structure on the ACT. The test is obsessed with whether you can correctly join two independent clauses and whether you can recognize when they have been joined incorrectly.

### 4.2.1 — The Five Methods (Memorize These)

| Method | Structure | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **1. Semicolon alone** | IC ; IC | *The data was conclusive; the hypothesis was confirmed.* |
| **2. Comma + Coordinating Conjunction (FANBOYS)** | IC, FANBOYS IC | *The data was conclusive, and the hypothesis was confirmed.* |
| **3. Semicolon + Transition Word + Comma** | IC; transition, IC | *The data was conclusive; therefore, the hypothesis was confirmed.* |
| **4. Period (Two Separate Sentences)** | IC. IC. | *The data was conclusive. The hypothesis was confirmed.* |
| **5. Colon** | IC: IC | *The data was conclusive: the hypothesis was confirmed.* (2nd explains or elaborates on 1st) |

**FANBOYS = For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So**

These are the ONLY seven coordinating conjunctions in English. If you see a comma followed by a word that is not one of these seven, it is almost certainly a comma splice (an error).

### 4.2.2 — The Three Illegal Joins (Run-On Sentences)

| Error | Structure | Why It's Wrong |
|---|---|---|
| **No punctuation (Fused Sentence)** | IC IC | Two ICs smashed together with no separation |
| **Comma Splice** | IC, IC | A comma alone is not strong enough to join two ICs |
| **"However" Comma Splice** | IC, however, IC | "However" is a transition word, not a coordinating conjunction |

**The "However" Trap:** This is one of the most common errors on the ACT. The word "however" looks like it should work with a comma, but it is a **conjunctive adverb** (transition word), not a coordinating conjunction. It cannot join two clauses by itself.

> ✗ *The data was conclusive, however, the hypothesis was confirmed.* (WRONG — comma splice)  
> ✓ *The data was conclusive; however, the hypothesis was confirmed.* (CORRECT — semicolon + transition + comma)  
> ✓ *The data was conclusive. However, the hypothesis was confirmed.* (CORRECT — period + transition + comma)  
> ✓ *The data was conclusive, and however, the hypothesis was confirmed.* (Technically correct but wordy — the ACT would prefer a simpler option)

**The Full Pattern for Transition Words:**
When using a transition word (however, therefore, moreover, furthermore, consequently, nevertheless, nonetheless, thus, hence, accordingly, similarly, likewise, instead, otherwise, etc.) to join two independent clauses:

> **IC ; transition, IC**

The semicolon goes BEFORE the transition. The comma goes AFTER the transition. This pattern is inviolable on the ACT.

### 4.2.3 — The Colon Join: Special Rules

The colon is unique among the five methods because it carries a **meaning requirement**: the second clause must explain, illustrate, elaborate, or summarize the first clause.

**Rule 1: The clause before the colon MUST be a complete sentence.**

> ✗ *The results were: surprising to everyone.* (WRONG — "The results were" is not a complete thought)  
> ✓ *The results were clear: the hypothesis was confirmed.* (CORRECT — "The results were clear" is a complete sentence)

**Rule 2: Never use a colon immediately after a verb or preposition that introduces the list.**

> ✗ *The ingredients include: flour, sugar, and eggs.* (WRONG — "include" is a verb)  
> ✓ *The ingredients include flour, sugar, and eggs.* (CORRECT — no colon needed)  
> ✓ *The list of ingredients is as follows: flour, sugar, and eggs.* (CORRECT — "The list of ingredients is as follows" is a complete sentence)

**Rule 3: The colon can introduce a single word for dramatic emphasis.**

> *She had one goal: victory.*

### 4.2.4 — Dependent Clauses as a Fix for Run-Ons

One of the most common ways the ACT tests run-on correction is by offering a dependent clause as the fix. Adding a subordinating conjunction to one of the two ICs converts it into a DC, which can then be attached to the remaining IC.

> ✗ *The experiment failed, the equipment was faulty.* (Comma splice)  
> ✓ *The experiment failed **because** the equipment was faulty.* (DC + IC = correct)  
> ✓ ***Because** the equipment was faulty, the experiment failed.* (DC, IC = correct — note the comma after the introductory dependent clause)

**Key Punctuation Rule for Dependent Clauses:**
- **DC before IC → COMMA:** *Because it was raining, we stayed inside.*
- **IC before DC → NO COMMA:** *We stayed inside because it was raining.*

This rule is tested repeatedly. The ACT loves to offer both versions as answer choices, and students who don't know the rule will guess.

### 4.2.5 — The Semicolon in Complex Lists

Beyond joining two independent clauses, the semicolon has one other legitimate use: **separating list items when the items themselves contain commas.**

> *The conference included speakers from Paris, France; London, England; and Berlin, Germany.*

Without semicolons, this list would be impenetrable. The semicolons act as "super commas" to clarify where one item ends and the next begins.

**ACT Note:** This usage appears less frequently than the IC;IC joining function, but it does appear, and it is the only other correct use of the semicolon on the test.

### 4.2.6 — Process for Evaluating Sentence Structure Questions

When you encounter a question where the answer choices vary in punctuation or sentence structure, execute this process:

**Step 1: Identify the Clauses**
Read the sentence (and surrounding context if needed) and identify each subject-verb pair. Determine which are independent and which are dependent.

**Step 2: Count the ICs**
- **One IC:** You have a simple sentence or a complex sentence (IC + DC). No run-on risk.
- **Two ICs:** You need one of the five legitimate joining methods. Check which answer uses one.
- **Three or more ICs:** You have a compound or compound-complex sentence. Each junction between ICs must be properly joined.

**Step 3: Eliminate Illegal Joins**
Eliminate any answer that uses:
- No punctuation between two ICs
- A comma alone between two ICs
- A transition word with only a comma (or commas) to join ICs
- A colon after an incomplete sentence
- A semicolon + FANBOYS (never correct)

**Step 4: Verify the Remaining Choice**
Confirm that the surviving answer uses one of the five legitimate methods and that the meaning is logical.

---

## 4.3 — Sentence Fragments

### 4.3.1 — What Is a Fragment?

A **fragment** is a group of words punctuated as a sentence but lacking one or more of the requirements of a complete sentence. On the ACT, fragments are always wrong.

A complete sentence requires:
1. A subject
2. A conjugated verb
3. A complete thought (not left hanging by a subordinating conjunction)

### 4.3.2 — Common Fragment Types on the ACT

**Type 1: Subordinating Conjunction Fragment**
A dependent clause left standing alone.

> ✗ *Because the experiment yielded unexpected results.*  
> ✓ *The team repeated the experiment because the initial results were unexpected.*  
> ✓ *Because the experiment yielded unexpected results, the team decided to repeat it.*

**Type 2: Relative Clause Fragment**
A relative clause standing alone.

> ✗ *Which was discovered entirely by accident.*  
> ✓ *The finding, which was discovered entirely by accident, revolutionized the field.*

**Type 3: "-ing" Fragment**
A gerund or participle phrase standing alone without a main clause.

> ✗ *Running through the forest during the thunderstorm.*  
> ✓ *She was running through the forest during the thunderstorm.*  
> ✓ *Running through the forest during the thunderstorm, she felt exhilarated.*

**Type 4: Missing Subject**
A verb phrase without a clear subject.

> ✗ *Went to the store and bought milk.*  
> ✓ *She went to the store and bought milk.*

**Type 5: Missing Verb**
A subject phrase without a conjugated verb.

> ✗ *The old house on the corner of Maple Street.*  
> ✓ *The old house on the corner of Maple Street was demolished.*

**Type 6: Prepositional Phrase Fragment**
A prepositional phrase punctuated as a sentence.

> ✗ *After the long and exhausting journey.*  
> ✓ *After the long and exhausting journey, they finally arrived home.*

### 4.3.3 — The ACT's Favorite Fragment Trick

The ACT frequently creates fragments by taking a perfectly good sentence and adding a subordinating conjunction to the beginning, then offering this fragment as an answer choice. Students who read quickly may not notice the conjunction.

> Original: *The discovery changed the course of scientific history.*  
> Fragment offered as choice: ***Which** changed the course of scientific history.*

The fragment begins with "which" (a relative pronoun), making it a dependent clause that cannot stand alone. The correct answer will either remove "which" or attach the clause to an existing sentence.

### 4.3.4 — Process for Fragment Detection

1. Read the entire sentence from start to finish.
2. Ask: "Is there a subject? Is there a conjugated verb? Does the thought feel complete?"
3. If the sentence begins with a subordinating conjunction (because, although, when, if, etc.) or a relative pronoun (which, who, that), check whether there is an independent clause to which it is attached.
4. If the answer choice is a fragment, eliminate it.

---

## 4.4 — Modifier Logic: The Complete System

Modifiers are words, phrases, or clauses that describe other words in a sentence. The ACT tests modifiers relentlessly, and the errors always involve **misplacement** or **illogical attachment**. The three modifier positions—introductory, middle, and ending—each have their own rules.

### 4.4.1 — Introductory Comma Clauses (ICCs)

**Definition:** An introductory comma clause is a dependent clause or phrase that appears at the beginning of a sentence, before the main clause. It is followed by a comma.

**Structure:** ICC, IC

**Examples:**
> *Walking down the street, she noticed the new restaurant.* (introductory participial phrase)  
> *Because the weather was nice, they decided to have a picnic.* (introductory dependent clause)  
> *After finishing his homework, Jake went to bed.* (introductory prepositional phrase with gerund)

#### The Cardinal Rule of ICCs:

> **The subject of the main clause (the IC that follows the comma) MUST be the logical performer of the action described in the ICC.**

This is the **dangling modifier** rule, and it is one of the most frequently tested concepts on the entire ACT English section.

**Correct:**
> *Walking down the street, **she** noticed the new restaurant.*  
> "She" is the one walking. This makes sense.

**Incorrect (Dangling Modifier):**
> ✗ *Walking down the street, **the trees** were beautiful.*  
> The trees were not walking. This is illogical.

**How to fix a dangling modifier:**
You have two options:
1. Change the subject of the main clause to one that can perform the action.
   > ✓ *Walking down the street, **I** noticed the beautiful trees.*
2. Rewrite the ICC to include its own subject.
   > ✓ ***As I walked** down the street, the trees were beautiful.*

#### ICCs Beginning with "-ing" Words (Participial Phrases)

These are the most common ICCs on the ACT. The "-ing" word (present participle) implies that the subject of the main clause is performing the action.

> *Rushing to catch the bus, **she** dropped her keys.* (She was rushing — correct)  
> ✗ *Rushing to catch the bus, **the keys** fell on the pavement.* (The keys were not rushing — dangling modifier)

#### ICCs Beginning with "-ed" Words (Past Participial Phrases)

These imply that the subject of the main clause is the recipient of the action.

> *Exhausted from the journey, **she** collapsed on the bed.* (She was exhausted — correct)  
> ✗ *Exhausted from the journey, **the bed** looked inviting.* (The bed was not exhausted — dangling modifier)

#### ICCs Beginning with Prepositions

Prepositional phrases at the beginning of a sentence function as ICCs when they describe an action or state that must be logically connected to the subject.

> *After three hours of deliberation, **the jury** reached a verdict.* (The jury deliberated — correct)  
> ✗ *After three hours of deliberation, **the verdict** was finally reached.* (The verdict did not deliberate — dangling modifier)

#### ICCs Beginning with Subordinating Conjunctions

When the ICC is a full dependent clause (with its own subject and verb), the modifier rule still applies to the subject of the main clause, but the logic test is slightly different: the situation described in the ICC must logically connect to the subject/action of the main clause.

> *Because she had studied diligently, **she** aced the exam.* (Logical connection — correct)  
> ✗ *Because she had studied diligently, **the exam** was aced easily.* (Technically acceptable in casual writing, but the ACT may flag this as a weak connection. The exam didn't study.)

### 4.4.2 — Middle Comma Clauses (MCCs) / Nonessential Clauses

**Definition:** A middle comma clause is a clause or phrase that interrupts a sentence between the subject and the verb (or at another point within the sentence) and is set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses on both sides.

**Structure:** Subject + [MCC] + Verb...

**Examples:**
> *The CEO, **who was appointed last year**, announced the merger.*  
> *The novel, **written in 1925**, remains a classic.*  
> *My brother, **surprisingly enough**, agreed to help.*

#### The Three Punctuation Options for MCCs

All three are interchangeable in terms of grammatical correctness, but they differ in emphasis:

| Punctuation | Emphasis Level | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| **Commas** | Standard | Neutral; the information is supplementary |
| **Dashes** | Most emphatic | Draws attention to the information; creates a dramatic pause |
| **Parentheses** | Least emphatic | De-emphasizes the information; treats it as an aside |

**ACT Rule:** The choice between commas, dashes, and parentheses is a style question, not a grammar error. If the ACT tests this, the correct answer will match the tone and emphasis of the passage. In general:
- Use **commas** for standard appositives and nonrestrictive clauses.
- Use **dashes** when the information is surprising, dramatic, or deserves special attention.
- Use **parentheses** when the information is a minor aside or tangential note.

#### The Appositive (A Special Type of MCC)

An **appositive** is a noun or noun phrase that renames or identifies another noun. Appositives are always set off by commas (or dashes/parentheses) when they are nonessential.

> *My friend **an accomplished pianist** performed at the gala.*  
> *The Amazon **the largest rainforest in the world** is home to millions of species.*

If the appositive is essential to identify which person/thing you mean, no commas are used:

> *The novel **Pride and Prejudice** was written by Jane Austen.* (Essential — there are many novels; we need to specify which one)

> *Novelist **Jane Austen** wrote Pride and Prejudice.* (Nonessential — "Jane Austen" is already identified; the name adds extra info. Commas needed.)

#### Testing MCCs on the ACT

The ACT tests MCCs in several ways:

1. **Missing commas:** An MCC without commas on both sides is an error.
   > ✗ *The CEO who was appointed last year announced the merger.* (If "who was appointed last year" is nonessential, it needs commas.)

2. **Unnecessary commas:** An essential clause with commas is an error.
   > ✗ *The students, who passed the exam, celebrated.* (This implies ALL students passed. If only some passed, no commas: *The students who passed the exam celebrated.*)

3. **Mismatched punctuation:** An MCC opened with a comma but closed with a period (or opened with a dash but closed with a parenthesis) is an error. The punctuation must match.

4. **MCCs that create subject-verb separation:** While grammatically acceptable, the ACT sometimes tests whether an MCC is so long or complex that it obscures the subject-verb connection. In such cases, the correct answer may restructure the sentence.

### 4.4.3 — Ending Comma Clauses (ECCs)

**Definition:** An ending comma clause is a clause or phrase that appears at the end of a sentence, after the main clause, and is set off by a comma.

**Structure:** IC, + modifier

**Examples:**
> *She completed the marathon, **exhausted but triumphant**.*  
> *The magician walked across the stage, **dazzling the crowd with every move**.*  
> *The team celebrated their victory, **which they had worked toward for years**.*

#### The Critical Rule for ECCs:

**An ECC that begins with an "-ing" adverb must logically describe the subject of the main clause.** However, unlike ICCs, ECCs are more flexible in placement. An ECC can be placed next to a noun that is NOT the subject, as long as the overall meaning is that the subject is performing the action.

**Correct:**
> *The magician walked across the stage, dazzling the crowd.*  
> The magician is the one dazzling. Even though "the crowd" is closer to "dazzling," the logical meaning is clear: the magician dazzles.

**This is a key difference from ICCs:** With ICCs, the subject MUST be the performer. With ECCs, the modifier can describe the action of the whole clause, even if it is placed near a different noun.

#### When ECCs Become Errors

An ECC becomes an error when:
1. **The comma is omitted**, creating a modifier error.
   > ✗ *She ran through the park feeling the sunshine on her face.* (Ambiguous — who is feeling? The grammar suggests "she," but without the comma, the structure is unclear.)
   > ✓ *She ran through the park, feeling the sunshine on her face.* (The comma clarifies the modifier relationship.)

2. **The modifier illogically attaches to the nearest noun.**
   > ✗ *The chef prepared the meal, garnishing it with fresh herbs that were delicious.* (This suggests the herbs were being garnished, which is nonsensical. The sentence needs restructuring.)

### 4.4.4 — The Complete Modifier Decision Tree

When you encounter a modifier question on the ACT, ask yourself these questions in order:

**Question 1: Where is the modifier positioned?**
- **Beginning of sentence → ICC.** The subject of the main clause must perform the action.
- **Middle of sentence → MCC.** Must be set off by commas/dashes/parentheses on both sides. Must be nonessential (or essential without commas).
- **End of sentence → ECC.** Must be set off by a comma. Must logically describe the action of the main clause.

**Question 2: Is the modifier logically connected to what it is supposed to describe?**
- For ICCs: Can the subject actually perform the action?
- For MCCs: Does the clause correctly describe the noun it interrupts?
- For ECCs: Does the clause logically describe the action of the sentence?

**Question 3: Is the punctuation correct?**
- MCCs: Matching commas, dashes, or parentheses on both sides.
- ECCs: Comma before the modifier.
- ICCs: Comma after the introductory clause.

---

## 4.5 — Parallelism

### 4.5.1 — What Is Parallelism?

Parallelism is the rule that elements in a list, comparison, or paired construction must have the **same grammatical form**. If you start listing items as nouns, all items must be nouns. If you start with an infinitive, all items must be infinitives. Mixing forms creates a parallelism error.

### 4.5.2 — Parallelism in Lists

**Rule:** All items in a list must share the same grammatical structure.

> ✗ *She enjoys **swimming**, **to run**, and **biking**.* (Mixed: gerund, infinitive, gerund)  
> ✓ *She enjoys **swimming**, **running**, and **biking**.* (All gerunds)  
> ✓ *She enjoys **to swim**, **to run**, and **to bike**.* (All infinitives)

**The ACT Pattern:** The test often presents lists where the first two items are parallel but the third breaks the pattern, or where the list items are parallel in form but one contains a word that doesn't fit the category.

### 4.5.3 — Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

Correlative conjunctions come in pairs. The structure that follows the first element must match the structure that follows the second element.

| Correlative Pair | Example (Correct) | Example (Incorrect) |
|---|---|---|
| **either...or** | *She **either** will call **or** will text.* | *She **either** will call **or** texts.* |
| **neither...nor** | *He **neither** confirmed **nor** denied the rumor.* | *He **neither** confirmed **nor** denying the rumor.* |
| **both...and** | *The plan is **both** innovative **and** practical.* | *The plan is **both** innovative **and** practically.* |
| **not only...but also** | *She **not only** excels academically **but also** athletically.* | *She **not only** excels academically **but also** in athletics.* |
| **whether...or** | *I don't know **whether** to laugh **or** to cry.* | *I don't know **whether** to laugh **or** crying.* |
| **as...as** | *The test was **as** difficult **as** expected.* | *The test was **as** difficult **as** expecting.* |
| **rather...than** | *I would **rather** read **than** watch TV.* | *I would **rather** read **than** watching TV.* |

**The Key Insight:** You don't need to memorize every possible combination. Instead, look at the grammatical form immediately after the first correlative word and ensure the same form appears after the second.

### 4.5.4 — Parallelism in Comparisons

When comparing two things, the items being compared must be grammatically parallel.

> ✗ *Running a marathon is harder than **to swim** a mile.*  
> ✓ ***Running** a marathon is harder than **swimming** a mile.*  
> ✓ *It is harder **to run** a marathon than **to swim** a mile.*

> ✗ *The new building is taller than **the old one is**.* (Technically acceptable in casual speech, but the ACT prefers strict parallelism.)  
> ✓ *The new building is taller than **the old one**.*  
> ✓ *The new building is taller than **is the old one**.* (Inverted but parallel)

### 4.5.5 — Parallelism with Verbs in a Series

When a single subject performs multiple actions, all verbs must be in the same form.

> ✗ *The artist **painted** the canvas, **added** details, and **was signing** the finished work.*  
> ✓ *The artist **painted** the canvas, **added** details, and **signed** the finished work.*

### 4.5.6 — The "Hidden" Parallelism Error

Sometimes the parallelism error is not obvious because the items are long or complex. The ACT loves to bury parallelism errors inside prepositional phrases or relative clauses.

> ✗ *The course requires **reading** extensive texts, **to write** weekly essays, and **participation** in class discussions.*  
> Items: "reading" (gerund), "to write" (infinitive), "participation" (noun) — NOT parallel  
> ✓ *The course requires **reading** extensive texts, **writing** weekly essays, and **participating** in class discussions.*  
> All three are gerunds — parallel.

### 4.5.7 — Process for Parallelism Questions

1. Identify the structural signal: a list (commas + "and/or"), a correlative conjunction (either/or, not only/but also), or a comparison (than, as).
2. Look at the grammatical form of the first item in the series.
3. Check that every subsequent item matches that form.
4. If the answer choices offer different forms, select the one that creates full parallelism.
5. Be especially alert for the last item in a list—this is where the ACT most commonly places the error.

---

## 4.6 — Advanced Sentence Structure Patterns

### 4.6.1 — Cumulative vs. Periodic Sentences

While the ACT does not test these terms directly, understanding them helps you evaluate sentence flow and structure.

- **Cumulative sentence:** The main clause comes first, followed by modifying details.
  > *She walked into the room, her eyes scanning every corner, her hand gripping the doorframe.*

- **Periodic sentence:** Modifiers and dependent clauses come first, building to the main clause at the end.
  > *Despite the rain, despite the cold, despite the exhaustion that threatened to overwhelm her, she kept walking.*

The ACT may test whether a sentence's structure effectively communicates its meaning. Periodic sentences create suspense; cumulative sentences feel more direct. Neither is inherently wrong, but the test may ask which structure better serves the passage's purpose.

### 4.6.2 — Sentence Combining

Some ACT questions ask you to combine two short sentences into one. The key is to combine them using one of the five legitimate methods (for ICs) or by subordinating one to the other.

**Two short sentences:**
> *The storm was severe. It caused widespread damage.*

**Possible combinations:**
1. *The storm was severe, and it caused widespread damage.* (comma + FANBOYS)
2. *The storm was severe; it caused widespread damage.* (semicolon)
3. *The storm was severe; consequently, it caused widespread damage.* (semicolon + transition + comma)
4. *The severe storm caused widespread damage.* (simple sentence — most concise)
5. *Because the storm was severe, it caused widespread damage.* (subordination)

**ACT Preference:** When combining sentences, the ACT almost always prefers the most concise option that preserves all original meaning. If one of the original sentences can be reduced to a modifier or combined into a single subject-verb structure, that is usually the correct answer.

### 4.6.3 — Sentence Splitting

Conversely, the ACT may ask you to split one long sentence into two. The key is to ensure that each resulting sentence is a complete independent clause.

> *The researcher conducted the experiment over a period of six months, and the results, which were published in a prestigious journal, exceeded all expectations.*

**Possible split:**
> *The researcher conducted the experiment over a period of six months. The results, which were published in a prestigious journal, exceeded all expectations.*

Both halves are complete sentences. The split is clean.

### 4.6.4 — The "Sentence Boundaries" Question Type

Some questions on the ACT ask whether a particular sentence should be divided into two sentences or kept as one. The decision depends on:

1. **Length:** If a sentence is excessively long and difficult to follow, splitting it may improve clarity.
2. **Focus:** If a sentence tries to communicate two distinct ideas, each idea may deserve its own sentence.
3. **Rhythm:** Varied sentence lengths create better rhythm. A passage of all long sentences or all short sentences feels monotonous.
4. **Logical connection:** If two ideas are closely related, keeping them in one sentence (properly joined) may better show their relationship.

---

## 4.7 — Subject-Verb Agreement in Complex Structures

(For a complete treatment of SVA, see Chapter 5. This section focuses on how SVA interacts with sentence structure.)

### 4.7.1 — The Core Principle

The verb must agree in number with its subject. This sounds simple, but the ACT makes it difficult by inserting words, phrases, and clauses between the subject and verb.

### 4.7.2 — The Isolation Process

When you see a subject-verb agreement question, use this process:

**Step 1: Find the verb in question.** Identify what the answer choices are varying (singular vs. plural verb).

**Step 2: Find the true subject.** Ask: "Who or what is performing this verb?"

**Step 3: Eliminate all distractor phrases.** Cross out:
- Prepositional phrases between subject and verb (*of the students, in the classroom, with the red hat*)
- Relative clauses (*who was running, which were discovered*)
- Comma clauses / appositives (*, who is my friend,*)
- Interrupting phrases (*, as well as the teacher,*)
- "Along with," "as well as," "in addition to," "together with" phrases

**Step 4: Identify the remaining subject and determine if it is singular or plural.**

**Step 5: Match the verb to the subject.**

### 4.7.3 — Common SVA Traps on the ACT

**Trap 1: Prepositional Phrase with a Plural Noun**
> *The box **of chocolates** is on the table.* (Subject = "box," singular)  
> ✗ *The box of chocolates **are** on the table.*

**Trap 2: Relative Clause Between Subject and Verb**
> *The results **that were published** in the journal **confirm** the theory.* (Subject = "results," plural)  
> ✗ *The results that were published in the journal **confirms** the theory.*

**Trap 3: "Each" or "Every" Before a Plural Noun**
> *Each **of the students** **has** a textbook.* (Subject = "each," singular)  
> ✗ *Each of the students **have** a textbook.*

**Trap 4: "There" Sentences**
When a sentence begins with "There," the subject comes AFTER the verb.
> *There **are** three reasons for this.* (Subject = "reasons," plural)  
> ✗ *There **is** three reasons for this.*

**Trap 5: Compound Subjects with "and"**
> *The cat **and** the dog **are** playing.* (Compound subject = plural)  
> Exception: If "and" connects two words that refer to a single entity: *The fish **and chips** **is** my favorite dish.* (One dish)

**Trap 6: "As Well as" / "Along With" / "In Addition To"**
These phrases do NOT create compound subjects. The subject is what comes BEFORE the phrase.
> *The teacher, **along with her students**, **is** fielding questions.* (Subject = "teacher," singular)  
> ✗ *The teacher, along with her students, **are** fielding questions.*

### 4.7.4 — Collective Nouns

Collective nouns (team, committee, family, group, audience, class, jury, staff, majority) are usually **singular** when the group acts as a unit and **plural** when members act individually.

> *The team **is** playing well.* (Acting as a unit)  
> *The team **are** arguing among themselves.* (Acting individually — more common in British English, but the ACT generally treats these as singular)

**ACT Default:** Treat collective nouns as singular unless the context clearly indicates individual members acting separately.

---

## 4.8 — Structural Redundancy and Wordiness

While wordiness is primarily a rhetorical skill (covered in Chapter 7), it intersects with sentence structure when a structural choice creates unnecessary words.

### 4.8.1 — Redundant Structure

> ✗ *The reason is because the experiment failed.* ("The reason is because" = redundant)  
> ✓ *The reason is that the experiment failed.*  
> ✓ *Because the experiment failed, the team regrouped.* (Even more concise)

> ✗ *Despite the fact that it was raining, we went outside.* (Wordy)  
> ✓ *Despite the rain, we went outside.*  
> ✓ *Although it was raining, we went outside.*

> ✗ *In the event that you need assistance, please call this number.* (Wordy)  
> ✓ *If you need assistance, please call this number.*

### 4.8.2 — The "Being" and "There Is/Are" Constructions

The ACT generally disfavors these constructions because they add words without adding meaning.

> ✗ *There are many reasons that explain why the policy failed.*  
> ✓ *Many reasons explain why the policy failed.*  
> ✓ *The policy failed for many reasons.*

> ✗ *The solution is being implemented by the committee.*  
> ✓ *The committee is implementing the solution.*

### 4.8.3 — Nominalization

Nominalization is the process of turning a verb into a noun, which often requires adding extra words.

> ✗ *The committee made a **decision** to conduct an **investigation**.* (Nominalizations: decision, investigation)  
> ✓ *The committee **decided** to **investigate**.* (Verbs: decided, investigate)

The ACT rarely asks you to fix nominalization directly, but recognizing it helps you identify the most concise answer choice.

---

## 4.9 — Putting It All Together: The Sentence Structure Diagnostic

When you encounter any question on the ACT English section that involves sentence structure, run this comprehensive diagnostic:

### Step 1: Read the Full Sentence
Never read only the underlined portion. Read the entire sentence, and if necessary, the sentences before and after.

### Step 2: Identify All Clauses
Find every subject-verb pair. Label each as independent (IC) or dependent (DC).

### Step 3: Check Clause Junctions
If there are two or more ICs, verify they are joined by one of the five legitimate methods. If not, you have found the error.

### Step 4: Check Modifiers
For every modifier (introductory, middle, or ending), verify:
- ICCs: Does the subject perform the action?
- MCCs: Is it properly set off? Is it essential or nonessential?
- ECCs: Does it logically describe the action? Is the comma present?

### Step 5: Check Parallelism
If there is a list, comparison, or correlative conjunction, verify that all elements are in the same grammatical form.

### Step 6: Check Subject-Verb Agreement
Isolate the subject from all intervening phrases and verify the verb matches in number.

### Step 7: Check for Fragments
Ensure every sentence has a subject, a verb, and a complete thought.

### Step 8: Evaluate Conciseness
If multiple answers are grammatically correct, choose the most concise one that preserves all meaning.

---

## 4.10 — Grammar Reference Tables

### Table 4.1: The Five Legitimate Ways to Join Two Independent Clauses

| Method | Punctuation | Example | When to Use |
|---|---|---|---|
| Semicolon | IC ; IC | *She ran; he walked.* | Closely related ideas of equal importance |
| Comma + FANBOYS | IC, and IC | *She ran, and he walked.* | Closely related ideas; the conjunction specifies the relationship |
| Semicolon + Transition + Comma | IC; however, IC | *She ran; however, he walked.* | Closely related ideas with a specific logical transition |
| Period | IC. IC. | *She ran. He walked.* | Distinct ideas that can stand alone |
| Colon | IC: IC | *She ran: he walked.* | The second clause explains or elaborates the first |

### Table 4.2: Transition Words by Logical Function

| Function | Transitions | Example Pattern |
|---|---|---|
| Addition | furthermore, moreover, additionally, also, besides, in addition | IC; furthermore, IC |
| Contrast | however, nevertheless, on the other hand, conversely, yet, still, instead | IC; however, IC |
| Cause/Effect | therefore, consequently, thus, hence, accordingly, as a result, so | IC; therefore, IC |
| Illustration | for example, for instance, specifically, to illustrate, in particular | IC; for example, IC |
| Similarity | similarly, likewise, in the same way, by the same token | IC; similarly, IC |
| Concession | granted, admittedly, to be sure, of course, naturally, certainly | Granted, IC; nevertheless, IC |
| Sequence | first, second, next, then, finally, subsequently, meanwhile | IC; next, IC |
| Conclusion | in conclusion, in summary, to sum up, ultimately, in short | IC; in conclusion, IC |
| Emphasis | indeed, in fact, as a matter of fact, certainly, undoubtedly | IC; indeed, IC |

### Table 4.3: Modifier Position Rules

| Position | Name | Rule | Comma Usage |
|---|---|---|---|
| Beginning | Introductory Comma Clause (ICC) | Subject must perform the action | Comma AFTER the ICC |
| Middle | Middle Comma Clause (MCC) | Must be nonessential (or essential without commas) | Commas/dashes/parentheses on BOTH sides |
| End | Ending Comma Clause (ECC) | Must logically describe the action of the main clause | Comma BEFORE the ECC |

### Table 4.4: Common Parallelism Errors

| Error Type | Incorrect | Correct |
|---|---|---|
| Mixed gerund/infinitive | *She likes **swimming** and **to run**.* | *She likes **swimming** and **running**.* |
| Mixed verb forms | *He **opened** the door and **was walking** inside.* | *He **opened** the door and **walked** inside.* |
| Correlative mismatch | *She **not only** is smart **but also** works hard.* | *She **not only** is smart **but also** is hardworking.* |
| Comparison mismatch | *Running is harder than **to swim**.* | *Running is harder than **swimming**.* |
| Mixed parts of speech | *The job requires **experience**, **patience**, and **to be** detail-oriented.* | *The job requires **experience**, **patience**, and **attention to detail**.* |

### Table 4.5: Quick Fragment Detection Checklist

| Check | If Missing → Fragment |
|---|---|
| Does the sentence have a subject? | ✗ *Running through the park.* (No subject — who is running?) |
| Does the sentence have a conjugated verb? | ✗ *The old house on the corner.* (No verb — what happened?) |
| Does the sentence express a complete thought? | ✗ *Because she was tired.* (Subordinating conjunction leaves us hanging) |
| If it begins with "which," "who," or "that," is there a main clause? | ✗ *Which was discovered in 1922.* (Attached to nothing) |

---

## 4.11 — Strategic Summary: Sentence Structure and Modifier Logic

**The 10 Commandments of Sentence Structure on the ACT:**

1. **Two independent clauses must be joined by one of five methods only.** No other method is legal.
2. **A comma alone can never join two independent clauses.** This is a comma splice—always wrong.
3. **"However" is not a coordinating conjunction.** It cannot join clauses with commas alone.
4. **A colon must be preceded by a complete sentence.** Never after a verb or preposition.
5. **A semicolon can never be paired with "and"** to join two clauses.
6. **The subject after an introductory comma clause must perform the action** described in the ICC.
7. **Middle comma clauses must be set off on both sides** with matching punctuation.
8. **Items in a list must be grammatically parallel.** Check every item, especially the last.
9. **The subject of a sentence can never be found inside a prepositional phrase, relative clause, or comma clause.**
10. **When in doubt, choose the most concise grammatically correct answer.**

---

## 4.12 — Common Misconceptions and How to Overcome Them

### Misconception 1: "Longer sentences are better."
**Reality:** The ACT rewards conciseness. A short, clear sentence is almost always preferable to a long, convoluted one—provided it contains all necessary information.

### Misconception 2: "If it sounds right, it is right."
**Reality:** Spoken English tolerates errors that formal written English does not. Dangling modifiers, comma splices, and parallelism errors often "sound fine" in speech because the listener fills in the missing logic. The ACT tests formal written English exclusively.

### Misconception 3: "Commas are just pauses."
**Reality:** Commas follow structural rules, not breath patterns. The ACT tests comma placement based on grammar, not intuition. Every comma in the correct answer must be justified by a specific rule.

### Misconception 4: "A sentence must have a subject and a verb, so any group of words with both is a complete sentence."
**Reality:** A group of words can have a subject and a verb and still be a fragment if it begins with a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun. "Because she ran" has a subject and a verb but is not a complete sentence.

### Misconception 5: "Modifiers can go anywhere as long as the reader understands the meaning."
**Reality:** In formal English, modifiers must be placed as close as possible to the words they modify. A modifier at the beginning of a sentence MUST describe the subject. Misplaced modifiers create ambiguity or absurdity, and the ACT treats both as errors.

---

## 4.13 — Advanced Practice Patterns

While this chapter does not include practice problems (per the instructions), here are the patterns you should look for when practicing with official ACT materials:

**Pattern 1: The Subject-Verb Separation Trap**
Look for sentences where the subject and verb are separated by a long prepositional phrase, relative clause, or appositive. Practice isolating the subject.

**Pattern 2: The Dangling Modifier at the Start**
Look for sentences beginning with "-ing" or "-ed" phrases. Check whether the subject of the main clause can logically perform the action.

**Pattern 3: The Comma Splice Disguised with a Transition**
Look for sentences where two independent clauses are joined by a comma + "however/therefore/moreover." This is always wrong.

**Pattern 4: The Broken Parallelism in a Long List**
Look for lists where the first two items match but the third (or fourth) breaks the pattern.

**Pattern 5: The Nonessential Clause Missing Commas**
Look for appositives or relative clauses that add extra information but lack the required commas.

**Pattern 6: The "There Is/Are" Wordiness**
Look for sentences beginning with "There is" or "There are" that could be rewritten more concisely.

**Pattern 7: The Colon After a Verb or Preposition**
Look for colons placed immediately after verbs like "include," "are," "is," or prepositions like "of," "by," "for." This is always wrong.

**Pattern 8: The Fragment Masquerading as a Sentence**
Look for answer choices that are dependent clauses (starting with "because," "although," "which," "that," "-ing" words) presented as complete sentences.

---

## 4.14 — Chapter Summary and Key Takeaways

Sentence structure and modifier logic are not just grammar topics—they are the **architectural principles** of the ACT English section. Every passage you read, every question you answer, and every answer choice you evaluate depends on your ability to:

1. **Identify clauses** and distinguish independent from dependent.
2. **Join clauses correctly** using one of the five legitimate methods.
3. **Recognize and fix run-ons, comma splices, and fragments.**
4. **Place modifiers logically** so they describe what they are meant to describe.
5. **Maintain parallelism** in lists, comparisons, and correlative constructions.
6. **Isolate subjects** from intervening phrases to check subject-verb agreement.
7. **Choose conciseness** when multiple answers are grammatically correct.

Master these skills, and you will have addressed the single largest category of ACT English questions. The remaining grammar topics (pronouns, verb tense, punctuation details) and rhetoric skills build on this foundation.

In the next chapter, we will deep-dive into **Punctuation Mastery**—the rules governing commas, semicolons, colons, dashes, apostrophes, and other marks that the ACT tests with surgical precision.

---

---


# Chapter 5: Deep-Dive Grammar Mastery — Verb Tenses, Agreement, and Pronoun Precision

---

## Introduction to the Triad of Grammar Precision

The ACT English section's "Conventions of Standard English" reporting category constitutes roughly 38–55% of all questions depending on the format you encounter. Within this category, three subcategories form the grammatical backbone of the test: **Verb Tenses** (ensuring temporal consistency and correct tense deployment), **Subject-Verb Agreement** (ensuring that the verb matches its subject in number), and **Pronoun Usage** (ensuring that every pronoun has a clear, unambiguous antecedent and takes the correct case form).

These three categories are not merely tested in isolation. The ACT deliberately layers them. A single question might test both subject-verb agreement AND verb tense simultaneously, or combine pronoun-antecedent agreement with case precision. To achieve mastery, you must understand each system at a foundational level, recognize how the systems interact, and develop the ability to parse even the most deliberately convoluted sentence structures the test architects construct.

This chapter will exhaustively dissect each of these three systems, rule by rule, nuance by nuance, trap by trap.

---

# PART ONE: VERB TENSES — THE COMPREHENSIVE SYSTEM

---

## 5.1 The Architecture of English Tenses

English contains twelve primary tense-aspect combinations, but the ACT tests a specific subset of these with high frequency. You do not need to memorize obscure tense forms, but you must have absolute command of the following:

- **Simple Present**
- **Simple Past**
- **Simple Future**
- **Present Perfect**
- **Past Perfect**
- **Future Perfect**
- **Present Progressive (Continuous)**
- **Past Progressive**
- **Present Perfect Progressive**
- **Past Perfect Progressive**

Each of these serves a distinct communicative function, and the ACT tests whether the writer has selected the tense that most accurately communicates the temporal reality being described.

### Core Principle: Temporal Consistency

Before examining each tense individually, you must internalize the single most important principle governing verb tense on the ACT:

> **Within a sentence, paragraph, or passage, verb tenses must remain logically consistent. When a shift in time occurs, the tense must change to reflect that shift. When no shift in time occurs, the tense must not change.**

This sounds deceptively simple. In practice, the ACT exploits this principle by constructing passages where the baseline tense shifts subtly, and you must detect whether that shift is warranted or erroneous.

**The ACT's favorite tense error pattern:**

A passage written primarily in the simple past tense that suddenly shifts to simple present for no logical reason, or vice versa.
