The student who reads passively absorbs the shift without questioning it. The student who reads actively asks: "Does this action actually occur in a different time frame than the surrounding actions, or is this an unjustified tense shift?"

---

## 5.2 Simple Present Tense

### Formation

| Subject | Verb Form |
|---|---|
| I / You / We / They | base form (walk, go, see) |
| He / She / It | base form + s/es (walks, goes, sees) |

### Uses on the ACT

1. **Habitual or repeated actions:** "She *walks* to school every morning."
2. **General truths or scientific facts:** "Water *boils* at 100 degrees Celsius."
3. **States of being (stative verbs):** "He *believes* in discipline," "The house *stands* on a hill."
4. **Scheduled future events (especially with timetables):** "The train *arrives* at 3:00 PM."

### Critical ACT Nuance: Stative Verbs

Some English verbs are **stative** — they describe states of being, possession, emotion, perception, or mental processes rather than actions. These verbs are rarely used in progressive (continuous -ing) forms in formal English, even though casual speech sometimes permits it.

**Common stative verbs to know:**

- **Possession:** own, possess, belong, contain, have (when meaning "possess")
- **Mental states:** know, believe, understand, remember, forget, recognize, suppose, think (when meaning "believe"), mean
- **Emotion/Desire:** love, hate, like, dislike, prefer, want, wish, desire
- **Perception:** see, hear, smell, taste, feel, seem, appear, sound
- **Other states:** consist, contain, exist, matter, weigh (when stating a fact), measure (when stating a fact)

**Example of ACT trap:**

- ❌ "I *am wanting* to improve my score." (stative verb in progressive — incorrect in formal English)
- ✅ "I *want* to improve my score."

- ❌ "The committee *is consisting* of twelve members."
- ✅ "The committee *consists* of twelve members."

However, some of these verbs have **different meanings** in their progressive forms:

- "I *think* this is correct" (think = believe → stative)
- "I *am thinking* about the problem" (think = consider → active, progressive is fine)
- "The soup *tastes* great" (tastes = has a flavor → stative)
- "She *is tasting* the soup" (tasting = actively testing flavor → active)
- "He *has* a car" (has = possesses → stative)
- "He *is having* lunch" (has = eating → active)

The ACT occasionally tests this distinction. When in doubt, ask whether the verb describes a state or an action.

### Simple Present for Narrative Purposes

Literary narrative passages sometimes use the **historical present** (also called the **narrative present**) to make past events feel more vivid. The ACT occasionally employs this technique in prose fiction excerpts. The key is that the use must be consistent — if the passage has adopted the historical present, it must maintain it.

---

## 5.3 Simple Past Tense

### Formation

The simple past uses a single verb form that does not change for person or number:

- Regular verbs: base form + ed (walked, talked, examined)
- Irregular verbs: memorized forms (went, saw, wrote, thought, ran, brought)

### Uses on the ACT

1. **Completed actions at a specific past time:** "She *walked* to school yesterday."
2. **Completed actions in a sequence:** "He *opened* the door, *entered* the room, and *sat* down."
3. **Past habitual actions (often used to):** "He *used to* play piano every evening."

### Irregular Verbs

While the ACT does not test obscure irregular verb forms, it does expect you to recognize the correct standard forms of common irregular verbs. Errors typically involve:

- Using the past participle without a helping verb where the simple past is required
- Confusing the simple past form with the past participle
- Creating non-standard forms (e.g., "bringed" instead of "brought")

**Common irregular verbs ACT-tested:**

| Base Form | Simple Past | Past Participle |
|---|---|---|
| begin | began | begun |
| break | broke | broken |
| bring | brought | brought |
| choose | chose | chosen |
| do | did | done |
| drink | drank | drunk |
| drive | drove | driven |
| eat | ate | eaten |
| forget | forgot | forgotten |
| give | gave | given |
| go | went | gone |
| lead | led | led |
| lie (recline) | lay | lain |
| lay (put down) | laid | laid |
| ride | rode | ridden |
| ring | rang | rung |
| rise | rose | risen |
| run | ran | run |
| see | saw | seen |
| speak | spoke | spoken |
| swim | swam | swum |
| take | took | taken |
| tear | tore | torn |
| write | wrote | written |

**Critical irregular verb trap: "Lead" vs. "Led"**

The base form "lead" (pronounced "seed") means to guide. The simple past is "led" (pronounced "led"). The ACT sometimes includes "lead" (the metal) as a distractor, which is pronounced "led" but spelled differently and means something entirely different. Context always resolves this.

---

## 5.4 Simple Future Tense

### Formation

- Subject + **will** + base form ("She will walk to school.")

### The "Would" Distinction

The ACT frequently tests the distinction between **will** and **would**:

- **Will** = future certainty from the present perspective: "I *will* take the ACT next month."
- **Would** = conditional, hypothetical, or future-in-the-past: "If I studied more, I *would* score higher." / "She said she *would* arrive at noon."

**ACT trap:** When a sentence begins with "If" (conditional), the conditional "would" is required, not "will":

- ❌ "If I *will* have time, I will help you."
- ✅ "If I *have* time, I will help you."

**The "if...will" exception:** "Will" is correct after "if" when it means "insist" or "be willing to":

- "If you *will* wait a moment, the doctor will see you." (= If you are willing to wait...)
- This is a niche test, but it exists.

---

## 5.5 Present Perfect Tense

This is the single most frequently tested tense on the ACT, and the one students most frequently mishandle.

### Formation

- Subject + **has/have** + past participle
- "She *has walked* to school many times." / "They *have completed* the project."

### Uses on the ACT

The present perfect communicates a relationship between the past and the present. It is used in the following specific circumstances:

**1. Actions that began in the past and continue into the present:**

- "I *have lived* in this city for ten years." (And I still live here.)
- "She *has been* a teacher since 2015." (And she is still a teacher.)

**Key signals:** *for* + duration, *since* + specific past point

**2. Actions that occurred at an unspecified past time, but have present relevance:**

- "I *have visited* Paris." (At some point before now — the experience shapes who I am now.)
- "Scientists *have discovered* a new species." (The discovery has current implications.)

**3. Recently completed actions with present results:**

- "She *has just finished* her homework." (Result: she is free now.)
- "He *has already eaten*." (Result: he is not hungry now.)

**Key signals:** *just, already, yet, recently, lately*

### The Present Perfect vs. Simple Past Distinction

This is one of the ACT's most commonly tested concepts. Here is how they differ:

| Present Perfect | Simple Past |
|---|---|
| "I *have read* that book." (at some unspecified time before now — the reading experience is relevant now) | "I *read* that book last summer." (specific past time — the action is complete and contained in the past) |
| "She *has visited* Tokyo." (experience — she knows Tokyo) | "She *visited* Tokyo in 2019." (specific past event) |

**Crucial rule: You CANNOT use the present perfect with a specific past time marker.**

- ❌ "I *have visited* Paris *last year*." ← Specific past time "last year" invalidates present perfect.
- ✅ "I *visited* Paris *last year*."
- ✅ "I *have visited* Paris." ← No specific past time — present perfect is acceptable.

**ACT trap phrases that invalidate present perfect:**

- last year, last week, last Monday
- yesterday
- in 2010
- three days ago
- when I was a child (specific past period)

**ACT trap phrases that signal present perfect:**

- for (duration): "for three years"
- since (point in time): "since 2019"
- already, yet, just, recently, lately
- so far, up to now, until now
- ever, never (in questions/experiences)
- this is the first/second/best/worst... (e.g., "This is the most beautiful place I *have ever visited*.")

### The "Been To" vs. "Gone To" Distinction

This niche distinction has appeared on the ACT:

- "She *has gone* to the store." = She went to the store and is still there (or is on her way there).
- "She *has been* to the store." = She went to the store and has now returned.

---

## 5.6 Past Perfect Tense

### Formation

- Subject + **had** + past participle
- "She *had walked* to school before the bus arrived."

### When to Use Past Perfect

The past perfect exists for one purpose: **to indicate that one past action occurred before another past action.** Without the past perfect, the sequence of events may be unclear.

**The rule: When two past actions are described and their chronological order is not signaled by words like "before" or "after," you must use the past perfect for the earlier action and the simple past for the later action.**

**Example:**

- "When I arrived at the party, John *had already left*." (John left FIRST, then I arrived. "Had left" = left before my arrival.)
- Compare: "When I arrived at the party, John *left*." (This means John left AFTER I arrived, or at the same time — a different meaning.)

**More examples showing the distinction:**

- "By the time the police arrived, the thief *had escaped*." (Escape happened before arrival.)
- "She *recognized* him immediately. She *had met* him years before." (The meeting happened before the recognition.)
- "The patient *had died* before the ambulance *reached* the hospital." (Dying preceded reaching.)

### When the Past Perfect Is NOT Needed

If the word **"before"** or **"after"** already establishes chronological order, simple past can be used for both clauses:

- "Before she *went* to college, she *traveled* through Europe." (Both simple past — "before" already shows order.)
- "After he *finished* dinner, he *watched* television." (Both simple past — "after" already establishes order.)

However, past perfect is STILL grammatically correct with "before" and "after"; it's simply not required because those words handle the sequencing. The ACT can test both versions, and both would be grammatically acceptable. The PAST PERFECT IS REQUIRED when NO sequencing word is present.

### Past Perfect as the "Most Past" Tense

Think of tenses as a timeline in a passage written in past tense:

```
Future ←——— NOW ———→ Simple Past ———→ Past Most (Past Perfect)
                         (earlier)           (even earlier)
```

When a passage is told in the past, the simple past is the baseline for actions going forward in the narrative. Anything that happened BEFORE the narrative's "present" moment (which is actually the past) requires the past perfect.

---

## 5.7 Future Perfect Tense

### Formation

- Subject + **will have** + past participle
- "By next June, she *will have graduated*."

### When to Use Future Perfect

For actions that will be completed before a specific future point:

- "By the time you *arrive*, I *will have finished* dinner." (Finishing dinner precedes your arrival.)
- "He *will have completed* his degree by 2026." (Completion before the future marker.)

**Key signal:** "by the time" or "by" + future time reference.

This tense is tested less frequently than present perfect and past perfect, but it does appear.

---

## 5.8 Progressive (Continuous) Tenses

### Formation

- Present Progressive: **am/is/are** + present participle (-ing form)
- Past Progressive: **was/were** + present participle
- Future Progressive: **will be** + present participle
- Present Perfect Progressive: **has/have been** + present participle
- Past Perfect Progressive: **had been** + present participle

### When Progressive Tenses Are Correct

The progressive aspect communicates that an action is **ongoing** at a particular moment in time. In contrast, the simple aspect communicates that an action is **completed, habitual, or general**.

### Key Contrasts Tested on the ACT

**1. Progressive vs. Simple for interrupted actions:**

- "She *was walking* (past progressive) to school when it *started* (simple past) to rain."
- The ongoing action (walking) is interrupted by a sudden event (rain).

**2. Parallel progressive actions:**

- "She *was reading* (past progressive) while he *was cooking* (past progressive)." (Two simultaneous ongoing actions.)

**3. Simple past for completed actions vs. progressive for ongoing:**

- "I *read* the entire book last night." (I finished it — completed action.)
- "I *was reading* a book when she called." (I was in the middle of it — ongoing action that was interrupted.)

**4. Present Perfect Progressive vs. Present Perfect Simple:**

- "I *have been studying* for three hours." (Emphasis on the ongoing duration — I am still studying.)
- "I *have studied* three chapters." (Emphasis on the completed result — three chapters are done.)

The progressive adds the dimension of **duration and ongoingness**. When the ACT tests whether to use simple or progressive, ask yourself: Is the focus on the action being ongoing at a specific time, or on the action being complete?

### Present Perfect Progressive

Formation: **has/have been** + present participle

- "She *has been studying* for three hours." (Began three hours ago, still going.)
- "They *have been waiting* since noon." (Started at noon, still waiting.)

This tense bridges past and present, emphasizing the continuity of the action. The signals "for" and "since" work with this tense just as they do with the present perfect.

---

## 5.9 Sequence of Tenses Across Sentences

The ACT frequently tests whether tenses are logically consistent across sentences within a paragraph. The rule:

**When all events in a passage occur in the same time frame, maintain the same tense.**

### Example of a Tense Error:

**❌ "The expedition traveled deep into the jungle. The heat was oppressive, and the humidity makes breathing difficult."**

The shift from past ("traveled," "was") to present ("makes") is unjustified. All these past:

**✅ "The expedition traveled deep into the jungle. The heat was oppressive, and the breathing was difficult."**

OR, if the passage was written in present tense:

**✅ "The expedition travels deep into the jungle. The heat is oppressive, and breathing is difficult."**

### Example Where Tense Shift IS Justified:

**"Shakespeare *wrote* over 300 years ago, but his plays *remain* universally relevant."**

- "Wrote" = past (historical fact: the writing happened long ago)
- "Remain" = present (the relevance exists NOW)

This shift is CORRECT because the two clauses describe events in genuinely different time frames.

---

## 5.10 Special Tense Phenomena

### Subjunctive Mood

The ACT occasionally tests the **subjunctive mood**, which expresses wishes, demands, suggestions, or hypothetical/contrary-to-fact conditions. The subjunctive uses **"were"** (not "was") for all subjects, and **"be"** for the base form in that-clauses following certain verbs.

**1. Contrary-to-fact hypotheticals (If I were...):**

- ❌ "If I *was* you, I would study harder."
- ✅ "If I *were* you, I**2. Wish about the present/future:**

- "I wish I *were* taller." (Meaning: I am not taller — contrary to fact.)

**3. Demands, suggestions, and requirements (that-clauses):**

- "The teacher insisted that he *arrive* on time." (Not "arrives" — subjunctive)
- "The committee recommended that she *be* appointed." (Not "is" — subjunctive)
- "It is essential that every student *submit* the form." (Not "submits" — subjunctive)

**Critical verb list for the mandative subjunctive (that-clause verbs):**

- demand, insist, suggest, recommend, require, ask, order, propose, prefer, desire, urge, advise, mandate

**The test:** Look for "that" after one of these verbs and confirm that the **base form of the verb** is used, not the conjugated present form.

### "Used to" vs. "Be Used to" vs. "Get Used to"

These are three completely different constructions that the ACT has tested:

- **"Used to" + base form** = past habitual action that no longer occurs
  - "I *used to play* piano." (I played as a habit in the past, but no longer.)

- **"Be used to" + noun/gerund** = be accustomed to something (in the present)
  - "I *am used to waking* up early." (It is normal for me now.)

- **"Get used to" + noun/gerund** = become accustomed to (process of adjustment)
  - "I am *getting used to waking* up early." (I am in the process of adapting.)

### Causative Verbs (Have, Make, Let)

- **Have someone do something** (no "to"): "I *had* my assistant *send* the email."
- **Make someone do something** (no "to"): "The teacher *made* the student *apologize*."
- **Let someone do something** (no "to"): "She *let* her children *stay* up late."

Note the base verb (no "to") in all three constructions. The ACT sometimes inserts "to" incorrectly in these structures.

### Reported/Indirect Speech Tense Shifts

When converting direct speech to reported (indirect) speech, tenses typically "shift back" one step:

| Direct Speech | Indirect/Reported Speech |
|---|---|
| "I am tired." (present) | She said she **was** tired. (shifted to past) |
| "I will come." (future) | He said he **would come**. (shifted to conditional) |
| "I went home." (past) | She said she **had gone** home. (shifted to past perfect) |
| "I have finished." (present perfect) | He said he **had finished**. ( perfect) |

The ACT may test this in passages that include dialogue or reported statements.

---

## 5.11 Tense Consistency: The Mastery Decision Tree

When you encounter a tense question on the ACT, follow this decision tree (mental, not to be written during the test — but practice until it becomes automatic):

**Step 1: What is the established tense of the surrounding sentences?**

Look at the verbs in the sentence before and after the question sentence. This establishes the baseline time frame.

**Step 2: Does the action in the question sentence occur in the same time frame?**

- **YES** → Keep the same tense.
- **NO** → Identify the actual time frame and select the tense that communicates it.

**Step 3: Is there a specific time marker (signal word)?**

- If you see "yesterday," "last year," "in 2010" → use **simple past** (never present perfect)
- If you see "since," "for," "already," "recently," "yet" → consider **present perfect**
- If you see "by the time" or "by" + future date → consider **future perfect**
- If you see "before" or "after" → simple past is sufficient for both clauses, though past perfect is still acceptable for the earlier action

**Step 4: Is the sentence expressing a contrary-to-fact condition or demand?**

- "If I were..." → subjunctive "were"
- "Insist/suggest/demand that [someone] [base verb]" → subjunctive base form

**Step 5: Is a verb stative or dynamic?**

- If stative (seem, appear, know, believe, possess) in a passage where the progressive seems tempting → use **simple**, not progressive
- If dynamic or expressing active consideration → progressive is acceptable

---

# PART TWO: SUBJECT-VERB AGREEMENT — THE COMPREHENSIVE SYSTEM

---

## 5.12 The Fundamental Law

The ACT tests subject-verb agreement (SVA) more frequently than any other single grammar concept. The fundamental rule is simple in theory:

> **A singular subject takes a singular verb. A plural subject takes a plural verb.**

The difficulty lies not in the rule itself but in the **identifying of the subject** — particularly when the ACT inserts misleading plural nouns, relative clauses, prepositional phrases, and other structural barriers between the subject and its verb.

### Singular vs. Plural Verb Forms

In the present tense, the distinction is clearest:

| Singular Verbs (3rd Person) | Plural Verbs (All Other Persons) |
|---|---|
| He/She/It **walks** | I/You/We/They **walk** |
| He/She/It **has** | I/You/We/They **have** |
| He/She/It **is** | I/You/We/They **are** |
| He/She/It **was** | I/You/We/They **were** |
| He/She/It **goes** | I/You/We/They **go** |
| He/She/It **runs** | I/We/They **run** |

Note: In the past tense, the only verb that shows person-based distinction is "was/were":

- I/He/She/It **was**
- You/We/They **were**

For all other verbs, the past tense is the same for all subjects (walked, ran, ate, etc.), which is why SVA questions primarily focus on present tense.

---

## 5.13 The Core Process: Finding the True Subject

This is the most important skill in the entire chapter. The ACT is designed so that the sentence's true subject is often separated from the verb by one or more distracting phrases. Your job is to **strip those phrases away** and identify the real subject.

### The Elimination Protocol

**Step 1: Locate the verb that needs to agree with its subject.**

**Step 2: Determine whether the subject comes before or after the verb.**

- In standard sentences: subject precedes the verb.
- In "there is/are" constructions: subject follows the verb.
- In questions: subject often follows the verb.

**Step 3: Cross out (mentally or by identifying) all intervening phrases that CANNOT contain the subject:**

These are the four categories of **dist eliminate:

**Category 1: Prepositional Phrases**

A prepositional phrase begins with a preposition and ends with a noun/pronoun (the object of the preposition). The object of the preposition is **NEVER** the subject.

Key prepositions to recognize:

- of, in, on, at, to, for, with, by, from, between, among, through, across, around, about, under, over, into, onto, upon, against, during, before, after, without, within, throughout, behind, beneath, beside, along, near, toward, above, below

**ACT favorite trap: "of the [plural noun]"**

- "The effectiveness of the new methods **is** clear." (Subject: "effectiveness" — singular — not "methods")
- "The results of the experiment **were** surprising." (Subject: "results" — plural)
- "The number of students **is** growing." (Subject: "number" — singular)
- "A number of students **are** protesting." (Subject: "students" — plural — "a number of" functions as a quantifier)

**Category 2: Relative Clauses**

Relative clauses begin with relative pronouns (who, whom, which, that, whose, where, when) and modify a noun. The relative pronoun may look like a subject, but it is **NOT** the subject of the main clause.

Identification: If you see "who," "which," or "that" immediately before a verb, that verb is inside the relative clause and relates to the relative pronoun, not to the main clause's subject.

**Example:**

- "The student [who *studies* hard] *succeeds*."
  - "studies" agrees with "who" = "student" (singular) → "studies" is singular (within the relative clause)
  - "succeeds" agrees with "The student" (subject of the main clause) → "succeeds" is singular (main clause)

- "The students [who *study* hard] *succeed*."
  - "study" agrees with "who" = "students" (plural) → "study" is plural
  - "succeed" agrees with "The students" (plural) → "succeed" is plural

- "The box of chocolates [which *was* on the table] *has - "was" refers to "box" (singular) — not "chocolates" — within the relative clause
  - "has" refers to "The box" (subject of the main clause)

**Category 3: Appositives (Middle Comma Clauses)**

An appositive is a noun or noun phrase set off by commas that renames another noun. Since a noun renaming another noun cannot be the subject (there is only one grammatical subject), you eliminate it.

- "The manager, **as well as** the employees, **is** attending."
  - "the employees" is in a structure ("as well as the employees") that is essentially an appositive-like addition — NOT part of the subject
  - Subject: "The manager" (singular) → "is"

- "My brother, **along with** his friends, **enjoys** hiking."
  - Subject: "My brother" (singular) → "enjoys"
  - "his friends" is within a phrase set off by an "along with" construction — not the subject

**Category 4: Phrases Starting with "Together With," "In Addition To," "As Well As," "Along With," "Including," "Accompanied by"**

These are NOT coordinating conjunctions. They do not create compound subjects. They create **interrupting phrases** that do not affect the subject's number.

**MEMORIZE THIS DISTINCTION:**

| Creates Compound Subject (plural) | Does NOT Create Compound Subject |
|---|---|
| and | as well as |
| | along with |
| | together with |
| | in addition to |
| | including |
| | accompanied by |
| | rather than |
| | no less than |
| | in the company of |

**Test:**

- "The teacher AND the students **are** here." (Compound: plural)
- "The teacher, as well as the students, **is** here." (Simple subject "teacher": singular)

---

## 5.14 Individual Rules for Subject-Verb Agreement

### Rule 1: "Each" and "Every" Are Always Singular

No matter what follows, the subjects "each" and "every" trigger singular verbs.

- "Each of the students **has** a textbook."
- "Every dog **needs** attention."
- "Each book on the shelf **belongs** to the library."
- "Every participant and observer **was** invited." (Even "every participant and observer" is treated as singular — "every" distributes individually)

### Rule 2: "None" — Contextual Usage

Traditionally, "none" is singular (meaning "not one"), but in modern usage, "none" can be plural when referring to a plural noun. The ACT generally treats "none" as singular for testing purposes, but this is one rule where the test is less rigid. Context and meaning typically guide the choice.

### Rule 3: "Either/Or" and "Neither/Nor" — Proximity Rule

When subjects are joined by "either...or" or "neither...nor," the verb agrees with the subject **closest to it** (the proximity rule):

- "Neither the students nor the teacher **was** present." (Teacher is closest → singular "was")
- "Neither the teacher nor the students **were** present." (Students is closest → plural "were")
- "Either the manager or the employees **are** responsible." (Employees → plural)
- "Either the employees or the manager **is** responsible." (Manager → singular)

**ACT test:** Rearrange the sentence in your mind so the closest subject is next to the verb, then check if the verb agrees.

### Rule 4: Collective Nouns — Usually Singular

Collective nouns refer to a group acting as a single unit. In American English (which the ACT follows), collective nouns are typically **singular**.

Common collective nouns:

- team, committee, audience, family, class, group, jury, crowd, staff, majority, band, orchestra, government, herd, flock, fleet

- "The team **is** winning." (Acting as one unit)
- "The committee **has** made its decision."
- "The audience **was** enthusiastic."

**Exception:** When the group members are acting individually (emphasizing internal disagreement), some writers use plural verbs, but the ACT generally tests collective nouns as singular. If you see a collective noun as the subject, start by assuming singular.

### Rule 5: Indefinite Pronouns

**Always Singular:**

- each, either, neither
- anyone, anybody, anything
- everyone, everybody, everything
- no one, nobody, nothing
- someone, somebody, something
- one, none (in formal/ACT usage)

- "Everyone **has** a role to play."
- "Nobody **knows** the answer."
- "One of the students **was** absent."
- "Each of the options **is** valid."

**Always Plural:**

- both, few, several, many

- "Both of the answers **are** correct."
were** selected."

**Contextual (Singular or Plural — depends on the "of" phrase meaning):**

- some, any, none, all, most

- "Some of the cake **is** eaten." (Cake = uncountable → singular)
- "Some of the cookies **are** eaten." (Cookies = countable plural → plural)
- "All of the water **is** contaminated." (Water = uncountable → singular)
- "All of the students **are** present." (Students = plural → plural)

For "all," "some," "any," "most," "none" + "of the [noun]," determine the number based on the noun after "of." But prefer the meaning: if the noun is uncountable or singular, use singular verb.

### Rule 6: "There Is" vs. "There Are"

When a sentence begins with "There," the subject follows the verb. Determine whether the subject is singular or plural:

- "There **is** table." (Book → singular)
- "There **are** books on the table." (Books → plural)
- "There **is** a dog and two cats in the yard." (The first item after the verb determines the verb — traditional/ACT approach)

### Rule 7: Sentences Beginning with "Here"

Same logic as "there":

- "Here **is** your book." (Book → singular)
- "Here **are** your books." (Books → plural)

### Rule 8: Titles, Quotations, and Words Referred to as Words — Always Singular

- "'Pride and Prejudice' **is** a classic novel."
- "Dollars **is** a misspelling of dollars." (The word itself is singular)

### Rule 9: Distance, Time, Amount, Weight, Volume as Unified Quantities — Singular

- "Five miles **is** a long distance to walk." (Five miles is one unified distance)
- "Ten dollars **is** too much for a coffee." (Unified amount)
- "Two weeks **is** a standard vacation period." (Unified time period)
- "Five pounds of flour **was** enough."

### Rule 10: "The Number Of" vs. "A Number Of"

**"The number of"** = singular (the quantity itself is one number):

- "The number of applicants **has** increased."

**"A number of"** = plural (meaning "several" or "many"):

- "A number of applicants **have** withdrawn."

### Rule 11: Mathematical Operations

- Addition: "Two and two **is** four." (Commonly singular — the result is one number)
- Multiplication: "Two times three **is** six."
- Subtraction: "Ten minus six **is** four."
- Division: "Six divided by two **is** three."

### Rule 12: Gerunds and Infinitives as Subjects — Singular

- "**Swimming** in the ocean **is** refreshing." (Swimming = singular subject)
- "**To err** is human." (To err = singular subject)
- "**Reading books** **improves** vocabulary." (Reading books = singular activity)

### Rule 13: Subordinate or Relative Clauses as Subjects — Singular

- "**What she said** **was** surprising." (The clause functions as one idea → singular)
- "**That he arrived late** **is** unfortunate." (Singular)

### Rule 14: "-Body" and "-One" Pronouns — Singular but Third Person

- "Everybody **has** permission." (Note the -s ending despite "everybody" seeming like a group)

---

## 5.15 Advanced SVA Traps and Patterns

### Trap 1: The Long Intervening Phrase

The ACT places long, complex, plural-filled phrases between the singular subject and the verb:

**Example:** "The analysis of the complex interactions between various economic factors **suggests** that inflation will decrease."

- Subject: "analysis" (singular)
- Intervening phrases: "of the complex interactions between various economic factors" (all eliminated)
- Verb: "suggests" (singular) ✓

**How to handle:** When you see a question testing SVA, immediately eliminate everything between the subject and verb that is inside prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and modifier clauses. What remains is just the subject and verb — check agreement.

### Trap 2: Inverted Word Order

The ACT sometimes inverts the normal subject-verb order:

- "Among the many artifacts **was** a golden mask." (Subject: "a golden mask" → singular → "was")
- "Beyond the mountains **lies** a hidden valley." (Subject: "a hidden valley" → singular → "lies")
- "Not only the students but also the professor **was** surprised." (Proximity rule: "professor" is closest → singular)

**How to handle:** Mentally rearrange the sentence into standard subject-verb order:

- "A golden mask **was** among the many artifacts."
- "A hidden valley **lies** beyond the mountains."

### Trap 3: "One of the [Plural] Who/That..."

This is one of the ACT's most frequently tested SVA patterns:

- "She is one of the students who **study** hard."
  - "Who" refers to "students" (plural) → "study" (plural)
  - Meaning: Among the students who study hard, she is one.

- "She is the only one of the students who **studies** hard."
  - "Who" refers to "the only one" (singular) → "studies" (singular)
  - Meaning: She is the only student who studies hard.

**The distinction:**

- "one of the [plural] + who/that" → the relative clause modifies the plural noun → plural verb
- "the only one of the [plural] + who/that" → the relative clause modifies "one" → singular verb

### Trap 4: "Every" and "Each" Before Compound Subjects

- "Every man and woman **is** entitled to vote." (Singular — "every" distributes individually)
- "Each book and magazine **has** been catalogued." (Singular)

### Trap 5: "More Than One" — Singular

- "More than one student **has** complained." (Despite the implication of plurality, this is grammatically singular.)

### Trap 6: "Many a" — Singular

- "Many a student **has** struggled with this concept." (Singular — "many a" is an idiomatic expression that takes a singular noun and verb.)

### Trap 7: Nouns That Look Plural but Are Singular

Some English nouns end in "-s" but are grammatically singular:

- **News:** "The news **is** at six o'clock."
- **Mathematics/Physics/Economics:** "Mathematics **is** required."
- **Measles/Mumps:** "Measles **is** preventable."
- **Athletics/Gymnastics:** "Athletics **promotes** fitness."
- **Politics:** "Politics **is** a dirty business."
- **Series:** "A series of lectures **is** planned."
- **Means:** "Every available means **has** been tried."

### Trap 8: Nouns That Look Singular but Are Plural

- **People:** "The people **are** happy." (Always plural)
- **Police:** "The police **are** investigating." (Always plural)
- **Cattle:** "The cattle **are** grazing." (Always plural)
- **Children, women, men, mice, geese, teeth, feet:** All irregular plurals — always take plural verbs.

### Trap 9: "What" as Subject — Usually Singular

- "What he said **was** surprising." (Singular — one thing)
- "What motivate them **are** ambition and curiosity." (When "what" refers to a plural idea, plural verb is possible, but ACT typically treats "what" as singular.)

### Trap 10: "Such" and "So"

- "Such **was** the impact of the discovery that..." (Singular)
- "Such **were** the challenges they faced..." (Plural — when "such" refers to a plural noun)

---

## 5.16 The SVA Mastery Checklist

When you encounter any SVA question, run through this mental checklist:

1. **Find the verb** that needs to agree.
2. **Find the true subject** by eliminating:
   - Prepositional phrases (of, in, on, to, for, with, by, from, between, among, etc.)
   - Relative clauses (who, which, that, whose)
   - Appositives and middle comma clauses
   - Phrases starting with "as well as," "along with," "together with," "in addition to," "including," "accompanied by"
3. **Determine if the subject is singular or plural** by applying the rules above.
4. **Match the verb to the subject's number.**
5. **Check for special cases:** "each/every," "either/or," "neither/nor," "there is/are," "one of the...who," "the number of" vs. "a number of," collective nouns, indefinite pronouns.

---

# PART THREE: PRONOUN USAGE — THE COMPREHENSIVE SYSTEM

---

## 5.17 The Pronoun Systems

Pronouns are words that take the place of nouns (their **antecedents**). The ACT tests pronouns in three primary ways:

1. **Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement** — Does the pronoun match its antecedent in number (singular/plural)?
2. **Pronoun Case** — Is the pronoun in the correct case (subject, object, possessive)?
3. **Pronoun Clarity** — Is the pronoun's reference unambiguous?

---

## 5.18 Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement

### The Fundamental Rule

A pronoun must agree in **number** (singular or plural) with its antecedent (the noun it replaces).

- "The student forgot **his** book." (Student = singular → "his" = singular)
- "The students forgot **their** books." (Students = plural → "their" = plural)

### The "They" as Singular Pronoun Issue

In contemporary English, the singular "they" has become widely accepted as a gender-neutral pronoun when referring to a person of unspecified gender. However, **the ACT maintains the traditional rule: "they," "them," "their," "theirs" are PLURAL pronouns and must refer to plural antecedents.**

**ACT approach:**

- ❌ bring **their** textbook." (Student = singular, their = plural → disagreement)
- ✅ "Every student should bring **his or her** textbook." (Singular antecedent → singular pronouns)
- ✅ "All students should bring **their** textbooks." (Students = plural, their = plural → agreement)

**Note:** The ACT does not test the singular "they" as a correct answer. If you see "they" referring to a singular antecedent, it is an error on this test, even though it is increasingly accepted in modern usage.

### Indefinite Pronoun Antecedents

Many indefinite pronouns are singular and require singular pronouns:

| Singular Indefinite Pronouns | Required Pronoun |
|---|---|
| everyone, everybody | his or her |
| anyone, anybody | his or her |
| someone, somebody | his or her |
| no one, nobody | his or her |
| each | his or her |
| either | his or her |
| neither | his or her |
| one | his (or its, depending on context) |

**ACT test:**

- ❌ "Everyone should bring **their** own lunch."
- ✅ "Everyone should bring **his or her** own lunch."
- ✅ "All students should bring **their** own lunches." (Rephrase to make antecedent plural)

**The "his or her" solution:** The ACT accepts "his or her" as the correct singular pronoun pair. It also accepts "he or she," "him or her," and "his or hers."

**Alternative solution:** Rewrite the sentence to make the antecedent plural, allowing "their":

- "All participants must submit **their** forms." (Participants = plural → "their" = correct)

### Compound Antecedents

When two antecedents are joined by "and," the pronoun is plural:

- "John and Mary went to **their** homes." (John + Mary = plural → "their")

When two antecedents are joined by "or" or "nor," the pronoun agrees with the **nearest** antecedent (similar to the proximity rule for SVA):

- "Neither the manager nor the employees submitted **their** reports." (Employees = plural → "their")
- "Neither the employees nor the manager submitted **his** report." (Manager = singular → "his")

### Collective Noun Antecedents

When a collective noun (team, committee, family, group) is the antecedent, the pronoun is typically **singular** (it) in American English:

- "The team won **its** first game." (Team acting as a unit → "its")
- "The committee released **its** report." (Committee as one entity → "its")

### Ambiguous Antecedent Reference

The ACT tests whether a pronoun clearly refers to one specific antecedent. If a sentence contains two or more nouns that could logically be the antecedent, the pronoun is **ambiguous** and the sentence must be revised.

**Example of ambiguous pronoun:**

- ❌ "John told Mark that **he** was fired." (Who was fired? John or Mark? "He" is ambiguous.)
- ✅ "John told Mark, '**You** are fired.'" (Direct quote removes ambiguity.)
- ✅ "John said that **Mark** was fired." (Naming the person removes ambiguity.)
- ✅ "John was fired, and he told **Mark** the news." (Restructuring removes ambiguity.)

**ACT test for ambiguous pronouns:** Look for sentences where a pronoun (he, she, it, they, him, her, them, his, her, its, their) could refer to more than one noun in the sentence or in the preceding sentence. The correct answer will either:

- Replace the pronoun with the specific noun
- Restructure the sentence to eliminate ambiguity
- Use a direct quote

---

## 5.19 Pronoun Case

English pronouns change form depending on their grammatical function in the sentence. These forms are called **cases**.

### The Three Cases

| Subject Pronouns | Object Pronouns | Possessive Pronouns |
|---|---|---|
| I | me | my / mine |
| you | you | your / yours |
| he | him | his |
| she | her | her / hers |
| it | it | its |
| we | us | our / ours |
| they | them | their / theirs |
| who | whom | whose |

### Subject Pronouns

Use subject pronouns when the pronoun is:

1. **The subject of a verb:** "**She** runs every morning."
2. **A predicate nominative (after a linking verb):** "It is **I**." / "The winner was **he**." (Formal — the ACT tests this in formal written English.)

### Object Pronouns

Use object pronouns when the pronoun is:

1. **The direct object:** "The teacher praised **him**."
2. **The indirect object:** "She gave **them** the books."
3. **The object of a preposition:** "Between **you** and **me**, this is difficult." / "For **whom** the bell tolls."

### The "Who" vs. "Whom" Decision

This is one of the most commonly tested pronoun case distinctions on the ACT.

**The test:**

1. Isolate the clause containing "who" or "whom."
2. Replace "who" with "he/she/they" and "whom" with "him/her/them."
3. If "he/she/they" sounds correct in the clause, use **who** (subject case).
4. If "him/her/them" sounds correct, use **whom** (object case).

**Examples:**

- "___ is coming to dinner?" → "**He** is coming to dinner." → **Who** is coming to dinner?
- "___ did you invite?" → "You invited **him**." → **Whom** did you invite?
- "The scientist ___ discovered the cure" → "**He** discovered the cure" → The scientist **who** discovered the cure
- "The scientist ___ we interviewed" → "We interviewed **him**" → The scientist **whom** we interviewed

**Critical ACT trap: "Who" vs. "Whom" after prepositions**

After a preposition, "whom" is always correct (in formal written English):

- "To **whom** did you send the letter?"
- "With **whom**- "The person about **whom** I spoke..." (Not "about who")

**However:** If the preposition is separated from "whom" and moved to the end of the clause, "who" can sometimes be acceptable in informal English, but the ACT prefers "whom" in these positions when the pronoun is functioning as an object:

- "Who are you talking to?" (Informal — ACT may flag this)
- "Whom are you talking to?" (Formal — ACT prefers this)
- "To whom are you talking?" (Most formal — always correct)

### Pronouns in Comparisons

The ACT tests pronoun case in comparative constructions (sentences using "than" or "as"):

The key is to **complete the comparison mentally** by adding the implied words:

- "She works harder than **I**." (Complete: "She works harder than **I** work." → "I" is the subject of the implied verb → subject pronoun)
- "She works harder than **me**." (Complete: "She works harder than [she works harder than] **me**." → "me" is the object → object pronoun)

**Which is correct?**

In formal English (which the ACT follows):

- "She works harder than **I**." → CORRECT (subject pronoun, with implied "do" or the verb understood)
- "She works harder than **me**." → INCORRECT in formal English (though common in speech)

**More examples:**

- "He is taller than **I**." (than I am)
- "She likes pizza more than **I**." (than I like pizza)
- "She likes pizza more than **me**." (than she likes me — different meaning!)

**The ACT loves this ambiguity trap.** If the sentence could be read either way, the pronoun case determines the meaning. The correct answer is the one where the pronoun case matches the intended meaning.

- "The coach praised the team more than **I**." (The coach praised the team more than I praised the team.)
- "The coach praised the team more than **me**." (The coach praised the team more than the coach praised me.)

### Pronouns in Compound Structures

When a pronoun is part of a compound (joined by "and" or "or"), the case test is to **remove the other person and see which pronoun sounds correct alone:**

- "Between you and **me**, this is easy." (Remove "you and": "Between **me**" → object pronoun ✓)
- ❌ "Between you and **I**, this is easy." (Remove "you and": "Between **I**" → incorrect)

- "She and **I** went to the store." (Remove "She and": "**I** went" → subject pronoun ✓)
- ❌ "Her and **I** went to the store." (Remove "Her and": "**I** went" → "Her" is wrong)

- "The gift was for Maria and **me**." (Remove "Maria and": "for **me**" → object pronoun ✓)
- ❌ "The gift was for Maria and **I**." (Remove "Maria and": "for **I**" → incorrect)

- "He and **she** are siblings." (Remove "He and": "**she** are" → subject pronoun ✓)
- ❌ "Him and **her** are siblings." (Remove "Him and": "**her** are" → incorrect)

**ACT trap:** The most common error is using "myself" instead of "I" or "me" in compound structures:

- ❌ "My brother and **myself** went to the store."
- ✅ "My brother and **I** went to the store."

"Myself" is a reflexive pronoun and should only be used when the subject and object are the same person:

- "I hurt **myself**." (Subject = I, Object = myself → same person → reflexive is correct)
- "I made dinner for **myself**." (Reflexive — correct)

### Possessive Pronouns with Gerunds

When a pronoun precedes a gerund (an -ing verb form functioning as a noun), the pronoun must be in the **possessive** case:

- ✅ "I appreciate **your** helping me." (The helping is the object of "appreciate" — "your" modifies the gerund "helping")
- ❌ "I appreciate **you** helping me." (Common in speech, but incorrect in formal English on the ACT)

- ✅ "**His** arriving late caused problems."
- ❌ "**Him** arriving late caused problems."

- ✅ "The teacher objected to **my** speaking during the lecture."
- ❌ "The teacher objected to **me** speaking during the lecture."

**This is a high-frequency ACT test point.** If you see a pronoun immediately before an -ing word that functions as a noun (gerund), the possessive case is required.

---

## 5.20 Reflexive and Intensive Pronouns

### Reflexive Pronoun Forms

| Base | Reflexive |
|---|---|
| I | myself |
| you | yourself |
| he | himself |
| she | herself |
| it | itself |
| we | ourselves |
| they | themselves |

### When Reflexive Pronouns Are Correct

1. **When the subject and object are the same entity:**
   - "She blamed **herself** for the mistake."
   - "They prepared **themselves** for the exam."

2. **For emphasis (intensive use):**
   - "The president **himself** attended the ceremony."
   - "I **myself** witnessed the event."

### When Reflexive Pronouns Are INCORRECT

Reflexive pronouns should NOT be used as substitutes for subject or object pronouns:

- ❌ "John and **myself** attended the meeting."
- ✅ "John and **I** attended the meeting."

- ❌ "The award was given to **myself**."
- ✅ "The award was given to **me**."

- ❌ "**Hisself** is not a word." (Non-standard — always incorrect)
- ✅ "He did it **himself**."

- ❌ "**Theirselves** is not a word." (Non-standard — always incorrect)
- ✅ "They did it **themselves**."

**ACT test:** If you see "myself," "himself," "herself," "itself," "ourselves," "themselves," or "yourself" in an answer choice, verify that the subject and object are the same entity. If they are not, the reflexive pronoun is incorrect.

---

## 5.21 Relative Pronouns

Relative pronouns introduce relative clauses (dependent clauses that modify nouns). The ACT tests the correct choice of relative pronoun.

### The Relative Pronoun Selection Guide

| Pronoun | Used For | Function |
|---|---|---|
| **who** | people (subject in the relative clause) | "The man **who** called you is here." |
| **whom** | people (object in the relative clause) | "The man **whom** you called is here." |
| **whose** | possession (people, animals, things) | "The man **whose** car was stolen..." |
| **which** | animals and things (nonrestrictive clauses) | "The book, **which** is on the table, is mine." |
| **that** | animals and things (restrictive clauses) | "The book **that** is on the table is mine." |
| **where** | places | "The city **where** I was born..." |
| **when** | times | "The year **when** she graduated..." |

### "Who/Whom/Whose" vs. "Which/That"

- Use **who/whom/whose** for people.
- Use **which/that** for animals and things.
- ❌ "The woman **which** lived next door..." → ✅ "The woman **who** lived next door..."

### "That" vs. "Which"

This distinction is tested on the ACT:

**"That"** introduces a **restrictive** (essential) clause — information necessary to identify the noun. No commas.

- "The book **that** is on the table is mine." (Which book? The one on the table — essential information → "that" + no commas)

**"Which"** introduces a **nonrestrictive** (nonessential) clause — additional information that could be removed. Requires commas.

- "The book is on the table, is mine." (The book is already identified — the location is extra information → "which" + commas)

**The test:** If the clause is set off by commas, use "which." If there are no commas, use "that."

**Exception:** "Which" can be used without commas in British English for restrictive clauses, but the ACT follows the American convention: "that" for restrictive, "which" for nonrestrictive.

### "Where" and "When" as Relative Pronouns

Use "where" for places and "when" for times, but only when the antecedent is a place or time noun:

- "This is the school **where** I studied." (School = place → "where" ✓)
- "I remember the day **when** we met." (Day = time → "when" ✓)

**Do not use "where" or "when" as substitutes for "in which" or "on which" in formal writing:**

- ❌ "The situation **where** we found ourselves..." (Situation is not a place)
- ✅ "The situation **in which** we found ourselves..."

### Omitting Relative Pronouns

In restrictive clauses, the relative pronoun can sometimes be omitted when it functions as the **object** of the clause:

- "The book **(that)** I read was fascinating." (Correct — "that" is the object of "read" → can be omitted)
- "The man **(whom)** I called didn't answer." (Correct — "whom" is the object of "called" → can be omitted)

**Cannot be omitted when it functions as the subject:**

- "The man **who** called me is here." (Cannot omit "who" — it is the subject of "called")
- "The book **that** is on the table is mine." (Cannot omit "that" — it is the subject of "is")

---

## 5.22 Demonstrative Pronouns and Determiners

The words **this, that, these, those** can function as pronouns (standing alone) or as determiners (modifying a noun).

### As Determiners (Modifying Nouns)

- **This/these** = singular/plural, near in space or time
- **That/those** = singular/plural, farther in space or time

**Agreement rule:** "This" and "that" modify singular nouns; "these" and "those" modify plural nouns.

- ❌ "These kind of problems are common." → ✅ "These **kinds** of problems are common." OR "This **kind** of problem is common."
- ❌ "Those type of books..." → ✅ "Those **types** of books..." OR "That **type** of book..."

**ACT test:** "These/those + kind/type/sort" is a common error. The demonstrative must agree with "kind/type/sort," not with the noun after "of."

### As Pronouns (Standing Alone)

- "**This** is my book." (Singular)
- "**Those** are my books." (Plural)

**Vague use of "this":** The ACT tests whether "this" has a clear referent. If "this" could refer to multiple ideas in the preceding sentence or passage, it is vague and must be replaced with "this + specific noun" or a more precise reference.

- ❌ "The company reduced costs and improved quality. **This** led to increased profits." (What led to profits? Reducing costs? Improving quality? Both? "This" is vague.)
- ✅ "The company reduced costs and improved quality. **These changes** led to increased profits." (Specific reference — clear)

---

## 5.23 Pronoun Consistency

Once a passage establishes a pronoun reference, it should remain consistent:

- ❌ "When **one** studies hard, **you** do well." (Shift from "one" to "you")
- ✅ "When **one** studies hard, **one** does well."
- ✅ "When **you** study hard, **you** do well."
- ✅ "When **students** study hard, **they** do well."

**The ACT tests pronoun shifts within sentences and paragraphs.** If a sentence begins with "one," it should not shift to "you" or "people" mid-sentence. If a passage uses "a student" as the antecedent, the pronoun should be "he or she" (or "one"), not "they."

---

## 5.24 The Pronoun Mastery Checklist

When you encounter any pronoun question, run through this mental checklist:

1. **Identify the pronoun's antecedent** (the noun it replaces).
2. **Check number agreement:** Does the pronoun match the antecedent in number? (Singular antecedent → singular pronoun; plural antecedent → plural pronoun)
3. **Check case:** Is the pronoun in the correct grammatical role? (Subject → subject pronoun; object → object pronoun; possession → possessive pronoun)
4. **Check clarity:** Does the pronoun have ONE clear antecedent? Or could it refer to multiple nouns?
5. **Check consistency:** Does the pronoun maintain the same person and number as the rest of the passage?
6. **Check relative pronoun choice:** Is "who" used for people? "which" for things (with commas)? "that" for things (without commas)? "whose" for possession?
7. **Check possessive + gerund:** If a pronoun precedes a gerund, is it in the possessive case?
8. **Check "who" vs. "whom":** Apply the he/him test.

---

# PART FOUR: INTEGRATED GRAMMAR ANALYSIS — HOW THE SYSTEMS INTERSECT

---

## 5.25 Multi-Layer Grammar Questions

The most challenging ACT English questions test multiple grammar concepts simultaneously. Here is how the systems intersect:

### SVA + Tense Combined

A question might require you to identify the correct subject (SVA) AND the correct tense:

**Example:** "The results of the study [that was/were conducted in 2019] [suggests/suggested] that the treatment [is/was] effective."

Analysis:

- "The results" = subject (plural) → "were" (not "was") for the relative clause modifying "study" — wait, the relative clause modifies "study" (singular) → "was conducted" ✓
- Main verb: "results" (plural) → "suggest" (not "suggests")
- Tense: The study was in 2019, and the results are being discussed in present context → "suggest" (present) or "suggested" (past) depending on whether the passage is in present or past tense

### Pronoun + SVA Combined

**Example:** "Each of the students who [was/were] selected [has/have] received [their/his or her] award."

Analysis:

- "who" refers to "students" (plural) → "were selected" ✓
- "Each" (subject of main clause) = singular → "has" ✓
- "Each" = singular antecedent → "his or her" (not "their") ✓

### Pronoun + Tense Combined

**Example:** "The scientist who [discovers/discovered] the cure in 1990 [has/have] since [won/wins] numerous awards."

Analysis:

- "discovered" (past — specific past time "1990") ✓
- "The scientist" (singular) → "has" ✓
- "has won" (present perfect — action in the past with present relevance) ✓

---

## 5.26 The Grammar of Parallel Structure

While parallelism is covered in greater detail in the sentence structure section, it intersects with verb tenses and pronouns in important ways:

**Parallel verb forms in a list:**

- ❌ "She likes **swimming**, **running**, and **to bike**." (Two gerunds + one infinitive → not parallel)
- ✅ "She likes **swimming**, **running**, and **biking**." (All gerunds → parallel)
- ✅ "She likes **to swim**, **to run**, and **to bike**." (All infinitives → parallel)

**Parallel pronoun usage:**

- ❌ "The teacher helped **him** and **I** with the assignment." (Object position → both should be object pronouns)
- ✅ "The teacher helped **him and me** with the assignment."

**Parallel verb tenses in a sequence:**

- ❌ "He **opened** the door, **walks** inside, and **sat** down." (Past → present → past → inconsistent)
- ✅ "He **opened** the door, **walked** inside, and **sat** down." (All past → consistent)

---

## 5.27 Common ACT Grammar Patterns: A Summary Reference

| Pattern | Correct Form | Incorrect Form |
|---|---|---|
| Subject + prepositional phrase + verb | "The box of chocolates **is**..." | "The box of chocolates **are**..." |
| "One of the [plural] who" | "one of the students who **study**..." | "one of the students who **studies**..." |
| "The only one of the [plural] who" | "the only one of the students who **studies**..." | "the only one of the students who **study**..." |
| "Each/every" + noun + verb | "Each student **has**..." | "Each student **have**..." |
| "Neither/nor" (proximity) | "Neither the cat nor the dogs **were**..." | "Neither the cat nor the dogs **was**..." |
| "A number of" | "A number of people **are**..." | "A number of people **is**..." |
| "The number of" | "The number of people **is**..." | "The number of people **are**..." |
| "There is/are" + subject | "There **is** a book..." / "There **are** books..." | "There **are** a book..." |
| "It is I" (formal) | "It is **I**." | "It is **me**." (ACT may test this) |
| "Between you and me" | "Between you and **me**..." | "Between you and **I**..." |
| "Than I" | "She is taller than **I**." | "She is taller than **me**." (ACT prefers formal) |
| Possessive + gerund | "I appreciate **your** helping." | "I appreciate **you** helping." |
| "Who" vs. "Whom" | "**Whom** did you see?" | "**Who** did you see?" (when object) |
| "That" vs. "Which" | Restrictive: "The book **that**..." / Nonrestrictive: "The book, **which**..." | "The book **which** is on the table..." (without commas) |
| Singular "they" | ❌ on ACT: "Everyone... **their**" | ✅ "Everyone... **his or her**" |
| Present perfect + specific past time | ❌ "I have seen him yesterday" | ✅ "I saw him yesterday" |
| Past perfect without past reference | ❌ "She had finished the report" (no past reference) | ✅ "She had finished the report before the meeting began" |

---

## 5.28 The Grammar Decision Flowchart

When you encounter any grammar question (whether it tests tenses, SVA, or pronouns), use this universal approach:

**Step 1: Read the full sentence (and surrounding sentences for context).**

**Step 2: Identify what the question is testing.**

- If the answer choices vary in verb form → tense or SVA
- If the answer choices vary in pronoun form → pronoun case, agreement, or clarity
- If the answer choices vary in punctuation → punctuation rules

**Step 3: Apply the specific rules for that category.**

- For tense: Determine the time frame, check for signal words, ensure consistency
- For SVA: Find the true subject, eliminate distractors, match number
- For pronouns: Find the antecedent, check number agreement, check case, check clarity

**Step 4: Eliminate all choices that violate a rule.**

**Step 5: If two choices remain, look for the subtle error in the wrong one.**

**Step 6: Confirm your answer by reading the sentence with your chosen answer inserted.**

---

## 5.29 Final Notes on Grammar Mastery

The grammar rules in this chapter are not suggestions — they are the **absolute, non-negotiable rules** that the ACT enforces. The test does not care about evolving usage, regional dialects, or informal conventions. It cares about **Standard Written English** as defined by formal grammar.

This means:

- "Between you and I" is ALWAYS wrong on the ACT, even though many educated people say it.
- "Everyone... their" is ALWAYS wrong on the ACT, even though it is increasingly accepted in published writing.
- "If I was you" is ALWAYS wrong on the ACT in a contrary-to-fact context.
- "Wh?" is ALWAYS correct when the pronoun is the object, even though "who" is common in speech.

Your job is to know these rules cold, apply them instantly, and never be fooled by the ACT's carefully constructed traps that exploit the gap between how people actually speak and how formal English requires them to write.

Master these rules. Practice them until they are automatic. And when you sit down on test day, let the rules — not your ear — be your guide.

---

*End of Chapter 5*

---


# Chapter 6: Rhetorical Skills Mastery — Transitions, Logic, and Paragraph Organization

---

## 6.0 — Introduction to Rhetorical Skills on the ACT English Test

The ACT English test is not merely an examination of grammatical correctness. Approximately one-half to slightly more than one-half of all questions (depending on the format—legacy or enhanced) are dedicated to **Rhetorical Skills**, officially classified under two reporting categories: **Production of Writing** and **Knowledge of Language**. These questions test your ability to function not just as a proofreader who spots comma errors, but as an **editor** who understands how ideas flow, how paragraphs cohere, how sentences connect logically, and how an author's purpose shapes every writing decision.

Rhetorical skills questions are fundamentally different from grammar questions in one critical respect: **they cannot be solved by "going by ear."** While grammar questions often have mechanically applicable rules (e.g., *a semicolon requires an independent clause on both sides*), rhetorical questions demand that you understand the **logical architecture** of a passage. You must read with the mindset of a structural engineer, examining how each beam (sentence) supports the arch (paragraph), and how each arch supports the bridge (the entire passage).

This chapter provides a comprehensive, exhaustive deep dive into every category of rhetorical skill tested on the ACT English section. We will cover:

1. Transition words and phrases — the most common rhetorical question type
2. Add/Delete questions — evaluating whether information belongs
3. Author intent and technique questions
4. Macro-logic and paragraph organization
5. Main idea and author's goal questions
6. Word choice, style, and tone (diction)
7. Concision and redundancy
8. Sentence placement and movement
9. Passage-level synthesis and concluding sentences

By the end of this chapter, you will possess the analytical framework necessary to approach any rhetorical question with confidence, precision, and a systematic methodology.

---

## 6.1 — The Architecture of Transitions: The Most Common Rhetorical Question Type

### 6.1.1 — Why Transitions Dominate

Transition questions are, statistically, the single most frequently tested rhetorical skill on the ACT English section. They appear in nearly every passage and can show up multiple times within a single passage. The reason is simple: transitions are the **connective tissue** of written language. They signal the logical relationship between ideas, and the ACT tests whether you can identify that relationship with surgical precision.

A transition question presents you with a sentence containing an underlined transition word or phrase. The answer choices will typically include transitions from different logical categories (e.g., contrast, addition, cause-effect). Your job is to determine which transition **most accurately reflects the logical relationship between the surrounding ideas**.

### 6.1.2 — The Eight Fundamental Logical Relationships

Every transition on the ACT English test falls into one of the following eight logical categories. Mastering these categories is not merely helpful—it is essential. You must be able to identify each relationship instantaneously, the way a musician recognizes intervals by ear.

---

#### **Category 1: Contrast**

**Definition:** The second idea presents an opposing, contradicting, or qualifying point relative to the first idea. The second idea may partially or fully negate the expectation created by the first.

**Common Transitions:**
- However
- Nevertheless
- On the other hand
- Conversely
- Whereas
- While (when used to mean "although" or "in contrast to the fact that")
- In contrast
- On the contrary
- Yet
- Although
- Even though
- Despite / Despite the fact that
- In spite of
- Nonetheless
- Instead
- Rather
- Still

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals that what follows is an **opposing point of view**, a **balance to an idea that is too positive or too negative**, or a **limitation on the claim just made**.

**Example Structure:**
> The experiment yielded promising results. **[CONTRAST]**, the sample size was too small to draw definitive conclusions.

Here, the first sentence creates a positive expectation (promising results). The second sentence limits or opposes that optimism (sample size too small). A contrast transition like "however" signals this logical pivot.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- "Whereas" and "while" are often used in formal writing to show direct contrast between two entities or situations, not just temporal simultaneity. *"The northern region is arid, whereas the southern region receives abundant rainfall."*
- "Despite" and "in spite of" show contrast but function as **prepositions**, not conjunctions. They are followed by a noun phrase or gerund, not a full clause. *"Despite the rain"* (correct) vs. *"Despite it rained"* (incorrect).
- "Nonetheless" operates almost identically to "nevertheless" but carries a slightly stronger connotation of perseverance or persistence in the face of the preceding obstacle.
- "On the contrary" is used specifically when **directly contradicting a negative statement or assumption**. It does not merely contrast; it actively refutes. *"The policy did not reduce poverty; on the contrary, it exacerbated inequality."*
- "Conversely" implies a **perfect inversion** or mirror-image relationship between two ideas. It is stronger than "however" and is best used when the two ideas are genuine opposites. *"Hot water expands; conversely, cold water contracts."*

---

#### **Category 2: Illustration / Support**

**Definition:** The second idea provides specific evidence, an example, or an emphasis that reinforces a general claim made in the first idea.

**Common Transitions:**
- In fact
- As a matter of fact
- Indeed
- To illustrate
- For example
- For instance
- Such as
- Specifically
- Namely
- In particular
- Particularly
- To demonstrate
- As evidence

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals what follows is **specific evidence or an example** designed to **give emphasis to a claim** by making it concrete.

**Example Structure:**
> The Renaissance was a period of extraordinary cultural achievement. **[ILLUSTRATION]**, Florence alone produced Leonardo da Vinci, Michelangelo, and Botticelli.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- "In fact" and "indeed" do NOT simply restate the preceding idea—they **intensify** it. They signal that what follows provides stronger, more compelling, or more precise evidence for the same point.
- "For example" and "for instance" are essentially interchangeable but test whether the example is **genuinely illustrative** of the preceding claim, not merely tangentially related.
- "Such as" introduces examples **within a sentence structure** (without a semicolon or period before it). It does not introduce a full independent clause. *"Several European countries, such as France and Germany, signed the agreement."*
- "Namely" signals that what follows is a **complete and exhaustive enumeration**, not merely an example. *"Three factors contributed to the collapse: namely, drought, famine, and political instability."*

---

#### **Category 3: Addition / Continuation**

**Definition:** The second idea adds new information, a supporting point, or an extension of the same line of reasoning already presented in the first idea. There is no contrast, no contradiction—only the accumulation of supporting or related evidence.

**Common Transitions:**
- Furthermore
- Additionally
- Moreover
- Also
- Too (used at the end of a sentence)
- As well as
- Not to mention
- Besides
- In addition
- Likewise
- Equally
- What is more

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals the addition of **new and supporting evidence** that advances the same argument or theme.

**Example Structure:**
> The new policy reduces carbon emissions significantly. **[ADDITION]**, it creates thousands of jobs in the renewable energy sector.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
**This is one of the most critical distinctions on the entire ACT English test:**

**"Furthermore" and "Moreover" are NOT synonymous, and the ACT exploits this constantly.**

- **"Furthermore"** signals that what follows will **extend the argument further**—it adds to the weight, scope, or intensity of the existing reasoning. It often implies that the argument is building toward a conclusion. It is cumulative and additive in the sense of building momentum.
  > *"The drug is effective in reducing symptoms. Furthermore, it has been shown to slow disease progression."*

- **"Moreover"** signals that what follows adds **an additional, reinforcing point** that makes the argument stronger. It emphasizes the quality or weight of the evidence rather than simply adding more of the same. It can imply that the added point is in some sense **more significant** or **more decisive** than what came before.
  > *"The defendant had motive and opportunity. Moreover, forensic evidence placed him at the scene of the crime."*

On the ACT, if the second sentence is simply adding a **parallel or supplementary** point, "furthermore" or "additionally" may be correct. If the second sentence is adding a **crucial, weightier, or more decisive** piece of evidence, "moreover" may be correct. The test often places both options as answer choices and expects you to distinguish based on the subtle relationship between the sentences.

- **"Additionally"** is the most neutral of the addition transitions. It simply adds a new point without any implication of weight or momentum. It is safe, straightforward, and often correct when neither "furthermore" nor "moreover" precisely fits.
- **"Also"** is informal and often appears at the beginning or within a sentence. On the ACT, it is rarely the best choice in formal prose, but it can be correct if all other options are worse.
- **"Likewise"** signals **parallel similarity** — what follows applies equally to a second subject as the preceding sentence established for a first subject. *"The first study confirmed the hypothesis. Likewise, the replication study produced similar results."*
- **"In addition"** functions identically to "additionally" but is slightly more formal.

---

#### **Category 4: Cause and Effect**

**Definition:** One idea causes, results in, produces, or logically leads to the other. The relationship may flow forward (cause → effect) or backward (effect ← cause), depending on sentence structure.

**Common Transitions:**
- Consequently
- As a result
- Because (of)
- Hence
- Therefore
- Thus
- Accordingly
- So
- For this reason
- Due to
- Owing to
- Since
- Thereby

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals a **cause-and-effect relationship** — the first idea either causes the second, or the second is the logical result of the first.

**Example Structure:**
> The drought lasted for three consecutive seasons. **[CAUSE-EFFECT]**, crop yields fell by forty percent.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- **"Consequently," "as a result," "therefore," "thus," "accordingly," "hence," and "for this reason"** all signal an **effect or result**. They follow a cause and introduce its consequence. They are typically used at the beginning of a sentence (followed by a comma) or within a clause.
- **"Because," "since," "due to," "owing to," and "because of"** signal a **cause or reason**. They introduce the explanation for an effect already stated or about to be stated.
- **"Thus" and "hence"** are slightly more formal than "so" and are preferred in academic writing. "So" is frequently used on the informal end of the spectrum but can be correct on the ACT if the context is less formal.
- **"Thereby"** is an adverb meaning "by that means" or "as a result of that action." It is followed by a present participle (-ing form). *"The company reduced overhead costs, thereby increasing its profit margins."*
- **"Accordingly"** carries a strong sense of **appropriate or expected consequence** — it implies that the effect follows naturally and logically from the cause, as the proper or fitting result.
- The ACT frequently tests whether you distinguish between cause-and-effect transitions and addition transitions. **The critical question is: does the second sentence CAUSE or RESULT FROM the first, or does it simply ADD to it?** If there is no causal relationship, a cause-effect transition is incorrect.

---

#### **Category 5: Concession**

**Definition:** The first idea acknowledges or admits the validity of a position, argument, or fact that might seem to undermine the main argument. The second idea then asserts the main argument despite that acknowledged limitation.

**Common Transitions:**
- Granted
- Nevertheless (also appears in Contrast)
- Although / Even though
- Regardless
- Despite / In spite of (also appear in Contrast)
- Even so
- Nonetheless (also appears in Contrast)
- Still (also appears in Contrast)
- Admittedly
- To be sure
- Of course
- Naturally

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition **cedes a prior claim** to give space for a **more important claim** that follows (or, if the concession comes second, acknowledges a limitation while maintaining the main point).

**Example Structure:**
> **[CONCESSION]**, the project was completed behind schedule. **[MAIN POINT]**, the final product exceeded all quality benchmarks.

Or:

> The final product exceeded all quality benchmarks, **[CONCESSION]** the project was completed behind schedule.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- Concession is **distinct from simple contrast**. A contrast simply opposes two ideas. A concession **acknowledges the strength or validity** of the opposing point before reaffirming the main argument. It involves a degree of **yielding** on a secondary point to stand firm on a primary one.
- "Granted" is almost always used to introduce a concession at the beginning of a sentence. *"Granted, the methodology has limitations. Nevertheless, the findings represent a significant advance."*
- "Admittedly" functions similarly to "granted" — it concedes a point, often reluctantly. *"Admittedly, the sample size was limited. However, the statistical significance remained robust."*
- "Even so" combines the idea of concession with continuation — it says, "Even given what was just stated, what follows remains true."
- The ACT sometimes tests concession by presenting the **main point first** and the **concession second**, which is less intuitive but equally valid. *"The theory remains the most widely accepted model in the field, although several anomalies have yet to be explained."*

---

#### **Category 6: Sequence / Order**

**Definition:** Ideas are presented in a specific temporal, chronological, or logical order. The transition signals where in the sequence the current idea falls.

**Common Transitions:**
- Finally
- Subsequently
- Previously
- Eventually
- Next
- Then
- Before / Beforehand
- After / Afterward
- Meanwhile
- Simultaneously
- Thereafter
- Henceforth
- First / Second / Third
- Initially
- In the end
- Ultimately

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals **order or sequencing** of events, steps, or ideas moving forward (or occasionally backward) through time or logic.

**Example Structure:**
> The committee reviewed the initial proposals. **[SEQUENCE]**, they conducted site visits to each location. **[SEQUENCE]**, they announced the winning design.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- **"Subsequently"** means "afterward" or "following that event." It establishes a clear **chronological succession**. *"The bill passed the House. Subsequently, it was sent to the Senate for consideration."*
- **"Finally"** signals the **last item in a sequence**, not merely a subsequent action. The ACT will test whether "finally" is appropriate only when the idea truly concludes a series. If the passage is still developing a sequence without reaching its end, "finally" is incorrect.
- **"Ultimately"** means "in the end" or "after everything has been considered." It implies a **definitive resolution** and is more emphatic than "finally." It carries a connotation of finality after a process of deliberation or development.
- **"Meanwhile"** signals **simultaneous occurrence** — two events happening at the same time. It is not sequential but parallel in time.
- **"Previously"** moves the timeline **backward**, indicating that something occurred before the last-mentioned event.
- **"Thereafter"** means "after that time/event." It is more formal than "afterward" and indicates that from a certain point forward, a condition held. *"The treaty was signed in 1945. Thereafter, trade between the two nations flourished."*

---

#### **Category 7: Similarity / Analogy**

**Definition:** The second idea is similar to, mirrors, or parallels the first idea. The comparison underscores a constant theme or shared characteristic.

**Common Transitions:**
- In the same way
- Similarly
- Likewise
- By the same token
- In a similar fashion
- Corresponding to
- Just as... so (too)
- Equally

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals **similarity** with some **constant theme** being maintained across the comparison.

**Example Structure:**
> The first reactor design prioritized efficiency. **[SIMILARITY]**, the second prototype emphasized safety.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- **"By the same token"** means "for the same reason" or "following the same logic." It implies that because one thing is true by a certain reasoning process, another related thing must also be true by the **same reasoning**. *"Lower interest rates encourage borrowing. By the same token, they can lead to inflationary pressure."*
- **"Likewise"** implies that what applies to the first subject or situation **equally applies** to the second. It is one of the most commonly tested transitions on the ACT.
- **"Similarly"** is the most neutral similarity transition. It simply indicates that the following idea is comparable or analogous to the preceding one.
- **"Equally"** is less commonly used as a transition at the start of a sentence but functions similarly when it appears.

---

#### **Category 8: Conclusion / Restatement**

**Definition:** The following idea summarizes, concludes, or restates the preceding material in a condensed form.

**Common Transitions:**
- In conclusion
- In summary
- To sum up
- In short
- In other words
- To put it briefly
- Overall
- All in all
- In essence
- In brief
- To recap
- On the whole

**True Relationship Signal:** The transition signals a **conclusion drawn from the preceding evidence** or a **restatement** that rephrases a more complex idea in simpler terms.

**Example Structure:**
> The study confirmed that regular exercise improves cardiovascular health, reduces stress, and enhances cognitive function. **[CONCLUSION]**, physical activity is one of the most impactful investments in overall well-being.

**Nuances to Watch For:**
- **"In other words"** is particularly interesting — it signals a **rephrasing or clarification** of the same idea, not a new point. It says: "Let me express the same concept differently for clarity." *"The patient is in an irreversible coma; in other words, there is no brain activity."*
- **"Overall"** and **"on the whole"** signal a **generalization after specific instances**. They synthesize multiple individual data points or arguments into a single broad conclusion.
- **"In short"** and **"to sum up"** reduce the preceding argument to its most essential form. They are typically used at the **end of a passage or section**, not in the middle of an ongoing argument.
- **"In essence"** distills the **fundamental nature** of the preceding material, stripping away specifics to reveal the core idea. *"The negotiations were complex and protracted. In essence, both sides wanted the same thing but could not agree on the method."*

---

### 6.1.3 — The Definitive Transition Process (Step-by-Step Methodology)

When you encounter a transition question, follow this exact process. Do not deviate from it. Speed and accuracy come from repetition of this framework until it becomes automatic.
