The answer choices are:
- Yes, because the sentence is [not needed / redundant / etc.]
- No, because the sentence provides [necessary information / a transition / etc.]

### 7.25 The Decision Framework for Add/Delete Questions

When evaluating whether a sentence should be added or deleted, ask yourself these questions in order:

**Step 1: What is the sentence's function?**
- Does it introduce a new idea?
- Does it provide an example or evidence?
- Does it restate a previous point?
- Does it transition between ideas?
- Does it provide background or context?
- Does it conclude or summarize?

**Step 2: Is this function already served elsewhere in the passage?**
- If the same information is provided in another sentence or paragraph, the sentence is likely **redundant** and should be **deleted** (or not added).
- If the information is genuinely new and needed, the sentence should be **kept** (or added).

**Step 3: Does the sentence connect logically to its surroundings?**
- Does it follow from the previous sentence?
- Does it set up the next sentence?
- Does it maintain the flow of ideas?
- If it disrupts the flow or introduces an unrelated tangent, it should be **deleted** (or not added).

**Step 4: Does the sentence serve the author's overall purpose?**
- Does it advance the argument, narrative, or explanation?
- Does it support the main idea of the paragraph?
- If it is off-topic or tangential, it should be **deleted** (or not added).

### 7.26 Common Reasons to Delete a Sentence

| Reason | Explanation | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Redundancy** | The sentence repeats information already stated | The passage already states that the Amazon rainforest produces 20% of the world's oxygen. A new sentence saying "The Amazon generates one-fifth of the Earth's oxygen supply" is redundant. |
| **Off-topic** | The sentence introduces an unrelated idea | A passage about the history of jazz music suddenly includes a sentence about classical composers. Unless the connection is made explicit, this is off-topic. |
| **Disrupts flow** | The sentence breaks the logical progression | A passage describes a problem, then its causes, then its effects. A sentence about an unrelated problem inserted between causes and effects disrupts the flow. |
| **Contradicts the passage** | The sentence says something that opposes the author's argument | The author argues that space exploration is valuable. A sentence saying "Space exploration has produced no tangible benefits" contradicts the passage. |
| **Too specific** | The sentence provides a level of detail that doesn't match the passage's scope | A general overview of the American Revolution includes a sentence about the exact number of buttons on George Washington's coat. This is inappropriately specific. |
| **Too general** | The sentence makes a broad claim that the passage doesn't support | A passage about one scientist's research includes a sentence making a sweeping claim about all scientists. |

### 7.27 Common Reasons to Keep or Add a Sentence

| Reason | Explanation | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Necessary context** | The sentence provides background the reader needs | A passage about a political scandal includes a sentence explaining who the politician is. Without this, the reader is lost. |
| **Transition** | The sentence connects two ideas that would otherwise be jarring | A passage shifts from discussing problems to proposing solutions. A transitional sentence bridges these sections. |
| **Supporting evidence** | The sentence provides an example, statistic, or anecdote that supports the main argument | An argument about the benefits of exercise includes a sentence citing a study showing reduced heart disease risk. |
| **Clarification** | The sentence explains a complex idea that was introduced | A passage introduces a technical term and then includes a sentence defining it. |
| **Conclusion** | The sentence wraps up the passage's argument or narrative | The final paragraph includes a sentence that summarizes the main points and restates the thesis. |
| **Emotional or rhetorical impact** | The sentence serves a narrative or persuasive purpose beyond pure information | A narrative passage includes a sentence that creates suspense or empathy. |

### 7.28 The "Add to What?" Trap

One of the most common traps on add/delete questions is the answer choice that says something like:

- "Yes, because the information is interesting"
- "Yes, because the fact is true"
- "No, because the information is untrue"

**These are almost always wrong.** The ACT does not care whether a fact is true or false in the real world. It cares whether the information *belongs in this passage*. A fact can be true and still be irrelevant. A detail can be fascinating and still be off-topic.

**The only relevant question is: Does this sentence serve the author's purpose in this passage?**

### 7.29 The "New Information" Nuance

Sometimes an add/delete question presents a sentence that introduces information not found anywhere else in the passage. This does NOT automatically mean it should be deleted. The key question is whether the new information is:

- **Relevant and necessary** → Keep/add
- **Relevant but unnecessary** (the passage works fine without it) → Delete
- **Irrelevant** → Delete

**Example:** A passage describes the life cycle of a butterfly. The author is considering adding a sentence about the migration patterns of monarch butterflies.

- If the passage is specifically about monarch butterflies → The sentence is relevant and likely should be added.
- If the passage is about butterflies in general and has not mentioned migration → The sentence may be relevant but could also be a tangent. Read carefully to determine whether the surrounding context supports this addition.
- If the passage is about the biological process of metamorphosis → The migration sentence is off-topic and should be deleted.

---

## Part VII: Relevance and Supporting Material

### 7.30 Evaluating Relevance

Relevance questions ask you to determine whether a particular piece of information is necessary or appropriate within the passage. They may appear as:

- "Which of the following is most relevant to the passage?"
- "The author includes the underlined information in order to..."
- "Which detail best supports the author's argument?"

**Strategy:**

1. **Identify the main idea** of the passage (or the paragraph, if the question is paragraph-specific).
2. **Evaluate each answer choice** against that main idea. The correct choice will directly support, illustrate, or advance the main idea.
3. **Eliminate choices that are tangential,** even if they are factually accurate or topically related in a broad sense.

### 7.31 Types of Supporting Material

The ACT passages may include various types of supporting material. Understanding these helps you evaluate whether a piece of support is effective:

| Type | Function | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Statistics/Data** | Provide quantitative evidence | "According to the EPA, 91% of plastic waste is not recycled." |
| **Examples** | Make abstract claims concrete | "Many species, including the dodo bird and the passenger pigeon, have gone extinct due to human activity." |
| **Anecdotes** | Provide personal, narrative evidence | "My grandmother grew up in a house without electricity, which shaped her appreciation for simple things." |
| **Expert Testimony** | Provide authoritative support | "Dr. Sarah Chen, a climatologist at MIT, argues that current warming trends are unprecedented." |
| **Analogies/Comparisons** | Make unfamiliar concepts relatable | "The structure of an atom is like a miniature solar system, with electrons orbiting the nucleus like planets orbiting the sun." |
| **Historical References** | Provide context or precedent | "The last time a similar policy was attempted was during the New Deal era." |
| **Definitions** | Clarify key terms | "Biodiversity refers to the variety of life in a particular ecosystem." |

### 7.32 Matching Support to Claim

When a question asks which piece of evidence best supports a claim, follow this process:

1. **Identify the claim** precisely. What exactly is the author arguing or asserting?
2. **Determine what kind of evidence** would best support it. A claim about frequency needs statistics. A claim about personal experience needs anecdotes. A claim about a process needs examples.
3. **Evaluate each answer choice** by asking: "Does this directly and specifically support the claim, or is it only loosely related?"
4. **Select the most direct and specific support.**

**Example:**

Claim: "Regular physical exercise significantly improves mental health outcomes."

Which piece of evidence best supports this?

*(A):* "Exercise has many health benefits."
→ Too vague. Does not specifically address mental health.

*(B):* "A 2019 meta-analysis of 49 studies found that participants who exercised regularly were 25% less likely to develop depression."
→ Direct, specific, and relevant. This is the correct answer.

*(C):* "Many doctors recommend exercise."
→ Vague and indirect. Does not address mental health specifically.

*(D):* "Exercise can be done in many different forms."
→ Irrelevant to the claim about mental health outcomes.

---

## Part VIII: Macro-Organization and Paragraph Logic

### 7.33 How the ACT Tests Organization

Organization questions ask you to evaluate the logical arrangement of ideas within a passage. They may ask:

- "Where should this sentence/paragraph be placed?"
- "What is the most logical order for these sentences/paragraphs?"
- "At which point should the author place the underlined sentence?"

### 7.34 Principles of Logical Organization

Effective organization follows these principles:

**1. General before specific**
- Introduce a broad idea before diving into details.
- "Many factors contribute to climate change. One significant factor is the burning of fossil fuels, which releases carbon dioxide into the atmosphere."

**2. Chronological order**
- When describing events or processes, proceed in time order.
- "First, the colonists arrived. Next, they established settlements. Finally, they negotiated treaties with indigenous peoples."

**3. Claim before evidence**
- State your point before providing support.
- "The new policy was ineffective. Test scores remained unchanged, and attendance rates actually declined."

**4. Name before reference**
- Introduce a person, concept, or term before using pronouns or shorthand to refer to it.
- "Dr. Maria Gonzalez published a groundbreaking study. Her research demonstrated..." (Not: "Her research demonstrated... Dr. Maria Gonzalez published a groundbreaking study.")

**5. Cause before effect (usually)**
- Present the cause before the effect for clarity.
- "Because of the drought, crop yields declined." (Cause → Effect)
- "Crop yields declined because of the drought." (Effect → Cause — also acceptable, but the cause is still identified)

**6. Problem before solution**
- Establish the problem before proposing solutions.
- "The city's infrastructure is aging. To address this, the mayor proposed a $2 billion renovation plan."

**7. Old information before new information**
- Begin sentences with information the reader already knows (from previous sentences) before introducing new information. This creates a smooth reading experience.

### 7.35 Strategy for Sentence Placement Questions

When asked where a sentence should be placed:

1. **Read the sentence to be placed.** Identify its topic, its pronouns, and any transitional words.
2. **Look for pronoun references.** If the sentence contains a pronoun ("this," "it," "they," "these"), find the noun it refers to. The sentence must be placed *after* that noun is introduced.
3. **Look for transitional words.** If the sentence begins with "however," it must follow a sentence it contrasts with. If it begins with "for example," it must follow a sentence it illustrates.
4. **Look for logical connections.** The sentence should connect to the sentence before it (as a continuation, example, or elaboration) AND to the sentence after it (as a setup, cause, or introduction).
5. **Test each option mentally.** If there are only a few possible positions, try the sentence in each position and see which creates the smoothest flow.

### 7.36 Strategy for Paragraph Ordering Questions

When asked about the most logical order for paragraphs:

1. **Identify the topic sentence** of each paragraph. This reveals what each paragraph is about.
2. **Look for transitional words** at the beginning of paragraphs. "However" signals contrast with the previous paragraph. "For example" signals illustration of a point made previously. "In conclusion" signals the final paragraph.
3. **Identify the introduction.** The first paragraph should introduce the topic, provide context, and (usually) present the thesis or main idea.
4. **Identify the conclusion.** The last paragraph should summarize, restate the thesis, or provide a final thought.
5. **Arrange the middle paragraphs** using the principles above (general→specific, chronological, claim→evidence, etc.).

---

## Part IX: Transitions — The Most Common Rhetorical Question Type

*(Note: Transitions were covered in the personal notes section, but we will expand here with additional depth and nuance.)*

### 7.37 Why Transitions Matter So Much on the ACT

Transition questions are the single most common type of rhetorical skills question on the ACT. They appear in virtually every passage and test your ability to identify the logical relationship between ideas and select the word or phrase that best expresses that relationship.

### 7.38 The Complete Transition Taxonomy

Below is an exhaustive taxonomy of transitions organized by logical relationship, with detailed notes on usage and common confusions.

#### Category 1: Addition
*These transitions add a new idea that supports or extends the previous one.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **Furthermore** | Adds a point that strengthens the argument. Formal. Often used when the new point is *more important* than the previous one. |
| **Moreover** | Similar to "furthermore" but often used when the new point is *equally important* or provides additional evidence. Some test prep sources distinguish them; on the ACT, they are largely interchangeable. |
| **Additionally** | Neutral addition. Less formal than "furthermore" or "moreover." |
| **Also** | Simple addition. Can appear at the beginning or middle of a sentence. |
| **In addition** | Neutral addition. Often followed by a comma. |
| **Besides** | Adds a point, sometimes with a connotation of "in addition to what has already been said." |
| **Likewise** | Adds a point that mirrors or parallels the previous one. |
| **Similarly** | Adds a point that is analogous to the previous one. |
| **Too** | Informal addition. Usually appears at the end of a sentence. |
| **As well as** | Addition within a sentence structure (not at the beginning). |

#### Category 2: Contrast
*These transitions signal that the upcoming idea differs from or opposes the previous one.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **However** | The most common contrast transition. Signals a direct opposition or qualification. Must be used with correct punctuation (semicolon before, comma after when joining independent clauses). |
| **Nevertheless** | Signals that despite the previous point, the following point is true. Slightly more concessive than "however." |
| **Nonetheless** | Similar to "nevertheless." |
| **On the other hand** | Presents an alternative perspective. Often paired with "on the one hand" but not always. |
| **Conversely** | Signals that the opposite is true. Used when the second idea is the *reverse* of the first. |
| **In contrast** | Signals a direct comparison of opposites. |
| **On the contrary** | Signals that the previous statement is wrong and the following statement corrects it. |
| **Yet** | A coordinating conjunction that signals contrast. Can appear at the beginning of a sentence on the ACT (despite traditional style guide prohibitions). |
| **But** | The simplest contrast transition. A coordinating conjunction. |
| **Although** | A subordinating conjunction that introduces a concessive clause. The contrast comes in the main clause. |
| **Even though** | Stronger version of "although." |
| **Though** | Less formal version of "although." Can appear at the beginning, middle, or end of a sentence. |
| **Whereas** | A subordinating conjunction that directly compares two contrasting ideas. |
| **While** | Can signal contrast (not time) when used at the beginning of a sentence. "While some scholars agree, others dispute the claim." |
| **Despite / In spite of** | Prepositional phrases that signal contrast. Followed by a noun or gerund, not a full clause. |
| **Regardless** | Signals that the following point holds true despite the previous one. |

#### Category 3: Cause and Effect
*These transitions signal a causal relationship between ideas.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **Therefore** | Signals a logical conclusion or result. Formal. |
| **Consequently** | Signals a direct result. Formal. |
| **As a result** | Signals a result. Less formal than "therefore" or "consequently." |
| **Thus** | Signals a conclusion or result. Can also mean "in this way" (illustration). |
| **Hence** | Signals a result. Formal. Sometimes means "from this point." |
| **Accordingly** | Signals that the result follows logically from the cause. |
| **So** | Simple cause-and-effect. Can be a coordinating conjunction. |
| **Because** | Introduces a cause. Subordinating conjunction. |
| **Since** | Can introduce a cause (not just a time reference). |
| **For** | As a coordinating conjunction, means "because." Rare in modern usage but appears on the ACT. |
| **Due to** | Prepositional phrase introducing a cause. Followed by a noun. |
| **Owing to** | Similar to "due to." Formal. |

#### Category 4: Illustration/Example
*These transitions signal that the upcoming sentence provides an example or specific evidence.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **For example** | The most common illustration transition. |
| **For instance** | Similar to "for example." Slightly less formal. |
| **To illustrate** | Formal. Often used in academic writing. |
| **Specifically** | Narrows from a general statement to a specific one. |
| **In particular** | Similar to "specifically." |
| **Such as** | Introduces examples within a sentence (not at the beginning). |
| **Namely** | Introduces a precise specification. Formal. |
| **Indeed** | Adds emphasis or confirmation to a previous point. |
| **In fact** | Adds emphasis, often strengthening a claim. |
| **As a matter of fact** | Similar to "in fact." Slightly wordier. |

#### Category 5: Sequence/Order
*These transitions signal the order of events, steps, or ideas.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **First / Second / Third** | Basic sequencing. |
| **Next** | The following step or point. |
| **Then** | The following step. Can be informal. |
| **Finally** | The last step or point in a series. |
| **Lastly** | Similar to "finally." |
| **Subsequently** | Formal. Means "afterward." |
| **Previously** | Means "before." |
| **Before** | Introduces an earlier event. |
| **After** | Introduces a later event. |
| **Meanwhile** | Signals simultaneous events. |
| **Eventually** | Signals that something happened after a period of time. |
| **Initially** | Signals the beginning of a sequence. |
| **Ultimately** | Signals the final outcome. |

#### Category 6: Similarity
*These transitions signal that the upcoming idea is similar to or reinforces the previous one.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **Similarly** | Signals a parallel or analogous idea. |
| **Likewise** | Signals that the same applies. |
| **In the same way** | Signals a parallel approach or idea. |
| **By the same token** | Formal. Signals that the same logic applies. |
| **Correspondingly** | Formal. Signals a matching relationship. |

#### Category 7: Concession
*These transitions signal that the author acknowledges a counterargument or limitation before returning to their main point.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **Granted** | Concedes a point before pivoting. |
| **Admittedly** | Concedes a point. |
| **Of course** | Concedes an obvious point. |
| **Naturally** | Concedes an expected point. |
| **To be sure** | Concedes a point. |
| **Certainly** | Concedes a point (when used concessively). |
| **It is true that** | Concedes a point before pivoting. |
| **Even so** | Concedes a point but maintains the main argument. |
| **Still** | Similar to "even so." |
| **All the same** | Similar to "even so." |

#### Category 8: Conclusion/Summary
*These transitions signal that the author is wrapping up or summarizing.*

| Transition | Notes |
|---|---|
| **In conclusion** | Signals the final summary. |
| **To conclude** | Similar to "in conclusion." |
| **In summary** | Signals a summary of main points. |
| **To sum up** | Similar to "in summary." |
| **In short** | Signals a concise restatement. |
| **In other words** | Signals a restatement in different terms. |
| **Overall** | Signals a general conclusion. |
| **Ultimately** | Signals the final point. |
| **All in all** | Signals a comprehensive summary. |

### 7.39 The Process for Transition Questions: A Detailed Walkthrough

**Step 1: Read the sentence containing the transition word AND the sentence before it.**

You need both to determine the logical relationship. The transition word is the *bridge* between two ideas, and you cannot understand a bridge without seeing both sides.

**Step 2: Identify the relationship WITHOUT looking at the answer choices.**

Before you look at the options, determine for yourself whether the second sentence:
- Adds to the first (addition)
- Contrasts with the first (contrast)
- Shows a result of the first (cause & effect)
- Provides an example of the first (illustration)
- Shows a sequence (order)
- Is similar to the first (similarity)
- Concedes a point (concession)
- Summarizes (conclusion)

**Step 3: Select the transition that matches your identified relationship.**

**Step 4: Verify by reading the full sentence with your chosen transition.** Does it make sense? Does it flow naturally?

### 7.40 Common Transition Traps

**Trap 1: "However" vs. "Therefore"**
These are the two most commonly confused transitions on the test. "However" signals contrast; "therefore" signals result. Students often choose "however" when the sentence is actually a result of the previous one.

*Example:* "The company invested heavily in research and development. [However/Therefore], it launched a groundbreaking new product."

The second sentence is a *result* of the first, not a contrast. "Therefore" is correct.

**Trap 2: "Moreover" vs. "However"**
"Moreover" adds a supporting point. "However" introduces a contrast. If the second sentence strengthens the first, "Moreover" is correct. If it opposes the first, "However" is correct.

**Trap 3: "For example" vs. "In contrast"**
If the second sentence provides a specific instance of the general claim in the first, "For example" is correct. If it presents an opposing idea, "In contrast" is correct.

**Trap 4: Transitions that change the meaning**
Sometimes two transitions are grammatically possible but create different meanings. The correct one must match the author's intent as revealed by the passage.

**Trap 5: The "NO CHANGE" trap on transition questions**
If the existing transition is incorrect, "NO CHANGE" is wrong. Always verify that the existing transition accurately reflects the logical relationship.

---

## Part X: Word Choice (Diction) Questions

### 7.41 What Diction Questions Test

Diction questions ask you to select the word that best fits the context. They test:

1. **Denotation** — Does the word mean what the sentence requires?
2. **Connotation** — Does the word's emotional association match the passage's tone?
3. **Precision** — Is the word specific enough for the context?
4. **Register** — Is the word appropriate for the passage's level of formality?
5. **Idiomatic usage** — Is the word used in its correct idiomatic context?

### 7.42 Strategy for Diction Questions

1. **Read the full sentence** containing the underlined word.
2. **Determine what the word needs to accomplish** in the sentence. What meaning must it convey? What tone must it match?
3. **Predict the type of word** needed before looking at the choices. (e.g., "I need a word that means 'to make something seem less important' and has a negative connotation.")
4. **Evaluate each choice** against your prediction.
5. **Eliminate choices** that fail on any dimension (wrong meaning, wrong connotation, wrong register, wrong idiomatic usage).
6. **If two choices remain,** look at the surrounding sentences for additional context clues.

### 7.43 Commonly Confused Word Pairs on the ACT

| Pair | Distinction |
|---|---|
| **Affect vs. Effect** | *Affect* (verb) = to influence; *Effect* (noun) = a result. (Note: "effect" can also be a verb meaning "to bring about," as in "to effect change.") |
| **Accept vs. Except** | *Accept* = to receive; *Except* = excluding. |
| **Adapt vs. Adopt vs. Adept** | *Adapt* = to adjust; *Adopt* = to take up or choose; *Adept* = skilled. |
| **Allusion vs. Illusion** | *Allusion* = an indirect reference; *Illusion* = a false perception. |
| **Ambiguous vs. Ambivalent** | *Ambiguous* = having multiple interpretations; *Ambivalent* = having mixed feelings. |
| **Complement vs. Compliment** | *Complement* = to complete or enhance; *Compliment* = to praise. |
| **Compose vs. Comprise** | *Compose* = to make up (the parts compose the whole); *Comprise* = to consist of (the whole comprises the parts). |
| **Continual vs. Continuous** | *Continual* = recurring frequently; *Continuous* = without interruption. |
| **Counsel vs. Council** | *Counsel* = advice or to advise; *Council* = a group of people. |
| **Disinterested vs. Uninterested** | *Disinterested* = impartial; *Uninterested* = not interested. |
| **Elicit vs. Illicit** | *Elicit* = to draw out; *Illicit* = illegal. |
| **Emigrate vs. Immigrate** | *Emigrate* = to leave one's country; *Immigrate* = to enter a new country. |
| **Ensure vs. Insure vs. Assure** | *Ensure* = to make certain; *Insure* = to provide insurance; *Assure* = to remove doubt from a person. |
| **Farther vs. Further** | *Farther* = physical distance; *Further* = figurative distance or degree. |
| **Fewer vs. Less** | *Fewer* = countable items; *Less* = uncountable quantities. |
| **Imply vs. Infer** | *Imply* = to suggest (speaker/writer); *Infer* = to conclude (listener/reader). |
| **Lay vs. Lie** | *Lay* = to put down (requires a direct object); *Lie* = to recline (no direct object). |
| **Precede vs. Proceed** | *Precede* = to come before; *Proceed* = to continue or go forward. |
| **Principal vs. Principle** | *Principal* = main or most important; *Principle* = a rule or belief. |
| **Stationary vs. Stationery** | *Stationary* = not moving; *Stationery* = writing materials. |
| **Than vs. Then** | *Than* = comparison; *Then* = time sequence. |
| **Their vs. There vs. They're** | *Their* = possessive; *There* = location/existence; *They're* = they are. |
| **Who vs. Whom** | *Who* = subject; *Whom* = object. |

### 7.44 Idiomatic Preposition Usage

The ACT occasionally tests whether a word is paired with the correct preposition. These are idiomatic—there is no general rule, so you must rely on your knowledge of standard English usage.

**Common idiomatic prepositions tested:**

| Correct Usage | Incorrect Usage |
|---|---|
| "accuse **of**" | "accuse **for**" |
| "adapt **to**" | "adapt **with**" |
| "agree **with** (a person)" | "agree **to** (a person)" |
| "agree **to** (a proposal)" | "agree **with** (a proposal)" |
| "agree **on** (a matter)" | "agree **about** (a matter)" |
| "angry **with**" | "angry **at**" (a person) |
| "anxious **about**" | "anxious **for**" (in the sense of worry) |
| "aware **of**" | "aware **about**" |
| "believe **in**" | "believe **to**" |
| "capable **of**" | "capable **to**" |
| "comply **with**" | "comply **to**" |
| "concerned **about/with**" | "concerned **for**" (in the sense of worry) |
| "consists **of**" | "consists **in**" (different meaning) |
| "deal **with**" | "deal **about**" |
| "depend **on**" | "depend **upon**" (also correct, but "on" is more common) |
| "differ **from**" | "differ **than**" |
| "different **from**" | "different **than**" (traditionally considered incorrect, though increasingly common) |
| "disappointed **in/with**" | "disappointed **about**" |
| "divide **among** (3+)" | "divide **between** (2)" |
| "equal **to**" | "equal **with**" |
| "familiar **with**" | "familiar **to**" (different meaning) |
| "fond **of**" | "fond **about**" |
| "forgetful **of**" | "forgetful **about**" |
| "free **of/from**" | "free **of**" (preferred) |
| "frightened **of/by**" | "frightened **from**" |
| "full **of**" | "full **with**" |
| "guilty **of**" | "guilty **for**" |
| "independent **of**" | "independent **from**" (also used, but "of" is preferred) |
| "incompatible **with**" | "incompatible **to**" |
| "inferior **to**" | "inferior **than**" |
| "in search **of**" | "in search **for**" |
| "interested **in**" | "interested **about**" |
| "jealous **of**" | "jealous **about**" |
| "married **to**" | "married **with**" |
| "necessity **for**" | "necessity **of**" (different meaning) |
| "need **for**" | "need **of**" (different meaning) |
| "object **to**" | "object **against**" |
| "opposed **to**" | "opposed **against**" |
| "particular **about**" | "particular **for**" |
| "patient **with**" | "patient **to**" |
| "persist **in**" | "persist **on**" |
| "pleased **with**" | "pleased **about**" |
| "point **at/to**" | "point **on**" |
| "prefer **to**" | "prefer **than**" |
| "prejudiced **against**" | "prejudiced **toward**" |
| "prevent **from**" | "prevent **to**" |
| "proper **for**" | "proper **to**" |
| "proud **of**" | "proud **about**" |
| "reason **for**" | "reason **of**" |
| "refer **to**" | "refer **at**" |
| **"regard** as" | "regard **to**" (different meaning) |
| "related **to**" | "related **with**" |
| "rely **on**" | "rely **upon**" (also correct) |
| "remind **of**" | "remind **about**" |
| "respond **to**" | "respond **with**" (different meaning) |
| "result **in**" | "result **to**" |
| "result **from**" | "result **because**" |
| "rich **in**" | "rich **with**" (different meaning) |
| "rude **to**" | "rude **with**" |
| "safe **from**" | "safe **of**" |
| "same **as**" | "same **than**" |
| "satisfied **with**" | "satisfied **about**" |
| "scared **of/by**" | "scared **from**" |
| "search **for**" | "search **of**" (different meaning) |
| "sensitive **to**" | "sensitive **about**" |
| "separate **from**" | "separate **than**" |
| "serious **about**" | "serious **for**" |
| "shocked **at/by**" | "shocked **from**" |
| "similar **to**" | "similar **with**" |
| "sorry **for**" (regret) | "sorry **about**" (also used) |
| "succeed **in**" | "succeed **at**" (also used, different nuance) |
| "suffer **from**" | "suffer **with**" (different meaning) |
| "superior **to**" | "superior **than**" |
| "surprised **at/by**" | "surprised **for**" |
| "suspect **of**" | "suspect **for**" |
| "sympathetic **to/with**" | "sympathetic **for**" |
| "tendency **to/toward**" | "tendency **for**" |
| "think **about/of**" | "think **on**" (different meaning) |
| "tired **of**" | "tired **from**" (different meaning) |
| "unaware **of**" | "unaware **about**" |
| "upset **with**" | "upset **for**" |
| "useful **for/to**" | "useful **with**" |
| "warn **of/about**" | "warn **from**" |
| "worthy **of**" | "worthy **to**" |

---

## Part XI: Combining Sentences and Sentence Combining Questions

### 7.45 The Sentence Combining Question Type

Some ACT questions ask you to combine two sentences into one. These test your ability to:

1. Eliminate redundancy
2. Use correct punctuation and conjunctions
3. Maintain the original meaning
4. Create the most concise and effective result

### 7.46 Strategies for Sentence Combining

**Strategy 1: Use a subordinating conjunction**
Turn one sentence into a dependent clause:
- "The storm was severe. It caused widespread damage." → "Because the storm was severe, it caused widespread damage." or "The severe storm caused widespread damage."

**Strategy 2: Use a relative clause**
- "The book was published in 1984. It became a bestseller." → "The book, which was published in 1984, became a bestseller."

**Strategy 3: Use a participial phrase**
- "She finished her homework. She went to bed." → "Having finished her homework, she went to bed."

**Strategy 4: Use a coordinating conjunction**
- "The museum is large. It contains rare artifacts." → "The museum is large and contains rare artifacts."

**Strategy 5: Use a semicolon**
- "The experiment was successful. The results exceeded expectations." → "The experiment was successful; the results exceeded expectations."

**Strategy 6: Use an appositive**
- "Dr. Smith is a renowned physicist. She won the Nobel Prize." → "Dr. Smith, a renowned physicist, won the Nobel Prize."

### 7.47 What to Avoid in Sentence Combining

- **Creating a run-on sentence** — Two independent clauses cannot be joined by only a comma.
- **Changing the meaning** — The combined sentence must preserve the original relationship between the ideas.
- **Creating unnecessary wordiness** — The combined version should be at least as concise as the original sentences.
- **Losing important details** — Do not omit information that was present in the original sentences.

---

## Part XII: Rhetorical Synthesis Questions

### 7.48 Understanding Rhetorical Synthesis

Rhetorical synthesis questions present information (often in bullet points, a chart, or a short list) and ask you to select the answer that best uses this information to achieve a specific rhetorical goal.

These questions test your ability to:
1. Read and understand data or notes
2. Identify the rhetorical purpose (e.g., to persuade, to inform, to emphasize)
3. Select the phrasing that most effectively achieves that purpose using the provided information

### 7.49 Strategy for Rhetorical Synthesis

1. **Identify the goal stated in the question.** What is the author trying to accomplish? (e.g., "emphasize the contrast," "provide evidence for the claim," "acknowledge a limitation")

2. **Review the provided information.** What data, facts, or notes are available?

3. **Evaluate each answer choice** against the goal:
   - Does it use the provided information accurately?
   - Does it achieve the stated rhetorical goal?
   - Is it concise and clear?

4. **Eliminate choices that:**
   - Misrepresent the provided information
   - Fail to achieve the stated goal
   - Introduce information not present in the provided material
   - Are unnecessarily wordy

5. **Select the choice that most directly, accurately, and effectively achieves the goal using the provided information.**

### 7.50 Common Rhetorical Synthesis Goals

| Goal | What to Look For |
|---|---|
| **Emphasize a contrast** | The answer should highlight the difference between two data points using contrast language ("while," "whereas," "in contrast") |
| **Provide evidence** | The answer should cite specific data from the provided information that supports the claim |
| **Acknowledge a limitation** | The answer should note a weakness or boundary condition in the data |
| **Introduce a topic** | The answer should present the subject clearly and engagingly, drawing on the provided information |
| **Conclude an argument** | The answer should summarize the key point using the most compelling data |
| **Concede a point** | The answer should acknowledge a counterargument or drawback using the provided information |

---

## Part XIII: The Interplay Between Grammar and Rhetoric

### 7.51 When Grammar Meets Rhetoric

Many questions on the ACT English section test both grammar and rhetoric simultaneously. For example:

- A sentence may be grammatically correct but rhetorically ineffective (too wordy, off-topic, tonally inconsistent).
- A sentence may be rhetorically appropriate but grammatically flawed (a run-on, a fragment, a punctuation error).
- The correct answer must satisfy **both** dimensions.

**The hierarchy:**
1. **First, eliminate choices with grammar errors.** A grammatically incorrect choice is always wrong, no matter how rhetorically effective it might seem.
2. **Then, among the grammatically correct choices, select the one that is rhetorically superior** (more concise, more precise, better toned, more relevant).

### 7.52 The "Grammatically Correct but Rhetorically Wrong" Trap

This is one of the most common traps on the ACT. The test includes answer choices that are perfectly grammatical but fail on rhetorical grounds:

- **Grammatically correct but wordy:** "The reason why the experiment failed is because the equipment was faulty." (Grammatically acceptable but redundant and wordy. "The experiment failed because the equipment was faulty" is better.)
- **Grammatically correct but off-topic:** A sentence that is well-written but introduces an idea not relevant to the paragraph's main point.
- **Grammatically correct but tonally inconsistent:** A sentence that uses casual language in a formal passage, or vice versa.
- **Grammatically correct but imprecise:** A sentence that uses a vague word where a specific word would be more effective.

**Always check for rhetorical effectiveness even when a choice is grammatically sound.**

---

## Part XIV: Passage-Level Strategy for Rhetorical Questions

### 7.53 Reading for Rhetoric

When you read each passage, do not just read for grammar errors. Read for the *whole passage's rhetorical architecture*:

1. **What is the author's main idea or purpose?** (Identify this within the first few sentences.)
2. **What is the tone?** (Formal? Casual? Persuasive? Informative? Personal? Objective?)
3. **How is the passage organized?** (Chronological? Problem-solution? Claim-evidence? Narrative?)
4. **What transitions connect the ideas?** (Are they accurate? Do they reflect the logical relationships?)
5. **Is every sentence pulling its weight?** (Or are there sentences that seem redundant, off-topic, or unnecessary?)

### 7.54 The "Does It Belong?" Test

For every sentence in the passage, mentally ask: "Does this belong?" If you can articulate *why* the sentence is there—what function it serves in the passage—then it belongs. If you cannot articulate its purpose, it may be a candidate for deletion (and a delete question may be coming).

### 7.55 The "Could It Be Better?" Test

For every sentence, also ask: "Could this be said more clearly, more concisely, or more effectively?" If the answer is yes, look for an answer choice that improves it.

---

## Part XV: Comprehensive Decision Tree for Rhetorical Questions

When you encounter a rhetorical skills question, use this decision tree:

```
START: What type of rhetorical question is this?
│
├── CONCISION: Is the sentence/phrase wordy or redundant?
│   ├── YES → Eliminate the wordy/redundant options. Choose the shortest
│   │         grammatically correct option that preserves all meaning.
│   └── NO → Move to next category.
│
├── REDUNDANCY: Does the sentence repeat information already in the passage?
│   ├── YES → If it's an add/delete question, DELETE. If it's a replacement
│   │         question, choose a version that eliminates the repetition.
│   └── NO → Move to next category.
│
├── TONE/STYLE: Does the word/sentence match the passage's tone and register?
│   ├── NO → Eliminate the inconsistent option. Choose the one that matches.
│   └── YES → Move to next category.
│
├── RELEVANCE: Does the sentence/idea belong in the passage?
│   ├── NO → If add/delete, DELETE. If replacement, choose the relevant option.
│   └── YES → Move to next category.
│
├── TRANSITION: Does the transition word accurately reflect the logical relationship?
│   ├── NO → Choose the transition that correctly matches the relationship.
│   └── YES → Move to next category.
│
├── WORD CHOICE: Is the most precise, contextually appropriate word used?
│   ├── NO → Choose the word with the correct denotation AND connotation.
│   └── YES → Move to next category.
│
└── ORGANIZATION: Is the sentence/paragraph in the most logical position?
    ├── NO → Choose the placement that creates the smoothest logical flow.
    └── YES → The current arrangement is likely correct.
```

---

## Part XVI: Common Misconceptions About Rhetorical Skills

### 7.56 Misconception 1: "If it sounds good, it's right."

**Reality:** Many incorrect answer choices *sound* good. They may use sophisticated vocabulary, complex sentence structures, or eloquent phrasing. But sounding good is not the same as *being* correct. The correct answer must be grammatically sound, rhetorically effective, and contextually appropriate. Always justify your choice with a specific reason, not just a feeling.

### 7.57 Misconception 2: "Longer answers are always wrong."

**Reality:** While concision is strongly favored, the correct answer is not *always* the shortest. If a longer answer choice includes necessary information that shorter choices omit, the longer choice may be correct. The principle is: *the shortest choice that preserves all essential meaning and is grammatically correct.*

### 7.58 Misconception 3: "Rhetorical questions are subjective."

**Reality:** Unlike real-world writing, where "good writing" can be subjective, the ACT English section has **one correct answer** for every question. The correct answer is correct because it best satisfies specific, identifiable criteria: concision, precision, relevance, tonal consistency, logical organization, and grammatical correctness. There is no room for "it could go either way."

### 7.59 Misconception 4: "I can rely on my ear for rhetorical questions."

**Reality:** Your ear is useful for some grammar questions (especially idioms and subject-verb agreement), but it is unreliable for rhetorical questions. Many incorrect answer choices sound perfectly natural in casual speech. The ACT tests formal written English, and your ear for formal written English may not be as developed as your ear for speech. Always justify your answer with a specific rhetorical principle.

### 7.60 Misconception 5: "Rhetorical skills can't be studied."

**Reality:** This is perhaps the most damaging misconception. Rhetorical skills can absolutely be studied and improved. The key is to:
1. Learn the principles (concision, relevance, tone, organization, etc.)
2. Practice applying them to real ACT passages
3. Analyze your mistakes to identify patterns
4. Build your vocabulary (especially connotations and precise meanings)
5. Read widely in formal written English to develop your ear

---

## Part XVII: Building Your Rhetorical Skills — A Long-Term Development Plan

### 7.71 Reading for Rhetorical Awareness

The best long-term preparation for rhetorical skills questions is reading high-quality formal writing. As you read, practice asking:

- What is the author's purpose?
- What is the tone?
- How does the author organize their ideas?
- What transitions connect the ideas?
- Is every sentence necessary? Could any be cut?
- Are there any redundancies?
- How does the author's word choice contribute to the tone?

**Recommended reading sources:**
- *The Atlantic* (magazine articles)
- *The New York Times* (opinion pieces and feature articles)
- *Scientific American* (science writing)
- *The Economist* (formal, precise prose)
- Academic journals in fields that interest you
- Classic essays (E.B. White, James Baldwin, Joan Didion, George Orwell)

### 7.72 Writing Practice

The more you write—and, crucially, the more you *revise* your writing—the better you will become at identifying rhetorical weaknesses. After writing anything, go back and:

1. Cut 10% of the words (you almost certainly can without losing meaning)
2. Replace vague words with specific ones
3. Check for redundancies
4. Verify that every sentence serves a purpose
5. Ensure transitions accurately reflect logical relationships
6. Confirm that the tone is consistent

### 7.73 Vocabulary Development for Rhetorical Precision

Building your vocabulary is not just about learning "big words." It is about learning the *precise* meanings and connotations of words so that you can identify which word best fits a given context.

**Focus on:**
- Near-synonyms with different connotations (see Section 7.11)
- Words that are commonly confused (see Section 7.43)
- Formal alternatives for casual words ("obtain" vs. "get," "sufficient" vs. "enough," "demonstrate" vs. "show")
- Precise verbs that replace vague verb + noun combinations ("investigate" vs. "conduct an investigation")

---

## Part XVIII: Quick-Reference Summary for Rhetorical Skills

### The 12 Commandments of ACT Rhetorical Skills

1. **Concision is king.** When two choices mean the same thing and are both correct, the shorter one is right.

2. **Redundancy is always wrong.** If the same idea is expressed twice, eliminate it.

3. **Every sentence must earn its place.** If a sentence does not serve the author's purpose, it should be deleted.

4. **Tone must be consistent.** The correct word matches the passage's register and emotional quality.

5. **Transitions must reflect actual logical relationships.** Read the surrounding context to determine whether the relationship is addition, contrast, cause-effect, illustration, sequence, similarity, concession, or conclusion.

6. **Precision matters.** Choose the word with the exact meaning and connotation required by the context.

7. **Relevance is non-negotiable.** A grammatically perfect sentence that is off-topic is wrong.

8. **Organization must be logical.** General before specific. Claim before evidence. Name before reference. Cause before effect (usually).

9. **Support must directly relate to the claim.** The best evidence is specific, relevant, and clearly connected to the argument.

10. **Read beyond the underlined portion.** Rhetorical questions almost always require context from the surrounding sentences or the passage as a whole.

11. **Predict before you peek.** For rhetorical questions, try to formulate your answer before looking at the choices. This prevents you from being seduced by attractive-sounding distractors.

12. **Justify every answer.** You should be able to articulate *why* the correct answer is correct and *why* each wrong answer is wrong. If you cannot, you are guessing—and guessing on rhetorical questions is dangerous.

---

## Part XIX: Advanced Rhetorical Analysis — Putting It All Together

### 7.81 The Holistic Passage Read

When you encounter a passage on the ACT English section, your first read-through should accomplish all of the following:

1. **Identify the genre:** Is this a narrative? An argument? An expository essay? A personal reflection?

2. **Identify the main idea:** What is the author's central point, story, or purpose? You should be able to state it in one sentence.

3. **Identify the tone:** What attitude does the author bring? Is it formal or casual? Serious or lighthearted? Objective or passionate?

4. **Identify the organization:** How does the passage move? Does it tell a story chronologically? Does it present an argument with evidence? Does it describe a process?

5. **Note the transitions:** As you read, pay attention to how each paragraph and sentence connects to the next. Are the transitions smooth and logical?

6. **Flag potential weaknesses:** As you read, note any sentences that seem wordy, redundant, off-topic, or tonally inconsistent. These are likely to be the subjects of questions.

This holistic reading takes time—but it is time well spent. It transforms you from a passive reader who reacts to questions into an active editor who anticipates them.

### 7.82 The Editor's Eye

The ultimate goal of your preparation is to develop what we call **the editor's eye**—the ability to look at a passage and immediately see its strengths and weaknesses, its redundancies and gaps, its tonal inconsistencies and organizational flaws.

This skill is not innate. It is developed through:
- **Knowledge** of the rules and principles (which this chapter has provided)
- **Practice** applying those rules to real passages (which your practice tests will provide)
- **Analysis** of your mistakes (which your review sessions will provide)
- **Reading** widely in formal English (which your daily habits will provide)

When you have developed the editor's eye, rhetorical skills questions become not just manageable but enjoyable. You will see the passage as a crafted object—one that can be improved through careful, principled editing. And that is exactly what the ACT wants you to do.

---

## Conclusion: The Rhetorical Skills Mindset

Rhetorical skills questions are not obstacles—they are opportunities. They are the questions that separate students who merely know grammar rules from students who can think critically about writing. They reward careful reading, precise thinking, and a deep understanding of how language works in context.

Master the principles in this chapter. Practice them relentlessly. Analyze your mistakes honestly. And on test day, approach each passage not as a test-taker searching for errors, but as an editor seeking to improve the writing. That shift in mindset—from reactive to proactive, from passive to editorial—is the key to rhetorical skills mastery.

The grammar half of the test asks: "Is this correct?"
The rhetoric half of the test asks: "Is this *effective*?"

Learn to answer both questions with confidence, and you will score in the 30s.

---

---


# Chapter 8: Advanced Time Management and Passage Navigation Strategies

---

## 8.0 — The Hidden Complexity of Time Management on the ACT English Section

Most students understand "time management" at a superficial level: "I have 45 minutes for 75 questions, so I need to move quickly." This observation, while factually correct, barely scratches the surface of what effective time management truly demands on the ACT English test. The reality is that time management on this exam is not merely about speed—it is about **strategic allocation of cognitive resources** across question types, passage types, and difficulty levels. It is about knowing when to spend extra seconds and when to cut losses. It is about building a passage-navigation system that minimizes redundant reading, prevents disorientation, and maximizes your ability to answer questions accurately under pressure.

This chapter will dissect every dimension of ACT English time management and passage navigation, transforming your approach from "try to go fast" into a precise, repeatable, optimized system.

---

## 8.1 — The Mathematical Framework: Understanding Your Time Budget

### The Raw Numbers

On the **legacy format** (paper-based), you have:

- **45 minutes** to answer **75 questions**
- That yields a theoretical average of **36 seconds per question** ($45 \times 60 \div 75 = 36$)

On the **enhanced format** (online from April 2025 / paper from September 2025), you have:

- **35 minutes** to answer **50 questions**
- That yields a theoretical average of **42 seconds per question** ($35 \times 60 \div 50 = 42$)

At first glance, the enhanced format appears more generous—6 extra seconds per question. However, this can be misleading. The enhanced format includes 10 unscored field test questions interspersed among the 40 scored questions, meaning you do not know which questions count and which do not. You must allocate time to all 50 questions as if they all matter, which means your effective "per-question" budget remains tight.

### The Real Budget: Accounting for Reading Time

The simple calculation above (total time ÷ total questions) is a starting point but is misleading because it assumes all 75 (or 50) questions consume equal time. In reality, they do not. Some questions can be answered in 10 seconds once you identify the grammatical issue. Others—particularly passage-level rhetorical questions, author-intent questions, and paragraph-reorganization questions—may require 60–90 seconds of careful analysis.

More importantly, you must account for **reading time**. If you simply read each passage start-to-finish before answering any questions, you will spend a significant chunk of your 35–45 minutes on pure reading—time that does not directly generate points. The key insight is this: **reading time is an investment, not a cost**. You invest reading time to build the contextual understanding that allows you to answer multiple questions efficiently afterward.

Here is a more realistic time budget for the legacy format:

| Activity | Estimated Time | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Initial passage skimming | 60–90 seconds per passage | A quick read for structure, tone, and main idea; not deep reading |
| Individual grammar/mechanics questions | 20–30 seconds each | These are fast if you know the rules |
| Rhetorical skills questions | 45–90 seconds each | These require more context, judgment, and often more reading |
| Passage-level / whole-passage questions | 60–120 seconds each | Highest time cost; saved for last per passage |
| Review / rechecking flagged questions | 2–4 minutes total | Only if you finish early |

This breakdown reveals a crucial principle: **you cannot allocate time equally across all questions**. Instead, you must build a system that spends time where it earns the most points.

---

## 8.2 — Question Classification and Time Allocation

Understanding the **hierarchy of question difficulty and time cost** is essential. Not all questions are created equal in terms of how much time they demand or how much "return on investment" they provide.

### Tier 1: Quick-Strike Questions (15–25 seconds each)

These are primarily grammar and mechanics questions where the error is identifiable by looking at the immediate sentence or the underlined portion plus one line of context:

- **Comma placement** (isolated rules, such as comma splices or FANBOYS commas)
- **Apostrophe errors** (its vs. it's, possessive vs. contraction)
- **Subject-verb agreement** (straightforward cases without confusing intervening phrases)
- **Pronoun case** (who vs. whom, she vs. her)
- **Simple verb tense shifts** (clear inconsistency within a paragraph)
- **Obvious redundancy** (e.g., "He returned back home")
- **Basic transition choices** (clearly contrastive or additive)

On the legacy format, expect approximately 20–30 questions to fall into this tier. On the enhanced format, approximately 15–20.

### Tier 2: Moderate Context Questions (30–55 seconds each)

These questions require reading beyond the immediate sentence—sometimes the full paragraph or even adjacent paragraphs:

- **Transition questions requiring full-paragraph context**
- **Modifier placement questions** (depends on understanding what the sentence is describing)
- **Verb tense questions involving multiple sentences** (determining whether past perfect is required)
- **Pronoun reference questions** (figuring out what the pronoun refers to)
- **Add or delete a sentence** (requires understanding paragraph logic)
- **Sentence placement questions** (where should this sentence go?)
- **Word choice / diction questions** (requires understanding tone and register)

Expect approximately 25–30 questions on the legacy format, 15–20 on the enhanced format.

### Tier 3: High-Investment Questions (60–120 seconds each)

These are the most time-intensive questions on the test. They demand careful reading, synthesis of multiple pieces of information, and careful elimination of subtly wrong answers:

- **Author's primary purpose / main goal questions**
- **"Which answer choice best..." rhetorical synthesis questions**
- **Paragraph or passage reorganization questions**
- **Questions requiring you to synthesize bulleted research notes** (primarily on the enhanced format)
- **Inference / logical completion questions** (where the passage ends and you must choose the most logical continuation)
- **Complex rhetorical strategy questions** (e.g., "Which of the following most effectively achieves the author's goal?")

Expect approximately 10–15 questions on the legacy format, 8–12 on the enhanced format.

### Why This Tiering Matters

If you treat every question as equal—spending exactly 36 seconds (or 42) on each—you will:

1. **Rush through Tier 3 questions** and make careless errors on questions that require patience
2. **Waste time on Tier 1 questions** where a quick identification of the rule yields a fast correct answer
3. **Create unnecessary risk** because you are not allocating your time budget efficiently

The optimal approach is to **bank time on quick questions and spend your savings on demanding questions**. For every Tier 1 question you answer in 20 seconds, you "save" 16 seconds compared to the average. If you save 16 seconds on 25 quick questions, you bank approximately 6.7 minutes—nearly 15–20% of your total test time—that you can redistribute to Tier 3 questions that genuinely need it.

---

## 8.3 — Passage Architecture and Navigation Models

### Understanding How ACT Passages Are Structured

Every ACT English passage follows a deliberate structural design. Although the content varies across genres (Literary Narrative, Humanities, Social Science, Natural Science), the organizational patterns are remarkably consistent:

1. **Opening**: Each passage has an introduction that establishes the topic, the tone, and usually the central idea or narrative thrust.
2. **Body paragraphs**: These develop the central idea through examples, chronological narration, argumentation, or exploration of subtopics. They are connected by transitions and logical progressions.
3. **Closing**: Most passages have a concluding section that resolves, summarizes, or reflects on what came before.

This predictable architecture is a weapon in your hands. If you understand typical passage structure, you can predict where the passage is going, which drastically reduces the time required to answer transition, organization, and purpose questions.

### The Five Passage-Navigation Models

There is no single "correct" way to navigate an ACT English passage. Different students thrive with different approaches. Below are five navigation models, analyzed in depth, with their advantages, disadvantages, and ideal user profiles.

---

#### Model A: Graf-by-Graf (Recommended for Most Students)

**Description**: Read one paragraph (graf = paragraph) of the passage. Then answer all questions anchored in that paragraph. Move to the next paragraph and repeat.

**Detailed Execution**:

1. **Step 1**: Begin with the first paragraph. Read it at a moderate pace—not skimming, but not lingering. You are building a mental map of the paragraph's content. Aim for 30–45 seconds depending on paragraph length.

2. **Step 2**: After reading the paragraph, look at all the underlined portions within it (these are numbered). Answer each question, referencing the paragraph text as needed.

3. **Step 3**: Some questions in the paragraph will require reading into the next paragraph (e.g., transition questions at the end of the paragraph). If so, read what you need, answer the question, and then proceed to the next paragraph for the full read.

4. **Step 4**: Save questions about the entire passage (those marked with a number in a box or highlighted asterisk) for after you have read all paragraphs.

**Advantages**:
- Provides excellent paragraph-level context before answering questions
- Prevents the disorientation of jumping around a passage you haven't read
- Naturally builds comprehension incrementally, making later questions easier
- Minimizes the need to re-read paragraphs

**Disadvantages**:
- Requires holding questions in working memory while you finish the paragraph
- Some students find the switch between "reading mode" and "question mode" jarring
- For very long paragraphs, you may need to re-read portions for specific questions

**Ideal for**: Students scoring in the mid-teens to low 20s who need to build contextual understanding systematically; students who get overwhelmed when they try to answer questions about text they haven't mentally processed.

---

#### Model B: Answer-as-You-Go (Full-Passage-First)

**Description**: Read the entire passage from beginning to end, taking 2–3 minutes. Then go back through the passage a second time, encountering each underlined portion and answering its question during this second pass.

**Detailed Execution**:

1. **Step 1 — Full Reading Pass**: Read the entire passage as you would a short article. Focus on understanding the main idea, the author's tone, the passage's structure, and the logical flow of ideas. Allow 1.5–3 minutes for this (passages are typically 300–400 words).

2. **Step 2 — Question Pass**: Return to the beginning. Locate each numbered underlined portion. Because you already understand the passage, your second pass is faster—you're simply executing the mechanics of answering questions, building on the comprehension you already built.

3. **Step 3 — Handling Passage-Level Questions**: These come naturally after both passes.

**Advantages**:
- Builds the strongest overall comprehension of any model
- Handles passage-level questions (purpose, main goal) almost automatically during the first reading
- The second pass is very fast because you already know what the passage says
- Eliminates the frustration of encountering a question about something you haven't read yet

**Disadvantages**:
- The initial reading pass takes significant time that could otherwise be spent answering questions
- If the passage is dense or poorly understood, you may need a partial third pass
- Students with weaker reading comprehension may struggle during the initial pass

**Ideal for**: Strong readers (students scoring 28+) who already comprehend passages quickly but tend to miss rhetorical/purpose questions because they haven't "felt" the whole passage's arc.

---

#### Model C: Sentence-by-Sentence (Minimalist)

**Description**: Do NOT read the passage in advance. When you encounter an underlined portion (question number), read the sentence containing it (and perhaps the sentence before and after) to answer that question. Move directly to the next numbered question.

**Detailed Execution**:

1. **Step 1**: Go directly to the first question. Identify the underlined portion.

2. **Step 2**: Read the sentence containing the underlined portion. If needed, read the sentence before and after for context.

3. **Step 3**: Answer the question.

4. **Step 4**: Move to the next question and repeat.

**Advantages**:
- Maximizes time spent on question-answering (the only point-earning activity)
- Eliminates "wasted" reading time
- Works well for grammar questions that are sentence-bound

**Disadvantages**:
- **Severely disadvantages rhetorical skills questions** (transitions, purpose, organization, add/delete) which require paragraph-level or passage-level context
- You may end up reading the same passage section multiple times from different angles, which is actually less efficient than reading it once
- High risk of missing the "big picture" needed for passage-level questions
- Can cause disorientation and anxiety because you never feel grounded in the passage

**Ideal for**: Honestly, this model is rarely ideal. It may work for students who are extremely strong readers and can absorb context almost instantaneously, but for most students, it sacrifices accuracy for speed. **This model is not recommended for students targeting a score above 25.**

---

#### Model D: Skim-then-Sentence-by-Sentence (Hybrid)

**Description**: Skim the entire passage in 60–90 seconds to get the gist (main idea, tone, structure). Then go question-by-question, reading each sentence with its surrounding context.

**Detailed Execution**:

1. **Step 1 — Skim**: Read the first sentence of each paragraph carefully. Read the last sentence of each paragraph carefully. Glance at the middle of each paragraph for key words and transitions. This gives you a "skeleton" of the passage.

2. **Step 2 — Question Execution**: Go to each question. Read the sentence with the underlined portion. Use your skim-knowledge to orient yourself quickly within the passage's logic.

3. **Step 3 — Deep Read as Needed**: For passage-level questions, you may need to read more carefully. Your skim has already identified where key ideas live, so you can go directly to the relevant section.

**Advantages**:
- Balances comprehension-building with time efficiency
- The skim is much faster than a full read but provides significantly more context than no read at all
- Helps you quickly locate relevant sections for passage-level questions
- Reduces re-reading because you already know where things are

**Disadvantages**:
- Skimming is a skill that requires practice to do effectively
- If you skim poorly, you may build a misleading mental map
- Still requires sentence-level reading for each question, which adds time

**Ideal for**: Students who find Model A too slow but Model C too risky. This is a strong choice for students in the 20–28 range who want to improve their rhetorical question accuracy without sacrificing too much time.

---

#### Model E: The Triage Model (Advanced)

**Description**: This is a sophisticated, adaptive approach that classifies each question in real-time and allocates time dynamically. It is the model used by top-scoring students (32+).

**Detailed Execution**:

1. **Step 1 — Quick Scan**: Spend 30–60 seconds scanning the passage. Read the first and last paragraphs fully. Read the first sentence of each body paragraph. This gives you the passage's skeleton.

2. **Step 2 — First Pass (Tier 1 Questions)**: Go through the passage question by question. When you encounter a question you can answer in under 30 seconds (clear grammar rule, obvious error), answer it immediately and move on. Do not linger. Do not second-guess.

3. **Step 3 — Second Pass (Tier 2 Questions)**: Return to questions that require more context. Now that you have answered the quick questions, you have a better understanding of the passage (because answering questions forces you to engage with the text). Use this enhanced understanding to tackle moderate questions.

4. **Step 4 — Third Pass (Tier 3 Questions)**: Finally, address passage-level questions, author-purpose questions, and any questions you flagged as difficult. By this point, you have read the passage multiple times through the lens of specific questions, and your comprehension is deep.

5. **Step 5 — Final Sweep**: If time permits, revisit any flagged questions. Do not change answers unless you find a specific error in your reasoning.

**Advantages**:
- Maximizes points per unit of time
- Prevents you from getting stuck on hard questions while leaving easy questions unanswered
- Builds comprehension incrementally through question engagement
- Naturally handles all three tiers of questions at their appropriate difficulty level
- Reduces anxiety because you know you will see each question multiple times if needed

**Disadvantages**:
- Requires strong metacognitive skills (you must accurately classify question difficulty in real-time)
- Requires discipline to move on from hard questions during the first pass
- Can feel chaotic if you are not comfortable with the passage
- Requires significant practice to execute smoothly

**Ideal for**: Students targeting 32+ who have strong grammar fundamentals and want to optimize their approach. This is the most powerful model but also the hardest to master.

---

## 8.4 — The Flag-and-Return System

Regardless of which navigation model you use, the **Flag-and-Return System** is a critical tactical tool.

### How It Works

1. **When you encounter a question that you cannot answer within your target time** (e.g., 45 seconds for a Tier 2 question, 30 seconds for a Tier 1 question), mark it with a symbol in your test booklet and move on immediately.

2. **Use a consistent flagging system**:
   - **Circle** = "I have not gotten to this yet"
   - **Single underline** = "I looked at this and guessed; come back if time"
   - **Double underline** = "I am completely stuck; skip entirely"

3. **After completing all questions you can answer**, return to flagged questions with your remaining time.

### Why This Works

The ACT English section has a peculiar property: **questions you find hard are not objectively harder than questions you find easy**. The difficulty is often a function of familiarity with the specific rule being tested. When you return to a flagged question after answering 20 other questions, you have:

- **Warmed up your grammar-processing circuits**
- **Built more contextual understanding of the passage**
- **Reduced anxiety** because you have already banked points from easier questions
- **Fresh eyes** — the same question that stumped you 10 minutes ago may now seem obvious

### The Critical Rule: Never Leave a Question Unanswered Before Moving On

If you are stuck on question 14, and you spend 3 minutes agonizing over it, you have:
- Potentially gotten it wrong anyway (worst case)
- Wasted 3 minutes that could have been spent correctly answering 4–5 easier questions later in the passage
- Increased your anxiety and reduced your confidence for subsequent questions

The flag-and-return system prevents all three of these outcomes.

---

## 8.5 — Pacing Checkpoints: The Internal Clock System

One of the most common time-management failures on the ACT English section is **losing track of time**. Students become absorbed in reading or in specific questions and suddenly realize they have 10 minutes left for 25 questions. This creates panic, which degrades performance on the remaining questions—the very questions that might have been easy.

### Building Your Internal Clock

The solution is to establish **pacing checkpoints**—pre-determined points in the test where you check your progress against the clock.

**For the Legacy Format (75 questions, 45 minutes)**:

| Checkpoint | Question # | Target Elapsed Time | Time Remaining |
|---|---|---|---|
| End of Passage 1 | ~Q15 | ~9 minutes | ~36 minutes |
| End of Passage 2 | ~Q30 | ~18 minutes | ~27 minutes |
| End of Passage 3 | ~Q45 | ~27 minutes | ~18 minutes |
| End of Passage 4 | ~Q60 | ~36 minutes | ~9 minutes |
| End of Passage 5 | Q75 | 45 minutes | 0 minutes |

**For the Enhanced Format (50 questions, 35 minutes)**:

| Checkpoint | Question # | Target Elapsed Time | Time Remaining |
|---|---|---|---|
| End of Passage 1 | ~Q8–10 | ~6 minutes | ~29 minutes |
| End of Passage 2 | ~Q16–20 | ~12 minutes | ~23 minutes |
| End of Passage 3 | ~Q24–30 | ~18 minutes | ~17 minutes |
| End of Passage 4 | ~Q32–40 | ~24 minutes | ~11 minutes |
| End of Passage 5 | Q41–50 | ~31 minutes | ~4 minutes buffer |

*(Note: The enhanced format has variable passage lengths, so these question numbers are approximate.)*

### What to Do at Each Checkpoint

1. **Glance at the clock.** Note the elapsed time.

2. **Compare to the target.** Are you ahead, on pace, or behind?

3. **If ahead**: Excellent. You have a time buffer. You can afford to spend a little extra time on the next passage's harder questions, or you can slow down slightly to reduce careless errors.

4. **If on pace**: Maintain your current approach. Do not speed up or slow down.

5. **If behind by 1–2 minutes**: Increase your pace slightly on the next passage. Skip any question that takes more than 45 seconds on the first pass. Flag and return.

6. **If behind by 3+ minutes**: Enter "triage mode." On the remaining passages, answer only Tier 1 questions on the first pass. Flag everything else. After completing all Tier 1 questions, use remaining time for Tier 2 and Tier 3. Guess on any remaining unanswered questions.

### Training Your Internal Clock

During practice tests, do not rely solely on the wall clock or your phone. Instead, practice **feeling** how long 9 minutes (or 6 minutes) is. After several practice tests, you should be able to sense when you have spent approximately the right amount of time on each passage without even looking at the clock. This internal sense frees up cognitive resources for actual question-solving.

---

## 8.6 — Strategic Reading: What to Read and What to Skip

### The Reading Efficiency Principle

Every word you read on the ACT English test costs you time. The question is: which words generate a return on that time investment, and which words are "dead weight"?

**High-value reading** (invest time here):
- The first and last sentences of each paragraph (they contain the topic sentence and concluding thought)
- Sentences containing underlined portions (obviously)
- Sentences immediately before and after an underlined portion (needed for context)
- Transition words and phrases (however, therefore, for example, in contrast, etc.)
- The first and last paragraphs of the passage (contain the main idea and conclusion)

**Low-value reading** (minimize time here):
- Long descriptive passages in Literary Narrative that do not contain underlined portions
- Detailed examples or evidence that are not referenced by any question
- Parenthetical asides or tangential information
- Passages of dense exposition in the middle of a passage that have no associated questions

### The "Question-First" Reading Technique

For certain question types, you can reverse the typical reading flow:

1. **Read the question stem first** (before reading the passage sentence). The question stem tells you what to look for.
2. **Identify the specific element being tested** (grammar rule, transition, organization, etc.).
3. **Read only the portion of the passage relevant to that element.**

For example, if a question asks, "Which of the following best describes the main purpose of the third paragraph?" you do not need to read the entire passage—you need to read the third paragraph (and perhaps the second and fourth for context). This targeted reading can save 30–60 seconds per passage-level question.

### The "Transition Anchor" Technique

Transitions are among the most frequently tested rhetorical elements on the ACT English section. The technique for handling them efficiently:

1. **Identify the transition word or logical relationship in the sentence containing the question.**
2. **Read the sentence immediately before the transition.** This sentence establishes the "setup"—the idea that the transition is modifying or connecting.
3. **Determine the logical relationship**: Is the second sentence adding to the first? Contrasting with it? Showing cause and effect? Providing an example?
4. **Match the relationship to the answer choice.**

This technique works because transitions are **local**—they connect two adjacent ideas. You rarely need to read more than two sentences to answer a transition question correctly. Resist the urge to read the entire paragraph unless the transition is at a paragraph boundary.

---

## 8.7 — The Art of Knowing When to Guess and Move On

This is perhaps the most difficult time-management skill to master, and it is the one that separates good scores from great scores.

### The 30-Second Rule

**If you have read the relevant context, considered all four answer choices, and you still cannot determine the correct answer within 30 seconds, guess, flag, and move on.**

This rule is not about giving up. It is about **maximizing your total score**. Consider this scenario:

- You spend 2 minutes agonizing over a difficult question and get it right. Cost: 2 minutes. Gain: 1 point.
- Alternatively, you spend 30 seconds, guess, and move on. You then use the remaining 1.5 minutes to answer two easier questions correctly. Cost: 30 seconds + 30 seconds + 30 seconds = 1.5 minutes. Gain: 2 points.

The second scenario yields more points in less time. This is the mathematical argument for the 30-second rule.

### The "Two-Pass" Mindset

Adopt the mindset that you will see every question at least twice if you need to. On the first pass, you answer everything you can answer quickly. On the second pass, you return to flagged questions with the benefit of time and context. This mindset reduces the pressure of any single question and prevents the catastrophic scenario of spending 3 minutes on question 27 and never reaching questions 45–75 (which might have been easy).

### When to Break the 30-Second Rule

There are two situations where you should spend more than 30 seconds before guessing:

1. **You are close to the answer.** If you have narrowed it to two choices and you can feel the correct answer "on the tip of your tongue," spend an extra 15–20 seconds to resolve it. The confidence of knowing you got a hard question right is worth the small time investment.

2. **The question is a passage-level question that you have not yet read enough context to answer.** In this case, the problem is not that the question is hard—it is that you have not read enough. Read more context, then answer. Do not guess on a passage-level question until you have read the relevant paragraphs.

---

## 8.8 — Bubble Strategy and Answer Documentation

### Paper-Based Test: Batch Bubbling

On the paper-based ACT, you fill in a separate answer sheet. This creates an opportunity for a significant time-saving technique: **batch bubbling**.

**How it works**:

1. **Solve questions in the test booklet only.** Do not alternate between the test booklet and the answer sheet for each question.

2. **After completing all questions in a passage (or a section of a passage), transfer all answers to the answer sheet at once.**

3. **Why this saves time**: The physical act of locating the correct question number on the answer sheet, aligning your pencil, and filling in the oval takes approximately 3–5 seconds per question. If you do this 75 times, that's $3.75$–$6.25$ minutes of pure administrative time. If you do it in 5 batches of 15, the overhead drops to approximately 45 seconds total—a savings of 3–5 minutes.

**Caveat**: This technique requires you to be disciplined about tracking which questions you have answered and which you have flagged. Use the margin of your test booklet to mark your answers (A, B, C, D) in small letters, then transfer them in batches.

### Computer-Based Test: No Batch Bubbling

On the computerized ACT, you select answers directly on the screen. There is no batch-bubbling opportunity. However, the computer-based test offers other time-management tools:

- **A flag button** to mark questions for review
- **A review screen** that shows which questions are unanswered, flagged, or completed
- **The ability to click directly to any question number** without scrolling

Use these tools aggressively. Flag every question you are unsure about. Use the review screen at the end of the section to ensure you have answered every question.

---

## 8.9 — Managing Cognitive Fatigue

The ACT English section is the first section of the test, which means you are freshest when you begin it. However, the section itself is 35–45 minutes of intense, focused work. Cognitive fatigue is real, and it affects your performance in ways you may not notice in real-time.

### The Fatigue Curve

Research on sustained attention shows that performance on cognitively demanding tasks follows a predictable pattern:

1. **Minutes 1–5**: Warm-up phase. Your brain is adjusting to the task. You may feel slightly sluggish or unfocused.

2. **Minutes 5–25**: Peak performance. Your brain is fully engaged, and you are processing information at maximum efficiency.

3. **Minutes 25–35**: Fatigue onset. Your processing speed begins to decline. You may start making careless errors on questions you would normally get right.
